I was apprenticed when I was seven year old.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh!  Indeed?\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Gradgrind, rather resentfully, as having been\r\ndefrauded of his good opinion.  \xe2\x80\x98I was not aware of its being the custom\r\nto apprentice young persons to\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Idleness,\xe2\x80\x99 Mr. Bounderby put in with a loud laugh.  \xe2\x80\x98No, by the Lord\r\nHarry!  Nor I!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Her father always had it in his head,\xe2\x80\x99 resumed Childers, feigning\r\nunconsciousness of Mr. Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s existence, \xe2\x80\x98that she was to be taught\r\nthe deuce-and-all of education.  How it got into his head, I can\xe2\x80\x99t say; I\r\ncan only say that it never got out.  He has been picking up a bit of\r\nreading for her, here\xe2\x80\x94and a bit of writing for her, there\xe2\x80\x94and a bit of\r\nciphering for her, somewhere else\xe2\x80\x94these seven years.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. E. W. B. Childers took one of his hands out of his pockets, stroked\r\nhis face and chin, and looked, with a good deal of doubt and a little\r\nhope, at Mr. Gradgrind.  From the first he had sought to conciliate that\r\ngentleman, for the sake of the deserted girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98When Sissy got into the school here,\xe2\x80\x99 he pursued, \xe2\x80\x98her father was as\r\npleased as Punch.  I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t altogether make out why, myself, as we were\r\nnot stationary here, being but comers and goers anywhere.  I suppose,\r\nhowever, he had this move in his mind\xe2\x80\x94he was always half-cracked\xe2\x80\x94and then\r\nconsidered her provided for.  If you should happen to have looked in\r\nto-night, for the purpose of telling him that you were going to do her\r\nany little service,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Childers, stroking his face again, and\r\nrepeating his look, \xe2\x80\x98it would be very fortunate and well-timed; very\r\nfortunate and well-timed.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98On the contrary,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Mr. Gradgrind.  \xe2\x80\x98I came to tell him that her\r\nconnections made her not an object for the school, and that she must not\r\nattend any more.  Still, if her father really has left her, without any\r\nconnivance on her part\xe2\x80\x94Bounderby, let me have a word with you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nUpon this, Mr. Childers politely betook himself, with his equestrian\r\nwalk, to the landing outside the door, and there stood stroking his face,\r\nand softly whistling.  While thus engaged, he overheard such phrases in\r\nMr. Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s voice as \xe2\x80\x98No.  _I_ say no.  I advise you not.  I say by\r\nno means.\xe2\x80\x99  While, from Mr. Gradgrind, he heard in his much lower tone\r\nthe words, \xe2\x80\x98But even as an example to Louisa, of what this pursuit which\r\nhas been the subject of a vulgar curiosity, leads to and ends in.  Think\r\nof it, Bounderby, in that point of view.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMeanwhile, the various members of Sleary\xe2\x80\x99s company gradually gathered\r\ntogether from the upper regions, where they were quartered, and, from\r\nstanding about, talking in low voices to one another and to Mr. Childers,\r\ngradually insinuated themselves and him into the room.  There were two or\r\nthree handsome young women among them, with their two or three husbands,\r\nand their two or three mothers, and their eight or nine little children,\r\nwho did the fairy business when required.  The father of one of the\r\nfamilies was in the habit of balancing the father of another of the\r\nfamilies on the top of a great pole; the father of a third family often\r\nmade a pyramid of both those fathers, with Master Kidderminster for the\r\napex, and himself for the base; all the fathers could dance upon rolling\r\ncasks, stand upon bottles, catch knives and balls, twirl hand-basins,\r\nride upon anything, jump over everything, and stick at nothing.  All the\r\nmothers could (and did) dance, upon the slack wire and the tight-rope,\r\nand perform rapid acts on bare-backed steeds; none of them were at all\r\nparticular in respect of showing their legs; and one of them, alone in a\r\nGreek chariot, drove six in hand into every town they came to.  They all\r\nassumed to be mighty rakish and knowing, they were not very tidy in their\r\nprivate dresses, they were not at all orderly in their domestic\r\narrangements, and the combined literature of the whole company would have\r\nproduced but a poor letter on any subject.  Yet there was a remarkable\r\ngentleness and childishness about these people, a special inaptitude for\r\nany kind of sharp practice, and an untiring readiness to help and pity\r\none another, deserving often of as much respect, and always of as much\r\ngenerous construction, as the every-day virtues of any class of people in\r\nthe world.\r\n\r\nLast of all appeared Mr. Sleary: a stout man as already mentioned, with\r\none fixed eye, and one loose eye, a voice (if it can be called so) like\r\nthe efforts of a broken old pair of bellows, a flabby surface, and a\r\nmuddled head which was never sober and never drunk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thquire!\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Sleary, who was troubled with asthma, and whose\r\nbreath came far too thick and heavy for the letter s, \xe2\x80\x98Your thervant!\r\nThith ith a bad piethe of bithnith, thith ith.  You\xe2\x80\x99ve heard of my Clown\r\nand hith dog being thuppothed to have morrithed?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe addressed Mr. Gradgrind, who answered \xe2\x80\x98Yes.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, Thquire,\xe2\x80\x99 he returned, taking off his hat, and rubbing the lining\r\nwith his pocket-handkerchief, which he kept inside for the purpose.  \xe2\x80\x98Ith\r\nit your intenthion to do anything for the poor girl, Thquire?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I shall have something to propose to her when she comes back,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr.\r\nGradgrind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Glad to hear it, Thquire.  Not that I want to get rid of the child, any\r\nmore than I want to thtand in her way.  I\xe2\x80\x99m willing to take her prentith,\r\nthough at her age ith late.  My voithe ith a little huthky, Thquire, and\r\nnot eathy heard by them ath don\xe2\x80\x99t know me; but if you\xe2\x80\x99d been chilled and\r\nheated, heated and chilled, chilled and heated in the ring when you wath\r\nyoung, ath often ath I have been, _your_ voithe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have lathted\r\nout, Thquire, no more than mine.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I dare say not,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Gradgrind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What thall it be, Thquire, while you wait?  Thall it be Therry?  Give it\r\na name, Thquire!\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Sleary, with hospitable ease.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Nothing for me, I thank you,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Gradgrind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t thay nothing, Thquire.  What doth your friend thay?  If you\r\nhaven\xe2\x80\x99t took your feed yet, have a glath of bitterth.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHere his daughter Josephine\xe2\x80\x94a pretty fair-haired girl of eighteen, who\r\nhad been tied on a horse at two years old, and had made a will at twelve,\r\nwhich she always carried about with her, expressive of her dying desire\r\nto be drawn to the grave by the two piebald ponies\xe2\x80\x94cried, \xe2\x80\x98Father, hush!\r\nshe has come back!\xe2\x80\x99  Then came Sissy Jupe, running into the room as she\r\nhad run out of it.  And when she saw them all assembled, and saw their\r\nlooks, and saw no father there, she broke into a most deplorable cry, and\r\ntook refuge on the bosom of the most accomplished tight-rope lady\r\n(herself in the family-way), who knelt down on the floor to nurse her,\r\nand to weep over her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ith an internal thame, upon my thoul it ith,\xe2\x80\x99 said Sleary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98O my dear father, my good kind father, where are you gone?  You are gone\r\nto try to do me some good, I know!  You are gone away for my sake, I am\r\nsure!  And how miserable and helpless you will be without me, poor, poor\r\nfather, until you come back!\xe2\x80\x99  It was so pathetic to hear her saying many\r\nthings of this kind, with her face turned upward, and her arms stretched\r\nout as if she were trying to stop his departing shadow and embrace it,\r\nthat no one spoke a word until Mr. Bounderby (growing impatient) took the\r\ncase in hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now, good people all,\xe2\x80\x99 said he, \xe2\x80\x98this is wanton waste of time.
