Your father has\r\nabsconded\xe2\x80\x94deserted you\xe2\x80\x94and you mustn\xe2\x80\x99t expect to see him again as long as\r\nyou live.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThey cared so little for plain Fact, these people, and were in that\r\nadvanced state of degeneracy on the subject, that instead of being\r\nimpressed by the speaker\xe2\x80\x99s strong common sense, they took it in\r\nextraordinary dudgeon.  The men muttered \xe2\x80\x98Shame!\xe2\x80\x99 and the women \xe2\x80\x98Brute!\xe2\x80\x99\r\nand Sleary, in some haste, communicated the following hint, apart to Mr.\r\nBounderby.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I tell you what, Thquire.  To thpeak plain to you, my opinion ith that\r\nyou had better cut it thort, and drop it.  They\xe2\x80\x99re a very good natur\xe2\x80\x99d\r\npeople, my people, but they\xe2\x80\x99re accuthtomed to be quick in their\r\nmovementh; and if you don\xe2\x80\x99t act upon my advithe, I\xe2\x80\x99m damned if I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbelieve they\xe2\x80\x99ll pith you out o\xe2\x80\x99 winder.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. Bounderby being restrained by this mild suggestion, Mr. Gradgrind\r\nfound an opening for his eminently practical exposition of the subject.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It is of no moment,\xe2\x80\x99 said he, \xe2\x80\x98whether this person is to be expected\r\nback at any time, or the contrary.  He is gone away, and there is no\r\npresent expectation of his return.  That, I believe, is agreed on all\r\nhands.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thath agreed, Thquire.  Thick to that!\xe2\x80\x99  From Sleary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well then.  I, who came here to inform the father of the poor girl,\r\nJupe, that she could not be received at the school any more, in\r\nconsequence of there being practical objections, into which I need not\r\nenter, to the reception there of the children of persons so employed, am\r\nprepared in these altered circumstances to make a proposal.  I am willing\r\nto take charge of you, Jupe, and to educate you, and provide for you.\r\nThe only condition (over and above your good behaviour) I make is, that\r\nyou decide now, at once, whether to accompany me or remain here.  Also,\r\nthat if you accompany me now, it is understood that you communicate no\r\nmore with any of your friends who are here present.  These observations\r\ncomprise the whole of the case.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98At the thame time,\xe2\x80\x99 said Sleary, \xe2\x80\x98I mutht put in my word, Thquire, tho\r\nthat both thides of the banner may be equally theen.  If you like,\r\nThethilia, to be prentitht, you know the natur of the work and you know\r\nyour companionth.  Emma Gordon, in whothe lap you\xe2\x80\x99re a lying at prethent,\r\nwould be a mother to you, and Joth\xe2\x80\x99phine would be a thithter to you.  I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t pretend to be of the angel breed myself, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t thay but what,\r\nwhen you mith\xe2\x80\x99d your tip, you\xe2\x80\x99d find me cut up rough, and thwear an oath\r\nor two at you.  But what I thay, Thquire, ith, that good tempered or bad\r\ntempered, I never did a horthe a injury yet, no more than thwearing at\r\nhim went, and that I don\xe2\x80\x99t expect I thall begin otherwithe at my time of\r\nlife, with a rider.  I never wath much of a Cackler, Thquire, and I have\r\nthed my thay.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe latter part of this speech was addressed to Mr. Gradgrind, who\r\nreceived it with a grave inclination of his head, and then remarked:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The only observation I will make to you, Jupe, in the way of influencing\r\nyour decision, is, that it is highly desirable to have a sound practical\r\neducation, and that even your father himself (from what I understand)\r\nappears, on your behalf, to have known and felt that much.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe last words had a visible effect upon her.  She stopped in her wild\r\ncrying, a little detached herself from Emma Gordon, and turned her face\r\nfull upon her patron.  The whole company perceived the force of the\r\nchange, and drew a long breath together, that plainly said, \xe2\x80\x98she will\r\ngo!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Be sure you know your own mind, Jupe,\xe2\x80\x99 Mr. Gradgrind cautioned her; \xe2\x80\x98I\r\nsay no more.  Be sure you know your own mind!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98When father comes back,\xe2\x80\x99 cried the girl, bursting into tears again after\r\na minute\xe2\x80\x99s silence, \xe2\x80\x98how will he ever find me if I go away!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You may be quite at ease,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Gradgrind, calmly; he worked out the\r\nwhole matter like a sum: \xe2\x80\x98you may be quite at ease, Jupe, on that score.\r\nIn such a case, your father, I apprehend, must find out Mr.\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thleary.  Thath my name, Thquire.  Not athamed of it.  Known all over\r\nEngland, and alwayth paythe ith way.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Must find out Mr. Sleary, who would then let him know where you went.  I\r\nshould have no power of keeping you against his wish, and he would have\r\nno difficulty, at any time, in finding Mr. Thomas Gradgrind of Coketown.\r\nI am well known.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well known,\xe2\x80\x99 assented Mr. Sleary, rolling his loose eye.  \xe2\x80\x98You\xe2\x80\x99re one of\r\nthe thort, Thquire, that keepth a prethiouth thight of money out of the\r\nhouthe.  But never mind that at prethent.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThere was another silence; and then she exclaimed, sobbing with her hands\r\nbefore her face, \xe2\x80\x98Oh, give me my clothes, give me my clothes, and let me\r\ngo away before I break my heart!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe women sadly bestirred themselves to get the clothes together\xe2\x80\x94it was\r\nsoon done, for they were not many\xe2\x80\x94and to pack them in a basket which had\r\noften travelled with them.  Sissy sat all the time upon the ground, still\r\nsobbing, and covering her eyes.  Mr. Gradgrind and his friend Bounderby\r\nstood near the door, ready to take her away.  Mr. Sleary stood in the\r\nmiddle of the room, with the male members of the company about him,\r\nexactly as he would have stood in the centre of the ring during his\r\ndaughter Josephine\xe2\x80\x99s performance.  He wanted nothing but his whip.\r\n\r\nThe basket packed in silence, they brought her bonnet to her, and\r\nsmoothed her disordered hair, and put it on.  Then they pressed about\r\nher, and bent over her in very natural attitudes, kissing and embracing\r\nher: and brought the children to take leave of her; and were a\r\ntender-hearted, simple, foolish set of women altogether.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now, Jupe,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Gradgrind.  \xe2\x80\x98If you are quite determined, come!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBut she had to take her farewell of the male part of the company yet, and\r\nevery one of them had to unfold his arms (for they all assumed the\r\nprofessional attitude when they found themselves near Sleary), and give\r\nher a parting kiss\xe2\x80\x94Master Kidderminster excepted, in whose young nature\r\nthere was an original flavour of the misanthrope, who was also known to\r\nhave harboured matrimonial views, and who moodily withdrew.  Mr. Sleary\r\nwas reserved until the last.  Opening his arms wide he took her by both\r\nher hands, and would have sprung her up and down, after the riding-master\r\nmanner of congratulating young ladies on their dismounting from a rapid\r\nact; but there was no rebound in Sissy, and she only stood before him\r\ncrying.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Good-bye, my dear!\xe2\x80\x99 said Sleary.  \xe2\x80\x98You\xe2\x80\x99ll make your fortun, I hope, and\r\nnone of our poor folkth will ever trouble you, I\xe2\x80\x99ll pound it.  I with\r\nyour father hadn\xe2\x80\x99t taken hith dog with him; ith a ill-conwenienth to have\r\nthe dog out of the billth.  But on thecond thoughth, he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have\r\nperformed without hith mathter, tho ith ath broad ath ith long!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWith that he regarded her attentively with his fixed eye, surveyed his\r\ncompany with his loose one, kissed her, shook his head, and handed her to\r\nMr. Gradgrind as to a horse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There the ith, Thquire,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, sweeping her with a professional\r\nglance as if she were being adjusted in her seat, \xe2\x80\x98and the\xe2\x80\x99ll do you\r\njuthtithe.  Good-bye, Thethilia!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Good-bye, Cecilia!\xe2\x80\x99  \xe2\x80\x98Good-bye, Sissy!\xe2\x80\x99  \xe2\x80\x98God bless you, dear!\xe2\x80\x99  In a\r\nvariety of voices from all the room.\r\n\r\nBut the riding-master eye had observed the bottle of the nine oils in her\r\nbosom, and he now interposed with \xe2\x80\x98Leave the bottle, my dear; ith large\r\nto carry; it will be of no uthe to you now.  Give it to me!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, no!\xe2\x80\x99 she said, in another burst of tears.  \xe2\x80\x98Oh, no!  Pray let me\r\nkeep it for father till he comes back!  He will want it when he comes\r\nback.  He had never thought of going away, when he sent me for it.  I\r\nmust keep it for him, if you please!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Tho be it, my dear.  (You thee how it ith, Thquire!)  Farewell,\r\nThethilia!  My latht wordth to you ith thith, Thtick to the termth of\r\nyour engagement, be obedient to the Thquire, and forget uth.  But if,\r\nwhen you\xe2\x80\x99re grown up and married and well off, you come upon any\r\nhorthe-riding ever, don\xe2\x80\x99t be hard upon it, don\xe2\x80\x99t be croth with it, give\r\nit a Bethpeak if you can, and think you might do wurth.  People mutht be\r\namuthed, Thquire, thomehow,\xe2\x80\x99 continued Sleary, rendered more pursy than\r\never, by so much talking; \xe2\x80\x98they can\xe2\x80\x99t be alwayth a working, nor yet they\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t be alwayth a learning.  Make the betht of uth; not the wurtht.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve got my living out of the horthe-riding all my life, I know; but I\r\nconthider that I lay down the philothophy of the thubject when I thay to\r\nyou, Thquire, make the betht of uth: not the wurtht!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe Sleary philosophy was propounded as they went downstairs and the\r\nfixed eye of Philosophy\xe2\x80\x94and its rolling eye, too\xe2\x80\x94soon lost the three\r\nfigures and the basket in the darkness of the street.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII\r\nMRS. SPARSIT\r\n\r\n\r\nMR. BOUNDERBY being a bachelor, an elderly lady presided over his\r\nestablishment, in consideration of a certain annual stipend.  Mrs.\r\nSparsit was this lady\xe2\x80\x99s name; and she was a prominent figure in\r\nattendance on Mr. Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s car, as it rolled along in triumph with the\r\nBully of humility inside.\r\n\r\nFor, Mrs. Sparsit had not only seen different days, but was highly\r\nconnected.  She had a great aunt living in these very times called Lady\r\nScadgers.  Mr. Sparsit, deceased, of whom she was the relict, had been by\r\nthe mother\xe2\x80\x99s side what Mrs. Sparsit still called \xe2\x80\x98a Powler.\xe2\x80\x99  Strangers\r\nof limited information and dull apprehension were sometimes observed not\r\nto know what a Powler was, and even to appear uncertain whether it might\r\nbe a business, or a political party, or a profession of faith.  The\r\nbetter class of minds, however, did not need to be informed that the\r\nPowlers were an ancient stock, who could trace themselves so exceedingly\r\nfar back that it was not surprising if they sometimes lost\r\nthemselves\xe2\x80\x94which they had rather frequently done, as respected\r\nhorse-flesh, blind-hookey, Hebrew monetary transactions, and the\r\nInsolvent Debtors\xe2\x80\x99 Court.\r\n\r\nThe late Mr. Sparsit, being by the mother\xe2\x80\x99s side a Powler, married this\r\nlady, being by the father\xe2\x80\x99s side a Scadgers.  Lady Scadgers (an immensely\r\nfat old woman, with an inordinate appetite for butcher\xe2\x80\x99s meat, and a\r\nmysterious leg which had now refused to get out of bed for fourteen\r\nyears) contrived the marriage, at a period when Sparsit was just of age,\r\nand chiefly noticeable for a slender body, weakly supported on two long\r\nslim props, and surmounted by no head worth mentioning.  He inherited a\r\nfair fortune from his uncle, but owed it all before he came into it, and\r\nspent it twice over immediately afterwards.  Thus, when he died, at\r\ntwenty-four (the scene of his decease, Calais, and the cause, brandy), he\r\ndid not leave his widow, from whom he had been separated soon after the\r\nhoneymoon, in affluent circumstances.  That bereaved lady, fifteen years\r\nolder than he, fell presently at deadly feud with her only relative, Lady\r\nScadgers; and, partly to spite her ladyship, and partly to maintain\r\nherself, went out at a salary.  And here she was now, in her elderly\r\ndays, with the Coriolanian style of nose and the dense black eyebrows\r\nwhich had captivated Sparsit, making Mr. Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s tea as he took his\r\nbreakfast.\r\n\r\nIf Bounderby had been a Conqueror, and Mrs. Sparsit a captive Princess\r\nwhom he took about as a feature in his state-processions, he could not\r\nhave made a greater flourish with her than he habitually did.  Just as it\r\nbelonged to his boastfulness to depreciate his own extraction, so it\r\nbelonged to it to exalt Mrs. Sparsit\xe2\x80\x99s.  In the measure that he would not\r\nallow his own youth to have been attended by a single favourable\r\ncircumstance, he brightened Mrs. Sparsit\xe2\x80\x99s juvenile career with every\r\npossible advantage, and showered waggon-loads of early roses all over\r\nthat lady\xe2\x80\x99s path.  \xe2\x80\x98And yet, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 he would say, \xe2\x80\x98how does it turn out\r\nafter all?  Why here she is at a hundred a year (I give her a hundred,\r\nwhich she is pleased to term handsome), keeping the house of Josiah\r\nBounderby of Coketown!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nNay, he made this foil of his so very widely known, that third parties\r\ntook it up, and handled it on some occasions with considerable briskness.\r\nIt was one of the most exasperating attributes of Bounderby, that he not\r\nonly sang his own praises but stimulated other men to sing them.  There\r\nwas a moral infection of clap-trap in him.  Strangers, modest enough\r\nelsewhere, started up at dinners in Coketown, and boasted, in quite a\r\nrampant way, of Bounderby.  They made him out to be the Royal arms, the\r\nUnion-Jack, Magna Charta, John Bull, Habeas Corpus, the Bill of Rights,\r\nAn Englishman\xe2\x80\x99s house is his castle, Church and State, and God save the\r\nQueen, all put together.  And as often (and it was very often) as an\r\norator of this kind brought into his peroration,\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x98Princes and lords may flourish or may fade,\r\n    A breath can make them, as a breath has made,\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x94it was, for certain, more or less understood among the company that he\r\nhad heard of Mrs. Sparsit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mr. Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit, \xe2\x80\x98you are unusually slow, sir, with\r\nyour breakfast this morning.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 he returned, \xe2\x80\x98I am thinking about Tom Gradgrind\xe2\x80\x99s whim;\xe2\x80\x99\r\nTom Gradgrind, for a bluff independent manner of speaking\xe2\x80\x94as if somebody\r\nwere always endeavouring to bribe him with immense sums to say Thomas,\r\nand he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t; \xe2\x80\x98Tom Gradgrind\xe2\x80\x99s whim, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, of bringing up the\r\ntumbling-girl.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The girl is now waiting to know,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit, \xe2\x80\x98whether she is to\r\ngo straight to the school, or up to the Lodge.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98She must wait, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 answered Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98till I know myself.  We\r\nshall have Tom Gradgrind down here presently, I suppose.  If he should\r\nwish her to remain here a day or two longer, of course she can, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Of course she can if you wish it, Mr. Bounderby.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I told him I would give her a shake-down here, last night, in order that\r\nhe might sleep on it before he decided to let her have any association\r\nwith Louisa.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Indeed, Mr. Bounderby?  Very thoughtful of you!\xe2\x80\x99  Mrs. Sparsit\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nCoriolanian nose underwent a slight expansion of the nostrils, and her\r\nblack eyebrows contracted as she took a sip of tea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s tolerably clear to _me_,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98that the little puss can\r\nget small good out of such companionship.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Are you speaking of young Miss Gradgrind, Mr. Bounderby?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, I\xe2\x80\x99m speaking of Louisa.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Your observation being limited to \xe2\x80\x9clittle puss,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98and there being two little girls in question, I did not know which might\r\nbe indicated by that expression.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Louisa,\xe2\x80\x99 repeated Mr. Bounderby.  \xe2\x80\x98Louisa, Louisa.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are quite another father to Louisa, sir.\xe2\x80\x99  Mrs. Sparsit took a\r\nlittle more tea; and, as she bent her again contracted eyebrows over her\r\nsteaming cup, rather looked as if her classical countenance were invoking\r\nthe infernal gods.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If you had said I was another father to Tom\xe2\x80\x94young Tom, I mean, not my\r\nfriend Tom Gradgrind\xe2\x80\x94you might have been nearer the mark.  I am going to\r\ntake young Tom into my office.  Going to have him under my wing, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Indeed?  Rather young for that, is he not, sir?\xe2\x80\x99  Mrs. Sparsit\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x98sir,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nin addressing Mr. Bounderby, was a word of ceremony, rather exacting\r\nconsideration for herself in the use, than honouring him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m not going to take him at once; he is to finish his educational\r\ncramming before then,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby.  \xe2\x80\x98By the Lord Harry, he\xe2\x80\x99ll have\r\nenough of it, first and last!  He\xe2\x80\x99d open his eyes, that boy would, if he\r\nknew how empty of learning _my_ young maw was, at his time of life.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nWhich, by the by, he probably did know, for he had heard of it often\r\nenough.  \xe2\x80\x98But it\xe2\x80\x99s extraordinary the difficulty I have on scores of such\r\nsubjects, in speaking to any one on equal terms.  Here, for example, I\r\nhave been speaking to you this morning about tumblers.  Why, what do\r\n_you_ know about tumblers?  At the time when, to have been a tumbler in\r\nthe mud of the streets, would have been a godsend to me, a prize in the\r\nlottery to me, you were at the Italian Opera.  You were coming out of the\r\nItalian Opera, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, in white satin and jewels, a blaze of splendour,\r\nwhen I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t a penny to buy a link to light you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I certainly, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Mrs. Sparsit, with a dignity serenely\r\nmournful, \xe2\x80\x98was familiar with the Italian Opera at a very early age.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Egad, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, so was I,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x94with the wrong side of it.  A\r\nhard bed the pavement of its Arcade used to make, I assure you.  People\r\nlike you, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, accustomed from infancy to lie on Down feathers, have no\r\nidea _how_ hard a paving-stone is, without trying it.  No, no, it\xe2\x80\x99s of no\r\nuse my talking to _you_ about tumblers.  I should speak of foreign\r\ndancers, and the West End of London, and May Fair, and lords and ladies\r\nand honourables.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I trust, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 rejoined Mrs. Sparsit, with decent resignation, \xe2\x80\x98it is\r\nnot necessary that you should do anything of that kind.  I hope I have\r\nlearnt how to accommodate myself to the changes of life.  If I have\r\nacquired an interest in hearing of your instructive experiences, and can\r\nscarcely hear enough of them, I claim no merit for that, since I believe\r\nit is a general sentiment.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 said her patron, \xe2\x80\x98perhaps some people may be pleased to\r\nsay that they do like to hear, in his own unpolished way, what Josiah\r\nBounderby, of Coketown, has gone through.  But you must confess that you\r\nwere born in the lap of luxury, yourself.  Come, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, you know you were\r\nborn in the lap of luxury.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I do not, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Mrs. Sparsit with a shake of her head, \xe2\x80\x98deny\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. Bounderby was obliged to get up from table, and stand with his back\r\nto the fire, looking at her; she was such an enhancement of his position.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And you were in crack society.  Devilish high society,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, warming\r\nhis legs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It is true, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Mrs. Sparsit, with an affectation of humility\r\nthe very opposite of his, and therefore in no danger of jostling it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You were in the tiptop fashion, and all the rest of it,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr.\r\nBounderby.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Mrs. Sparsit, with a kind of social widowhood upon\r\nher.  \xe2\x80\x98It is unquestionably true.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. Bounderby, bending himself at the knees, literally embraced his legs\r\nin his great satisfaction and laughed aloud.  Mr. and Miss Gradgrind\r\nbeing then announced, he received the former with a shake of the hand,\r\nand the latter with a kiss.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Can Jupe be sent here, Bounderby?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Mr. Gradgrind.\r\n\r\nCertainly.  So Jupe was sent there.  On coming in, she curtseyed to Mr.\r\nBounderby, and to his friend Tom Gradgrind, and also to Louisa; but in\r\nher confusion unluckily omitted Mrs. Sparsit.  Observing this, the\r\nblustrous Bounderby had the following remarks to make:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now, I tell you what, my girl.  The name of that lady by the teapot, is\r\nMrs. Sparsit.  That lady acts as mistress of this house, and she is a\r\nhighly connected lady.  Consequently, if ever you come again into any\r\nroom in this house, you will make a short stay in it if you don\xe2\x80\x99t behave\r\ntowards that lady in your most respectful manner.  Now, I don\xe2\x80\x99t care a\r\nbutton what you do to _me_, because I don\xe2\x80\x99t affect to be anybody.  So far\r\nfrom having high connections I have no connections at all, and I come of\r\nthe scum of the earth.  But towards that lady, I do care what you do; and\r\nyou shall do what is deferential and respectful, or you shall not come\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I hope, Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Gradgrind, in a conciliatory voice, \xe2\x80\x98that\r\nthis was merely an oversight.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My friend Tom Gradgrind suggests, Mrs. Sparsit,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98that\r\nthis was merely an oversight.  Very likely.  However, as you are aware,\r\nma\xe2\x80\x99am, I don\xe2\x80\x99t allow of even oversights towards you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are very good indeed, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Mrs. Sparsit, shaking her head\r\nwith her State humility.  \xe2\x80\x98It is not worth speaking of.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSissy, who all this time had been faintly excusing herself with tears in\r\nher eyes, was now waved over by the master of the house to Mr. Gradgrind.\r\nShe stood looking intently at him, and Louisa stood coldly by, with her\r\neyes upon the ground, while he proceeded thus:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Jupe, I have made up my mind to take you into my house; and, when you\r\nare not in attendance at the school, to employ you about Mrs. Gradgrind,\r\nwho is rather an invalid.  I have explained to Miss Louisa\xe2\x80\x94this is Miss\r\nLouisa\xe2\x80\x94the miserable but natural end of your late career; and you are to\r\nexpressly understand that the whole of that subject is past, and is not\r\nto be referred to any more.  From this time you begin your history.  You\r\nare, at present, ignorant, I know.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, sir, very,\xe2\x80\x99 she answered, curtseying.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I shall have the satisfaction of causing you to be strictly educated;\r\nand you will be a living proof to all who come into communication with\r\nyou, of the advantages of the training you will receive.  You will be\r\nreclaimed and formed.  You have been in the habit now of reading to your\r\nfather, and those people I found you among, I dare say?\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr.\r\nGradgrind, beckoning her nearer to him before he said so, and dropping\r\nhis voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Only to father and Merrylegs, sir.  At least I mean to father, when\r\nMerrylegs was always there.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Never mind Merrylegs, Jupe,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Gradgrind, with a passing frown.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t ask about him.  I understand you to have been in the habit of\r\nreading to your father?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98O, yes, sir, thousands of times.  They were the happiest\xe2\x80\x94O, of all the\r\nhappy times we had together, sir!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIt was only now when her sorrow broke out, that Louisa looked at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And what,\xe2\x80\x99 asked Mr. Gradgrind, in a still lower voice, \xe2\x80\x98did you read to\r\nyour father, Jupe?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98About the Fairies, sir, and the Dwarf, and the Hunchback, and the\r\nGenies,\xe2\x80\x99 she sobbed out; \xe2\x80\x98and about\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Hush!\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Gradgrind, \xe2\x80\x98that is enough.  Never breathe a word of\r\nsuch destructive nonsense any more.  Bounderby, this is a case for rigid\r\ntraining, and I shall observe it with interest.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Mr. Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98I have given you my opinion already, and\r\nI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do as you do.  But, very well, very well.  Since you are bent\r\nupon it, _very_ well!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSo, Mr. Gradgrind and his daughter took Cecilia Jupe off with them to\r\nStone Lodge, and on the way Louisa never spoke one word, good or bad.\r\nAnd Mr. Bounderby went about his daily pursuits.  And Mrs. Sparsit got\r\nbehind her eyebrows and meditated in the gloom of that retreat, all the\r\nevening.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII\r\nNEVER WONDER\r\n\r\n\r\nLET us strike the key-note again, before pursuing the tune.\r\n\r\nWhen she was half a dozen years younger, Louisa had been overheard to\r\nbegin a conversation with her brother one day, by saying \xe2\x80\x98Tom, I\r\nwonder\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94upon which Mr. Gradgrind, who was the person overhearing, stepped\r\nforth into the light and said, \xe2\x80\x98Louisa, never wonder!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHerein lay the spring of the mechanical art and mystery of educating the\r\nreason without stooping to the cultivation of the sentiments and\r\naffections.  Never wonder.  By means of addition, subtraction,\r\nmultiplication, and division, settle everything somehow, and never\r\nwonder.  Bring to me, says M\xe2\x80\x99Choakumchild, yonder baby just able to walk,\r\nand I will engage that it shall never wonder.\r\n\r\nNow, besides very many babies just able to walk, there happened to be in\r\nCoketown a considerable population of babies who had been walking against\r\ntime towards the infinite world, twenty, thirty, forty, fifty years and\r\nmore.  These portentous infants being alarming creatures to stalk about\r\nin any human society, the eighteen denominations incessantly scratched\r\none another\xe2\x80\x99s faces and pulled one another\xe2\x80\x99s hair by way of agreeing on\r\nthe steps to be taken for their improvement\xe2\x80\x94which they never did; a\r\nsurprising circumstance, when the happy adaptation of the means to the\r\nend is considered.  Still, although they differed in every other\r\nparticular, conceivable and inconceivable (especially inconceivable),\r\nthey were pretty well united on the point that these unlucky infants were\r\nnever to wonder.  Body number one, said they must take everything on\r\ntrust.  Body number two, said they must take everything on political\r\neconomy.  Body number three, wrote leaden little books for them, showing\r\nhow the good grown-up baby invariably got to the Savings-bank, and the\r\nbad grown-up baby invariably got transported.  Body number four, under\r\ndreary pretences of being droll (when it was very melancholy indeed),\r\nmade the shallowest pretences of concealing pitfalls of knowledge, into\r\nwhich it was the duty of these babies to be smuggled and inveigled.  But,\r\nall the bodies agreed that they were never to wonder.\r\n\r\nThere was a library in Coketown, to which general access was easy.  Mr.\r\nGradgrind greatly tormented his mind about what the people read in this\r\nlibrary: a point whereon little rivers of tabular statements periodically\r\nflowed into the howling ocean of tabular statements, which no diver ever\r\ngot to any depth in and came up sane.  It was a disheartening\r\ncircumstance, but a melancholy fact, that even these readers persisted in\r\nwondering.  They wondered about human nature, human passions, human hopes\r\nand fears, the struggles, triumphs and defeats, the cares and joys and\r\nsorrows, the lives and deaths of common men and women!  They sometimes,\r\nafter fifteen hours\xe2\x80\x99 work, sat down to read mere fables about men and\r\nwomen, more or less like themselves, and about children, more or less\r\nlike their own.  They took De Foe to their bosoms, instead of Euclid, and\r\nseemed to be on the whole more comforted by Goldsmith than by Cocker.\r\nMr. Gradgrind was for ever working, in print and out of print, at this\r\neccentric sum, and he never could make out how it yielded this\r\nunaccountable product.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am sick of my life, Loo.  I, hate it altogether, and I hate everybody\r\nexcept you,\xe2\x80\x99 said the unnatural young Thomas Gradgrind in the\r\nhair-cutting chamber at twilight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You don\xe2\x80\x99t hate Sissy, Tom?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I hate to be obliged to call her Jupe.  And she hates me,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom,\r\nmoodily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, she does not, Tom, I am sure!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98She must,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom.  \xe2\x80\x98She must just hate and detest the whole set-out\r\nof us.  They\xe2\x80\x99ll bother her head off, I think, before they have done with\r\nher.  Already she\xe2\x80\x99s getting as pale as wax, and as heavy as\xe2\x80\x94I am.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nYoung Thomas expressed these sentiments sitting astride of a chair before\r\nthe fire, with his arms on the back, and his sulky face on his arms.  His\r\nsister sat in the darker corner by the fireside, now looking at him, now\r\nlooking at the bright sparks as they dropped upon the hearth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98As to me,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom, tumbling his hair all manner of ways with his sulky\r\nhands, \xe2\x80\x98I am a Donkey, that\xe2\x80\x99s what _I_ am.  I am as obstinate as one, I\r\nam more stupid than one, I get as much pleasure as one, and I should like\r\nto kick like one.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Not me, I hope, Tom?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, Loo; I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t hurt _you_.  I made an exception of you at first.  I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know what this\xe2\x80\x94jolly old\xe2\x80\x94Jaundiced Jail,\xe2\x80\x99 Tom had paused to find a\r\nsufficiently complimentary and expressive name for the parental roof, and\r\nseemed to relieve his mind for a moment by the strong alliteration of\r\nthis one, \xe2\x80\x98would be without you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Indeed, Tom?  Do you really and truly say so?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why, of course I do.  What\xe2\x80\x99s the use of talking about it!\xe2\x80\x99 returned Tom,\r\nchafing his face on his coat-sleeve, as if to mortify his flesh, and have\r\nit in unison with his spirit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Because, Tom,\xe2\x80\x99 said his sister, after silently watching the sparks\r\nawhile, \xe2\x80\x98as I get older, and nearer growing up, I often sit wondering\r\nhere, and think how unfortunate it is for me that I can\xe2\x80\x99t reconcile you\r\nto home better than I am able to do.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what other girls know.\r\nI can\xe2\x80\x99t play to you, or sing to you.  I can\xe2\x80\x99t talk to you so as to\r\nlighten your mind, for I never see any amusing sights or read any amusing\r\nbooks that it would be a pleasure or a relief to you to talk about, when\r\nyou are tired.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, no more do I.  I am as bad as you in that respect; and I am a Mule\r\ntoo, which you\xe2\x80\x99re not.  If father was determined to make me either a Prig\r\nor a Mule, and I am not a Prig, why, it stands to reason, I must be a\r\nMule.  And so I am,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom, desperately.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s a great pity,\xe2\x80\x99 said Louisa, after another pause, and speaking\r\nthoughtfully out of her dark corner: \xe2\x80\x98it\xe2\x80\x99s a great pity, Tom.  It\xe2\x80\x99s very\r\nunfortunate for both of us.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh!  You,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom; \xe2\x80\x98you are a girl, Loo, and a girl comes out of it\r\nbetter than a boy does.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t miss anything in you.  You are the only\r\npleasure I have\xe2\x80\x94you can brighten even this place\xe2\x80\x94and you can always lead\r\nme as you like.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are a dear brother, Tom; and while you think I can do such things, I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t so much mind knowing better.  Though I do know better, Tom, and am\r\nvery sorry for it.\xe2\x80\x99  She came and kissed him, and went back into her\r\ncorner again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I wish I could collect all the Facts we hear so much about,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom,\r\nspitefully setting his teeth, \xe2\x80\x98and all the Figures, and all the people\r\nwho found them out: and I wish I could put a thousand barrels of\r\ngunpowder under them, and blow them all up together!  However, when I go\r\nto live with old Bounderby, I\xe2\x80\x99ll have my revenge.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Your revenge, Tom?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I mean, I\xe2\x80\x99ll enjoy myself a little, and go about and see something, and\r\nhear something.  I\xe2\x80\x99ll recompense myself for the way in which I have been\r\nbrought up.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But don\xe2\x80\x99t disappoint yourself beforehand, Tom.  Mr. Bounderby thinks as\r\nfather thinks, and is a great deal rougher, and not half so kind.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh!\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom, laughing; \xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind that.  I shall very well know how\r\nto manage and smooth old Bounderby!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nTheir shadows were defined upon the wall, but those of the high presses\r\nin the room were all blended together on the wall and on the ceiling, as\r\nif the brother and sister were overhung by a dark cavern.  Or, a fanciful\r\nimagination\xe2\x80\x94if such treason could have been there\xe2\x80\x94might have made it out\r\nto be the shadow of their subject, and of its lowering association with\r\ntheir future.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What is your great mode of smoothing and managing, Tom?  Is it a\r\nsecret?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh!\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom, \xe2\x80\x98if it is a secret, it\xe2\x80\x99s not far off.  It\xe2\x80\x99s you.  You are\r\nhis little pet, you are his favourite; he\xe2\x80\x99ll do anything for you.  When\r\nhe says to me what I don\xe2\x80\x99t like, I shall say to him, \xe2\x80\x9cMy sister Loo will\r\nbe hurt and disappointed, Mr. Bounderby.  She always used to tell me she\r\nwas sure you would be easier with me than this.\xe2\x80\x9d  That\xe2\x80\x99ll bring him\r\nabout, or nothing will.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAfter waiting for some answering remark, and getting none, Tom wearily\r\nrelapsed into the present time, and twined himself yawning round and\r\nabout the rails of his chair, and rumpled his head more and more, until\r\nhe suddenly looked up, and asked:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Have you gone to sleep, Loo?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, Tom.  I am looking at the fire.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You seem to find more to look at in it than ever I could find,\xe2\x80\x99 said\r\nTom.  \xe2\x80\x98Another of the advantages, I suppose, of being a girl.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Tom,\xe2\x80\x99 enquired his sister, slowly, and in a curious tone, as if she were\r\nreading what she asked in the fire, and it was not quite plainly written\r\nthere, \xe2\x80\x98do you look forward with any satisfaction to this change to Mr.\r\nBounderby\xe2\x80\x99s?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why, there\xe2\x80\x99s one thing to be said of it,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Tom, pushing his\r\nchair from him, and standing up; \xe2\x80\x98it will be getting away from home.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There is one thing to be said of it,\xe2\x80\x99 Louisa repeated in her former\r\ncurious tone; \xe2\x80\x98it will be getting away from home.  Yes.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Not but what I shall be very unwilling, both to leave you, Loo, and to\r\nleave you here.  But I must go, you know, whether I like it or not; and I\r\nhad better go where I can take with me some advantage of your influence,\r\nthan where I should lose it altogether.  Don\xe2\x80\x99t you see?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, Tom.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe answer was so long in coming, though there was no indecision in it,\r\nthat Tom went and leaned on the back of her chair, to contemplate the\r\nfire which so engrossed her, from her point of view, and see what he\r\ncould make of it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Except that it is a fire,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom, \xe2\x80\x98it looks to me as stupid and blank\r\nas everything else looks.  What do you see in it?  Not a circus?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t see anything in it, Tom, particularly.  But since I have been\r\nlooking at it, I have been wondering about you and me, grown up.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Wondering again!\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I have such unmanageable thoughts,\xe2\x80\x99 returned his sister, \xe2\x80\x98that they\r\n_will_ wonder.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then I beg of you, Louisa,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Gradgrind, who had opened the door\r\nwithout being heard, \xe2\x80\x98to do nothing of that description, for goodness\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsake, you inconsiderate girl, or I shall never hear the last of it from\r\nyour father.  And, Thomas, it is really shameful, with my poor head\r\ncontinually wearing me out, that a boy brought up as you have been, and\r\nwhose education has cost what yours has, should be found encouraging his\r\nsister to wonder, when he knows his father has expressly said that she is\r\nnot to do it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nLouisa denied Tom\xe2\x80\x99s participation in the offence; but her mother stopped\r\nher with the conclusive answer, \xe2\x80\x98Louisa, don\xe2\x80\x99t tell me, in my state of\r\nhealth; for unless you had been encouraged, it is morally and physically\r\nimpossible that you could have done it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I was encouraged by nothing, mother, but by looking at the red sparks\r\ndropping out of the fire, and whitening and dying.  It made me think,\r\nafter all, how short my life would be, and how little I could hope to do\r\nin it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Nonsense!\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Gradgrind, rendered almost energetic.  \xe2\x80\x98Nonsense!\r\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t stand there and tell me such stuff, Louisa, to my face, when you\r\nknow very well that if it was ever to reach your father\xe2\x80\x99s ears I should\r\nnever hear the last of it.  After all the trouble that has been taken\r\nwith you!  After the lectures you have attended, and the experiments you\r\nhave seen!  After I have heard you myself, when the whole of my right\r\nside has been benumbed, going on with your master about combustion, and\r\ncalcination, and calorification, and I may say every kind of ation that\r\ncould drive a poor invalid distracted, to hear you talking in this absurd\r\nway about sparks and ashes!  I wish,\xe2\x80\x99 whimpered Mrs. Gradgrind, taking a\r\nchair, and discharging her strongest point before succumbing under these\r\nmere shadows of facts, \xe2\x80\x98yes, I really _do_ wish that I had never had a\r\nfamily, and then you would have known what it was to do without me!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX\r\nSISSY\xe2\x80\x99S PROGRESS\r\n\r\n\r\nSISSY JUPE had not an easy time of it, between Mr. M\xe2\x80\x99Choakumchild and\r\nMrs. Gradgrind, and was not without strong impulses, in the first months\r\nof her probation, to run away.  It hailed facts all day long so very\r\nhard, and life in general was opened to her as such a closely ruled\r\nciphering-book, that assuredly she would have run away, but for only one\r\nrestraint.\r\n\r\nIt is lamentable to think of; but this restraint was the result of no\r\narithmetical process, was self-imposed in defiance of all calculation,\r\nand went dead against any table of probabilities that any Actuary would\r\nhave drawn up from the premises.  The girl believed that her father had\r\nnot deserted her; she lived in the hope that he would come back, and in\r\nthe faith that he would be made the happier by her remaining where she\r\nwas.\r\n\r\nThe wretched ignorance with which Jupe clung to this consolation,\r\nrejecting the superior comfort of knowing, on a sound arithmetical basis,\r\nthat her father was an unnatural vagabond, filled Mr. Gradgrind with\r\npity.  Yet, what was to be done?  M\xe2\x80\x99Choakumchild reported that she had a\r\nvery dense head for figures; that, once possessed with a general idea of\r\nthe globe, she took the smallest conceivable interest in its exact\r\nmeasurements; that she was extremely slow in the acquisition of dates,\r\nunless some pitiful incident happened to be connected therewith; that she\r\nwould burst into tears on being required (by the mental process)\r\nimmediately to name the cost of two hundred and forty-seven muslin caps\r\nat fourteen-pence halfpenny; that she was as low down, in the school, as\r\nlow could be; that after eight weeks of induction into the elements of\r\nPolitical Economy, she had only yesterday been set right by a prattler\r\nthree feet high, for returning to the question, \xe2\x80\x98What is the first\r\nprinciple of this science?\xe2\x80\x99 the absurd answer, \xe2\x80\x98To do unto others as I\r\nwould that they should do unto me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. Gradgrind observed, shaking his head, that all this was very bad;\r\nthat it showed the necessity of infinite grinding at the mill of\r\nknowledge, as per system, schedule, blue book, report, and tabular\r\nstatements A to Z; and that Jupe \xe2\x80\x98must be kept to it.\xe2\x80\x99  So Jupe was kept\r\nto it, and became low-spirited, but no wiser.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It would be a fine thing to be you, Miss Louisa!\xe2\x80\x99 she said, one night,\r\nwhen Louisa had endeavoured to make her perplexities for next day\r\nsomething clearer to her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Do you think so?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I should know so much, Miss Louisa.  All that is difficult to me now,\r\nwould be so easy then.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You might not be the better for it, Sissy.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSissy submitted, after a little hesitation, \xe2\x80\x98I should not be the worse,\r\nMiss Louisa.\xe2\x80\x99  To which Miss Louisa answered, \xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThere had been so little communication between these two\xe2\x80\x94both because\r\nlife at Stone Lodge went monotonously round like a piece of machinery\r\nwhich discouraged human interference, and because of the prohibition\r\nrelative to Sissy\xe2\x80\x99s past career\xe2\x80\x94that they were still almost strangers.\r\nSissy, with her dark eyes wonderingly directed to Louisa\xe2\x80\x99s face, was\r\nuncertain whether to say more or to remain silent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are more useful to my mother, and more pleasant with her than I can\r\never be,\xe2\x80\x99 Louisa resumed.  \xe2\x80\x98You are pleasanter to yourself, than _I_ am\r\nto _my_self.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But, if you please, Miss Louisa,\xe2\x80\x99 Sissy pleaded, \xe2\x80\x98I am\xe2\x80\x94O so stupid!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nLouisa, with a brighter laugh than usual, told her she would be wiser\r\nby-and-by.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x99 said Sissy, half crying, \xe2\x80\x98what a stupid girl I am.  All\r\nthrough school hours I make mistakes.  Mr. and Mrs. M\xe2\x80\x99Choakumchild call\r\nme up, over and over again, regularly to make mistakes.  I can\xe2\x80\x99t help\r\nthem.  They seem to come natural to me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mr. and Mrs. M\xe2\x80\x99Choakumchild never make any mistakes themselves, I\r\nsuppose, Sissy?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98O no!\xe2\x80\x99 she eagerly returned.  \xe2\x80\x98They know everything.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Tell me some of your mistakes.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am almost ashamed,\xe2\x80\x99 said Sissy, with reluctance.  \xe2\x80\x98But to-day, for\r\ninstance, Mr. M\xe2\x80\x99Choakumchild was explaining to us about Natural\r\nProsperity.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98National, I think it must have been,\xe2\x80\x99 observed Louisa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, it was.\xe2\x80\x94But isn\xe2\x80\x99t it the same?\xe2\x80\x99 she timidly asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You had better say, National, as he said so,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Louisa, with her\r\ndry reserve.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98National Prosperity.  And he said, Now, this schoolroom is a Nation.\r\nAnd in this nation, there are fifty millions of money.  Isn\xe2\x80\x99t this a\r\nprosperous nation?  Girl number twenty, isn\xe2\x80\x99t this a prosperous nation,\r\nand a\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t you in a thriving state?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What did you say?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Louisa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Miss Louisa, I said I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know.  I thought I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t know whether it\r\nwas a prosperous nation or not, and whether I was in a thriving state or\r\nnot, unless I knew who had got the money, and whether any of it was mine.\r\nBut that had nothing to do with it.  It was not in the figures at all,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsaid Sissy, wiping her eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That was a great mistake of yours,\xe2\x80\x99 observed Louisa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, Miss Louisa, I know it was, now.  Then Mr. M\xe2\x80\x99Choakumchild said he\r\nwould try me again.  And he said, This schoolroom is an immense town, and\r\nin it there are a million of inhabitants, and only five-and-twenty are\r\nstarved to death in the streets, in the course of a year.  What is your\r\nremark on that proportion?  And my remark was\xe2\x80\x94for I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t think of a\r\nbetter one\xe2\x80\x94that I thought it must be just as hard upon those who were\r\nstarved, whether the others were a million, or a million million.  And\r\nthat was wrong, too.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Of course it was.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then Mr. M\xe2\x80\x99Choakumchild said he would try me once more.  And he said,\r\nHere are the stutterings\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Statistics,\xe2\x80\x99 said Louisa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, Miss Louisa\xe2\x80\x94they always remind me of stutterings, and that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nanother of my mistakes\xe2\x80\x94of accidents upon the sea.  And I find (Mr.\r\nM\xe2\x80\x99Choakumchild said) that in a given time a hundred thousand persons went\r\nto sea on long voyages, and only five hundred of them were drowned or\r\nburnt to death.  What is the percentage?  And I said, Miss;\xe2\x80\x99 here Sissy\r\nfairly sobbed as confessing with extreme contrition to her greatest\r\nerror; \xe2\x80\x98I said it was nothing.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Nothing, Sissy?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Nothing, Miss\xe2\x80\x94to the relations and friends of the people who were\r\nkilled.  I shall never learn,\xe2\x80\x99 said Sissy.  \xe2\x80\x98And the worst of all is,\r\nthat although my poor father wished me so much to learn, and although I\r\nam so anxious to learn, because he wished me to, I am afraid I don\xe2\x80\x99t like\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nLouisa stood looking at the pretty modest head, as it drooped abashed\r\nbefore her, until it was raised again to glance at her face.  Then she\r\nasked:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Did your father know so much himself, that he wished you to be well\r\ntaught too, Sissy?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSissy hesitated before replying, and so plainly showed her sense that\r\nthey were entering on forbidden ground, that Louisa added, \xe2\x80\x98No one hears\r\nus; and if any one did, I am sure no harm could be found in such an\r\ninnocent question.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, Miss Louisa,\xe2\x80\x99 answered Sissy, upon this encouragement, shaking her\r\nhead; \xe2\x80\x98father knows very little indeed.  It\xe2\x80\x99s as much as he can do to\r\nwrite; and it\xe2\x80\x99s more than people in general can do to read his writing.\r\nThough it\xe2\x80\x99s plain to _me_.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Your mother?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Father says she was quite a scholar.  She died when I was born.  She\r\nwas;\xe2\x80\x99 Sissy made the terrible communication nervously; \xe2\x80\x98she was a\r\ndancer.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Did your father love her?\xe2\x80\x99  Louisa asked these questions with a strong,\r\nwild, wandering interest peculiar to her; an interest gone astray like a\r\nbanished creature, and hiding in solitary places.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98O yes!  As dearly as he loves me.  Father loved me, first, for her sake.\r\nHe carried me about with him when I was quite a baby.  We have never been\r\nasunder from that time.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yet he leaves you now, Sissy?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Only for my good.  Nobody understands him as I do; nobody knows him as I\r\ndo.  When he left me for my good\xe2\x80\x94he never would have left me for his\r\nown\xe2\x80\x94I know he was almost broken-hearted with the trial.  He will not be\r\nhappy for a single minute, till he comes back.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Tell me more about him,\xe2\x80\x99 said Louisa, \xe2\x80\x98I will never ask you again.\r\nWhere did you live?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98We travelled about the country, and had no fixed place to live in.\r\nFather\xe2\x80\x99s a;\xe2\x80\x99 Sissy whispered the awful word, \xe2\x80\x98a clown.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98To make the people laugh?\xe2\x80\x99 said Louisa, with a nod of intelligence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes.  But they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t laugh sometimes, and then father cried.  Lately,\r\nthey very often wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t laugh, and he used to come home despairing.\r\nFather\xe2\x80\x99s not like most.  Those who didn\xe2\x80\x99t know him as well as I do, and\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t love him as dearly as I do, might believe he was not quite right.\r\nSometimes they played tricks upon him; but they never knew how he felt\r\nthem, and shrunk up, when he was alone with me.  He was far, far timider\r\nthan they thought!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And you were his comfort through everything?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe nodded, with the tears rolling down her face.  \xe2\x80\x98I hope so, and father\r\nsaid I was.  It was because he grew so scared and trembling, and because\r\nhe felt himself to be a poor, weak, ignorant, helpless man (those used to\r\nbe his words), that he wanted me so much to know a great deal, and be\r\ndifferent from him.  I used to read to him to cheer his courage, and he\r\nwas very fond of that.  They were wrong books\xe2\x80\x94I am never to speak of them\r\nhere\xe2\x80\x94but we didn\xe2\x80\x99t know there was any harm in them.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And he liked them?\xe2\x80\x99 said Louisa, with a searching gaze on Sissy all this\r\ntime.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98O very much!  They kept him, many times, from what did him real harm.\r\nAnd often and often of a night, he used to forget all his troubles in\r\nwondering whether the Sultan would let the lady go on with the story, or\r\nwould have her head cut off before it was finished.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And your father was always kind?  To the last?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Louisa\r\ncontravening the great principle, and wondering very much.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Always, always!\xe2\x80\x99 returned Sissy, clasping her hands.  \xe2\x80\x98Kinder and kinder\r\nthan I can tell.  He was angry only one night, and that was not to me,\r\nbut Merrylegs.  Merrylegs;\xe2\x80\x99 she whispered the awful fact; \xe2\x80\x98is his\r\nperforming dog.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why was he angry with the dog?\xe2\x80\x99 Louisa demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Father, soon after they came home from performing, told Merrylegs to\r\njump up on the backs of the two chairs and stand across them\xe2\x80\x94which is one\r\nof his tricks.  He looked at father, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t do it at once.\r\nEverything of father\xe2\x80\x99s had gone wrong that night, and he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t pleased\r\nthe public at all.  He cried out that the very dog knew he was failing,\r\nand had no compassion on him.  Then he beat the dog, and I was\r\nfrightened, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cFather, father!  Pray don\xe2\x80\x99t hurt the creature who\r\nis so fond of you!  O Heaven forgive you, father, stop!\xe2\x80\x9d  And he stopped,\r\nand the dog was bloody, and father lay down crying on the floor with the\r\ndog in his arms, and the dog licked his face.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nLouisa saw that she was sobbing; and going to her, kissed her, took her\r\nhand, and sat down beside her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Finish by telling me how your father left you, Sissy.  Now that I have\r\nasked you so much, tell me the end.  The blame, if there is any blame, is\r\nmine, not yours.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Dear Miss Louisa,\xe2\x80\x99 said Sissy, covering her eyes, and sobbing yet; \xe2\x80\x98I\r\ncame home from the school that afternoon, and found poor father just come\r\nhome too, from the booth.  And he sat rocking himself over the fire, as\r\nif he was in pain.  And I said, \xe2\x80\x9cHave you hurt yourself, father?\xe2\x80\x9d (as he\r\ndid sometimes, like they all did), and he said, \xe2\x80\x9cA little, my darling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAnd when I came to stoop down and look up at his face, I saw that he was\r\ncrying.  The more I spoke to him, the more he hid his face; and at first\r\nhe shook all over, and said nothing but \xe2\x80\x9cMy darling;\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cMy love!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHere Tom came lounging in, and stared at the two with a coolness not\r\nparticularly savouring of interest in anything but himself, and not much\r\nof that at present.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am asking Sissy a few questions, Tom,\xe2\x80\x99 observed his sister.  \xe2\x80\x98You have\r\nno occasion to go away; but don\xe2\x80\x99t interrupt us for a moment, Tom dear.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh! very well!\xe2\x80\x99 returned Tom.  \xe2\x80\x98Only father has brought old Bounderby\r\nhome, and I want you to come into the drawing-room.  Because if you come,\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s a good chance of old Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s asking me to dinner; and if you\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t, there\xe2\x80\x99s none.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ll come directly.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ll wait for you,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom, \xe2\x80\x98to make sure.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSissy resumed in a lower voice.  \xe2\x80\x98At last poor father said that he had\r\ngiven no satisfaction again, and never did give any satisfaction now, and\r\nthat he was a shame and disgrace, and I should have done better without\r\nhim all along.  I said all the affectionate things to him that came into\r\nmy heart, and presently he was quiet and I sat down by him, and told him\r\nall about the school and everything that had been said and done there.\r\nWhen I had no more left to tell, he put his arms round my neck, and\r\nkissed me a great many times.  Then he asked me to fetch some of the\r\nstuff he used, for the little hurt he had had, and to get it at the best\r\nplace, which was at the other end of town from there; and then, after\r\nkissing me again, he let me go.  When I had gone down-stairs, I turned\r\nback that I might be a little bit more company to him yet, and looked in\r\nat the door, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cFather dear, shall I take Merrylegs?\xe2\x80\x9d  Father\r\nshook his head and said, \xe2\x80\x9cNo, Sissy, no; take nothing that\xe2\x80\x99s known to be\r\nmine, my darling;\xe2\x80\x9d and I left him sitting by the fire.  Then the thought\r\nmust have come upon him, poor, poor father! of going away to try\r\nsomething for my sake; for when I came back, he was gone.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I say!  Look sharp for old Bounderby, Loo!\xe2\x80\x99 Tom remonstrated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There\xe2\x80\x99s no more to tell, Miss Louisa.  I keep the nine oils ready for\r\nhim, and I know he will come back.  Every letter that I see in Mr.\r\nGradgrind\xe2\x80\x99s hand takes my breath away and blinds my eyes, for I think it\r\ncomes from father, or from Mr. Sleary about father.  Mr. Sleary promised\r\nto write as soon as ever father should be heard of, and I trust to him to\r\nkeep his word.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Do look sharp for old Bounderby, Loo!\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom, with an impatient\r\nwhistle.  \xe2\x80\x98He\xe2\x80\x99ll be off if you don\xe2\x80\x99t look sharp!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAfter this, whenever Sissy dropped a curtsey to Mr. Gradgrind in the\r\npresence of his family, and said in a faltering way, \xe2\x80\x98I beg your pardon,\r\nsir, for being troublesome\xe2\x80\x94but\xe2\x80\x94have you had any letter yet about me?\xe2\x80\x99\r\nLouisa would suspend the occupation of the moment, whatever it was, and\r\nlook for the reply as earnestly as Sissy did.  And when Mr. Gradgrind\r\nregularly answered, \xe2\x80\x98No, Jupe, nothing of the sort,\xe2\x80\x99 the trembling of\r\nSissy\xe2\x80\x99s lip would be repeated in Louisa\xe2\x80\x99s face, and her eyes would follow\r\nSissy with compassion to the door.  Mr. Gradgrind usually improved these\r\noccasions by remarking, when she was gone, that if Jupe had been properly\r\ntrained from an early age she would have remonstrated to herself on sound\r\nprinciples the baselessness of these fantastic hopes.  Yet it did seem\r\n(though not to him, for he saw nothing of it) as if fantastic hope could\r\ntake as strong a hold as Fact.\r\n\r\nThis observation must be limited exclusively to his daughter.  As to Tom,\r\nhe was becoming that not unprecedented triumph of calculation which is\r\nusually at work on number one.  As to Mrs. Gradgrind, if she said\r\nanything on the subject, she would come a little way out of her wrappers,\r\nlike a feminine dormouse, and say:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Good gracious bless me, how my poor head is vexed and worried by that\r\ngirl Jupe\xe2\x80\x99s so perseveringly asking, over and over again, about her\r\ntiresome letters!  Upon my word and honour I seem to be fated, and\r\ndestined, and ordained, to live in the midst of things that I am never to\r\nhear the last of.  It really is a most extraordinary circumstance that it\r\nappears as if I never was to hear the last of anything!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAt about this point, Mr. Gradgrind\xe2\x80\x99s eye would fall upon her; and under\r\nthe influence of that wintry piece of fact, she would become torpid\r\nagain.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X\r\nSTEPHEN BLACKPOOL\r\n\r\n\r\nI ENTERTAIN a weak idea that the English people are as hard-worked as any\r\npeople upon whom the sun shines.  I acknowledge to this ridiculous\r\nidiosyncrasy, as a reason why I would give them a little more play.\r\n\r\nIn the hardest working part of Coketown; in the innermost fortifications\r\nof that ugly citadel, where Nature was as strongly bricked out as killing\r\nairs and gases were bricked in; at the heart of the labyrinth of narrow\r\ncourts upon courts, and close streets upon streets, which had come into\r\nexistence piecemeal, every piece in a violent hurry for some one man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npurpose, and the whole an unnatural family, shouldering, and trampling,\r\nand pressing one another to death; in the last close nook of this great\r\nexhausted receiver, where the chimneys, for want of air to make a\r\ndraught, were built in an immense variety of stunted and crooked shapes,\r\nas though every house put out a sign of the kind of people who might be\r\nexpected to be born in it; among the multitude of Coketown, generically\r\ncalled \xe2\x80\x98the Hands,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94a race who would have found more favour with some\r\npeople, if Providence had seen fit to make them only hands, or, like the\r\nlower creatures of the seashore, only hands and stomachs\xe2\x80\x94lived a certain\r\nStephen Blackpool, forty years of age.\r\n\r\nStephen looked older, but he had had a hard life.  It is said that every\r\nlife has its roses and thorns; there seemed, however, to have been a\r\nmisadventure or mistake in Stephen\xe2\x80\x99s case, whereby somebody else had\r\nbecome possessed of his roses, and he had become possessed of the same\r\nsomebody else\xe2\x80\x99s thorns in addition to his own.  He had known, to use his\r\nwords, a peck of trouble.  He was usually called Old Stephen, in a kind\r\nof rough homage to the fact.\r\n\r\nA rather stooping man, with a knitted brow, a pondering expression of\r\nface, and a hard-looking head sufficiently capacious, on which his\r\niron-grey hair lay long and thin, Old Stephen might have passed for a\r\nparticularly intelligent man in his condition.  Yet he was not.  He took\r\nno place among those remarkable \xe2\x80\x98Hands,\xe2\x80\x99 who, piecing together their\r\nbroken intervals of leisure through many years, had mastered difficult\r\nsciences, and acquired a knowledge of most unlikely things.  He held no\r\nstation among the Hands who could make speeches and carry on debates.\r\nThousands of his compeers could talk much better than he, at any time.\r\nHe was a good power-loom weaver, and a man of perfect integrity.  What\r\nmore he was, or what else he had in him, if anything, let him show for\r\nhimself.\r\n\r\nThe lights in the great factories, which looked, when they were\r\nilluminated, like Fairy palaces\xe2\x80\x94or the travellers by express-train said\r\nso\xe2\x80\x94were all extinguished; and the bells had rung for knocking off for the\r\nnight, and had ceased again; and the Hands, men and women, boy and girl,\r\nwere clattering home.  Old Stephen was standing in the street, with the\r\nold sensation upon him which the stoppage of the machinery always\r\nproduced\xe2\x80\x94the sensation of its having worked and stopped in his own head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yet I don\xe2\x80\x99t see Rachael, still!\xe2\x80\x99 said he.\r\n\r\nIt was a wet night, and many groups of young women passed him, with their\r\nshawls drawn over their bare heads and held close under their chins to\r\nkeep the rain out.  He knew Rachael well, for a glance at any one of\r\nthese groups was sufficient to show him that she was not there.  At last,\r\nthere were no more to come; and then he turned away, saying in a tone of\r\ndisappointment, \xe2\x80\x98Why, then, ha\xe2\x80\x99 missed her!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBut, he had not gone the length of three streets, when he saw another of\r\nthe shawled figures in advance of him, at which he looked so keenly that\r\nperhaps its mere shadow indistinctly reflected on the wet pavement\xe2\x80\x94if he\r\ncould have seen it without the figure itself moving along from lamp to\r\nlamp, brightening and fading as it went\xe2\x80\x94would have been enough to tell\r\nhim who was there.  Making his pace at once much quicker and much softer,\r\nhe darted on until he was very near this figure, then fell into his\r\nformer walk, and called \xe2\x80\x98Rachael!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe turned, being then in the brightness of a lamp; and raising her hood\r\na little, showed a quiet oval face, dark and rather delicate, irradiated\r\nby a pair of very gentle eyes, and further set off by the perfect order\r\nof her shining black hair.  It was not a face in its first bloom; she was\r\na woman five and thirty years of age.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah, lad!  \xe2\x80\x99Tis thou?\xe2\x80\x99  When she had said this, with a smile which would\r\nhave been quite expressed, though nothing of her had been seen but her\r\npleasant eyes, she replaced her hood again, and they went on together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I thought thou wast ahind me, Rachael?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Early t\xe2\x80\x99night, lass?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x99Times I\xe2\x80\x99m a little early, Stephen! \xe2\x80\x99times a little late.  I\xe2\x80\x99m never to\r\nbe counted on, going home.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Nor going t\xe2\x80\x99other way, neither, \xe2\x80\x99t seems to me, Rachael?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, Stephen.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe looked at her with some disappointment in his face, but with a\r\nrespectful and patient conviction that she must be right in whatever she\r\ndid.  The expression was not lost upon her; she laid her hand lightly on\r\nhis arm a moment as if to thank him for it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98We are such true friends, lad, and such old friends, and getting to be\r\nsuch old folk, now.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, Rachael, thou\xe2\x80\x99rt as young as ever thou wast.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98One of us would be puzzled how to get old, Stephen, without \xe2\x80\x99t other\r\ngetting so too, both being alive,\xe2\x80\x99 she answered, laughing; \xe2\x80\x98but, anyways,\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99re such old friends, and t\xe2\x80\x99 hide a word of honest truth fro\xe2\x80\x99 one\r\nanother would be a sin and a pity.  \xe2\x80\x99Tis better not to walk too much\r\ntogether.  \xe2\x80\x99Times, yes!  \xe2\x80\x99Twould be hard, indeed, if \xe2\x80\x99twas not to be at\r\nall,\xe2\x80\x99 she said, with a cheerfulness she sought to communicate to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x99Tis hard, anyways, Rachael.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Try to think not; and \xe2\x80\x99twill seem better.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ve tried a long time, and \xe2\x80\x99ta\xe2\x80\x99nt got better.  But thou\xe2\x80\x99rt right; \xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmight mak fok talk, even of thee.  Thou hast been that to me, Rachael,\r\nthrough so many year: thou hast done me so much good, and heartened of me\r\nin that cheering way, that thy word is a law to me.  Ah, lass, and a\r\nbright good law!  Better than some real ones.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Never fret about them, Stephen,\xe2\x80\x99 she answered quickly, and not without\r\nan anxious glance at his face.  \xe2\x80\x98Let the laws be.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, with a slow nod or two.  \xe2\x80\x98Let \xe2\x80\x99em be.  Let everything be.\r\nLet all sorts alone.  \xe2\x80\x99Tis a muddle, and that\xe2\x80\x99s aw.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Always a muddle?\xe2\x80\x99 said Rachael, with another gentle touch upon his arm,\r\nas if to recall him out of the thoughtfulness, in which he was biting the\r\nlong ends of his loose neckerchief as he walked along.  The touch had its\r\ninstantaneous effect.  He let them fall, turned a smiling face upon her,\r\nand said, as he broke into a good-humoured laugh, \xe2\x80\x98Ay, Rachael, lass,\r\nawlus a muddle.  That\xe2\x80\x99s where I stick.  I come to the muddle many times\r\nand agen, and I never get beyond it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThey had walked some distance, and were near their own homes.  The\r\nwoman\xe2\x80\x99s was the first reached.  It was in one of the many small streets\r\nfor which the favourite undertaker (who turned a handsome sum out of the\r\none poor ghastly pomp of the neighbourhood) kept a black ladder, in order\r\nthat those who had done their daily groping up and down the narrow stairs\r\nmight slide out of this working world by the windows.  She stopped at the\r\ncorner, and putting her hand in his, wished him good night.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Good night, dear lass; good night!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe went, with her neat figure and her sober womanly step, down the dark\r\nstreet, and he stood looking after her until she turned into one of the\r\nsmall houses.  There was not a flutter of her coarse shawl, perhaps, but\r\nhad its interest in this man\xe2\x80\x99s eyes; not a tone of her voice but had its\r\necho in his innermost heart.\r\n\r\nWhen she was lost to his view, he pursued his homeward way, glancing up\r\nsometimes at the sky, where the clouds were sailing fast and wildly.\r\nBut, they were broken now, and the rain had ceased, and the moon\r\nshone,\xe2\x80\x94looking down the high chimneys of Coketown on the deep furnaces\r\nbelow, and casting Titanic shadows of the steam-engines at rest, upon the\r\nwalls where they were lodged.  The man seemed to have brightened with the\r\nnight, as he went on.\r\n\r\nHis home, in such another street as the first, saving that it was\r\nnarrower, was over a little shop.  How it came to pass that any people\r\nfound it worth their while to sell or buy the wretched little toys, mixed\r\nup in its window with cheap newspapers and pork (there was a leg to be\r\nraffled for to-morrow-night), matters not here.  He took his end of\r\ncandle from a shelf, lighted it at another end of candle on the counter,\r\nwithout disturbing the mistress of the shop who was asleep in her little\r\nroom, and went upstairs into his lodging.\r\n\r\nIt was a room, not unacquainted with the black ladder under various\r\ntenants; but as neat, at present, as such a room could be.  A few books\r\nand writings were on an old bureau in a corner, the furniture was decent\r\nand sufficient, and, though the atmosphere was tainted, the room was\r\nclean.\r\n\r\nGoing to the hearth to set the candle down upon a round three-legged\r\ntable standing there, he stumbled against something.  As he recoiled,\r\nlooking down at it, it raised itself up into the form of a woman in a\r\nsitting attitude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s mercy, woman!\xe2\x80\x99 he cried, falling farther off from the figure.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Hast thou come back again!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSuch a woman!  A disabled, drunken creature, barely able to preserve her\r\nsitting posture by steadying herself with one begrimed hand on the floor,\r\nwhile the other was so purposeless in trying to push away her tangled\r\nhair from her face, that it only blinded her the more with the dirt upon\r\nit.  A creature so foul to look at, in her tatters, stains and splashes,\r\nbut so much fouler than that in her moral infamy, that it was a shameful\r\nthing even to see her.\r\n\r\nAfter an impatient oath or two, and some stupid clawing of herself with\r\nthe hand not necessary to her support, she got her hair away from her\r\neyes sufficiently to obtain a sight of him.  Then she sat swaying her\r\nbody to and fro, and making gestures with her unnerved arm, which seemed\r\nintended as the accompaniment to a fit of laughter, though her face was\r\nstolid and drowsy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Eigh, lad?  What, yo\xe2\x80\x99r there?\xe2\x80\x99  Some hoarse sounds meant for this, came\r\nmockingly out of her at last; and her head dropped forward on her breast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Back agen?\xe2\x80\x99 she screeched, after some minutes, as if he had that moment\r\nsaid it.  \xe2\x80\x98Yes!  And back agen.  Back agen ever and ever so often.  Back?\r\nYes, back.  Why not?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nRoused by the unmeaning violence with which she cried it out, she\r\nscrambled up, and stood supporting herself with her shoulders against the\r\nwall; dangling in one hand by the string, a dunghill-fragment of a\r\nbonnet, and trying to look scornfully at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ll sell thee off again, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll sell thee off again, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll sell\r\nthee off a score of times!\xe2\x80\x99 she cried, with something between a furious\r\nmenace and an effort at a defiant dance.  \xe2\x80\x98Come awa\xe2\x80\x99 from th\xe2\x80\x99 bed!\xe2\x80\x99  He\r\nwas sitting on the side of it, with his face hidden in his hands.  \xe2\x80\x98Come\r\nawa! from \xe2\x80\x99t.  \xe2\x80\x99Tis mine, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve a right to t\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAs she staggered to it, he avoided her with a shudder, and passed\xe2\x80\x94his\r\nface still hidden\xe2\x80\x94to the opposite end of the room.  She threw herself\r\nupon the bed heavily, and soon was snoring hard.  He sunk into a chair,\r\nand moved but once all that night.  It was to throw a covering over her;\r\nas if his hands were not enough to hide her, even in the darkness.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI\r\nNO WAY OUT\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE Fairy palaces burst into illumination, before pale morning showed the\r\nmonstrous serpents of smoke trailing themselves over Coketown.  A\r\nclattering of clogs upon the pavement; a rapid ringing of bells; and all\r\nthe melancholy mad elephants, polished and oiled up for the day\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmonotony, were at their heavy exercise again.\r\n\r\nStephen bent over his loom, quiet, watchful, and steady.  A special\r\ncontrast, as every man was in the forest of looms where Stephen worked,\r\nto the crashing, smashing, tearing piece of mechanism at which he\r\nlaboured.  Never fear, good people of an anxious turn of mind, that Art\r\nwill consign Nature to oblivion.  Set anywhere, side by side, the work of\r\nGOD and the work of man; and the former, even though it be a troop of\r\nHands of very small account, will gain in dignity from the comparison.\r\n\r\nSo many hundred Hands in this Mill; so many hundred horse Steam Power.\r\nIt is known, to the force of a single pound weight, what the engine will\r\ndo; but, not all the calculators of the National Debt can tell me the\r\ncapacity for good or evil, for love or hatred, for patriotism or\r\ndiscontent, for the decomposition of virtue into vice, or the reverse, at\r\nany single moment in the soul of one of these its quiet servants, with\r\nthe composed faces and the regulated actions.  There is no mystery in it;\r\nthere is an unfathomable mystery in the meanest of them, for\r\never.\xe2\x80\x94Supposing we were to reverse our arithmetic for material objects,\r\nand to govern these awful unknown quantities by other means!\r\n\r\nThe day grew strong, and showed itself outside, even against the flaming\r\nlights within.  The lights were turned out, and the work went on.  The\r\nrain fell, and the Smoke-serpents, submissive to the curse of all that\r\ntribe, trailed themselves upon the earth.  In the waste-yard outside, the\r\nsteam from the escape pipe, the litter of barrels and old iron, the\r\nshining heaps of coals, the ashes everywhere, were shrouded in a veil of\r\nmist and rain.\r\n\r\nThe work went on, until the noon-bell rang.  More clattering upon the\r\npavements.  The looms, and wheels, and Hands all out of gear for an hour.\r\n\r\nStephen came out of the hot mill into the damp wind and cold wet streets,\r\nhaggard and worn.  He turned from his own class and his own quarter,\r\ntaking nothing but a little bread as he walked along, towards the hill on\r\nwhich his principal employer lived, in a red house with black outside\r\nshutters, green inside blinds, a black street door, up two white steps,\r\nBOUNDERBY (in letters very like himself) upon a brazen plate, and a round\r\nbrazen door-handle underneath it, like a brazen full-stop.\r\n\r\nMr. Bounderby was at his lunch.  So Stephen had expected.  Would his\r\nservant say that one of the Hands begged leave to speak to him?  Message\r\nin return, requiring name of such Hand.  Stephen Blackpool.  There was\r\nnothing troublesome against Stephen Blackpool; yes, he might come in.\r\n\r\nStephen Blackpool in the parlour.  Mr. Bounderby (whom he just knew by\r\nsight), at lunch on chop and sherry.  Mrs. Sparsit netting at the\r\nfireside, in a side-saddle attitude, with one foot in a cotton stirrup.\r\nIt was a part, at once of Mrs. Sparsit\xe2\x80\x99s dignity and service, not to\r\nlunch.  She supervised the meal officially, but implied that in her own\r\nstately person she considered lunch a weakness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now, Stephen,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with _you_?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nStephen made a bow.  Not a servile one\xe2\x80\x94these Hands will never do that!\r\nLord bless you, sir, you\xe2\x80\x99ll never catch them at that, if they have been\r\nwith you twenty years!\xe2\x80\x94and, as a complimentary toilet for Mrs. Sparsit,\r\ntucked his neckerchief ends into his waistcoat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now, you know,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Bounderby, taking some sherry, \xe2\x80\x98we have never\r\nhad any difficulty with you, and you have never been one of the\r\nunreasonable ones.  You don\xe2\x80\x99t expect to be set up in a coach and six, and\r\nto be fed on turtle soup and venison, with a gold spoon, as a good many\r\nof \xe2\x80\x99em do!\xe2\x80\x99  Mr. Bounderby always represented this to be the sole,\r\nimmediate, and direct object of any Hand who was not entirely satisfied;\r\n\xe2\x80\x98and therefore I know already that you have not come here to make a\r\ncomplaint.  Now, you know, I am certain of that, beforehand.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, sir, sure I ha\xe2\x80\x99 not coom for nowt o\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 kind.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. Bounderby seemed agreeably surprised, notwithstanding his previous\r\nstrong conviction.  \xe2\x80\x98Very well,\xe2\x80\x99 he returned.  \xe2\x80\x98You\xe2\x80\x99re a steady Hand, and\r\nI was not mistaken.  Now, let me hear what it\xe2\x80\x99s all about.  As it\xe2\x80\x99s not\r\nthat, let me hear what it is.  What have you got to say?  Out with it,\r\nlad!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nStephen happened to glance towards Mrs. Sparsit.  \xe2\x80\x98I can go, Mr.\r\nBounderby, if you wish it,\xe2\x80\x99 said that self-sacrificing lady, making a\r\nfeint of taking her foot out of the stirrup.\r\n\r\nMr. Bounderby stayed her, by holding a mouthful of chop in suspension\r\nbefore swallowing it, and putting out his left hand.  Then, withdrawing\r\nhis hand and swallowing his mouthful of chop, he said to Stephen:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now you know, this good lady is a born lady, a high lady.  You are not\r\nto suppose because she keeps my house for me, that she hasn\xe2\x80\x99t been very\r\nhigh up the tree\xe2\x80\x94ah, up at the top of the tree!  Now, if you have got\r\nanything to say that can\xe2\x80\x99t be said before a born lady, this lady will\r\nleave the room.
