Anything?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, I can\xe2\x80\x99t say that I have heard anything particular.  Our\r\npeople are a bad lot, ma\xe2\x80\x99am; but that is no news, unfortunately.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What are the restless wretches doing now?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Mrs. Sparsit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Merely going on in the old way, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.  Uniting, and leaguing, and\r\nengaging to stand by one another.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It is much to be regretted,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit, making her nose more\r\nRoman and her eyebrows more Coriolanian in the strength of her severity,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98that the united masters allow of any such class-combinations.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bitzer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Being united themselves, they ought one and all to set their faces\r\nagainst employing any man who is united with any other man,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs.\r\nSparsit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98They have done that, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Bitzer; \xe2\x80\x98but it rather fell\r\nthrough, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I do not pretend to understand these things,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit, with\r\ndignity, \xe2\x80\x98my lot having been signally cast in a widely different sphere;\r\nand Mr. Sparsit, as a Powler, being also quite out of the pale of any\r\nsuch dissensions.  I only know that these people must be conquered, and\r\nthat it\xe2\x80\x99s high time it was done, once for all.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Bitzer, with a demonstration of great respect for\r\nMrs. Sparsit\xe2\x80\x99s oracular authority.  \xe2\x80\x98You couldn\xe2\x80\x99t put it clearer, I am\r\nsure, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAs this was his usual hour for having a little confidential chat with\r\nMrs. Sparsit, and as he had already caught her eye and seen that she was\r\ngoing to ask him something, he made a pretence of arranging the rulers,\r\ninkstands, and so forth, while that lady went on with her tea, glancing\r\nthrough the open window, down into the street.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Has it been a busy day, Bitzer?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Mrs. Sparsit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Not a very busy day, my lady.  About an average day.\xe2\x80\x99  He now and then\r\nslided into my lady, instead of ma\xe2\x80\x99am, as an involuntary acknowledgment\r\nof Mrs. Sparsit\xe2\x80\x99s personal dignity and claims to reverence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The clerks,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit, carefully brushing an imperceptible\r\ncrumb of bread and butter from her left-hand mitten, \xe2\x80\x98are trustworthy,\r\npunctual, and industrious, of course?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, pretty fair, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.  With the usual exception.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe held the respectable office of general spy and informer in the\r\nestablishment, for which volunteer service he received a present at\r\nChristmas, over and above his weekly wage.  He had grown into an\r\nextremely clear-headed, cautious, prudent young man, who was safe to rise\r\nin the world.  His mind was so exactly regulated, that he had no\r\naffections or passions.  All his proceedings were the result of the\r\nnicest and coldest calculation; and it was not without cause that Mrs.\r\nSparsit habitually observed of him, that he was a young man of the\r\nsteadiest principle she had ever known.  Having satisfied himself, on his\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s death, that his mother had a right of settlement in Coketown,\r\nthis excellent young economist had asserted that right for her with such\r\na steadfast adherence to the principle of the case, that she had been\r\nshut up in the workhouse ever since.  It must be admitted that he allowed\r\nher half a pound of tea a year, which was weak in him: first, because all\r\ngifts have an inevitable tendency to pauperise the recipient, and\r\nsecondly, because his only reasonable transaction in that commodity would\r\nhave been to buy it for as little as he could possibly give, and sell it\r\nfor as much as he could possibly get; it having been clearly ascertained\r\nby philosophers that in this is comprised the whole duty of man\xe2\x80\x94not a\r\npart of man\xe2\x80\x99s duty, but the whole.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Pretty fair, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.  With the usual exception, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 repeated Bitzer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah\xe2\x80\x94h!\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit, shaking her head over her tea-cup, and taking\r\na long gulp.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mr. Thomas, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, I doubt Mr. Thomas very much, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, I don\xe2\x80\x99t like his\r\nways at all.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Bitzer,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit, in a very impressive manner, \xe2\x80\x98do you\r\nrecollect my having said anything to you respecting names?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I beg your pardon, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.  It\xe2\x80\x99s quite true that you did object to names\r\nbeing used, and they\xe2\x80\x99re always best avoided.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Please to remember that I have a charge here,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit, with\r\nher air of state.  \xe2\x80\x98I hold a trust here, Bitzer, under Mr. Bounderby.\r\nHowever improbable both Mr. Bounderby and myself might have deemed it\r\nyears ago, that he would ever become my patron, making me an annual\r\ncompliment, I cannot but regard him in that light.  From Mr. Bounderby I\r\nhave received every acknowledgment of my social station, and every\r\nrecognition of my family descent, that I could possibly expect.  More,\r\nfar more.  Therefore, to my patron I will be scrupulously true.  And I do\r\nnot consider, I will not consider, I cannot consider,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit,\r\nwith a most extensive stock on hand of honour and morality, \xe2\x80\x98that I\r\n_should_ be scrupulously true, if I allowed names to be mentioned under\r\nthis roof, that are unfortunately\xe2\x80\x94most unfortunately\xe2\x80\x94no doubt of\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x94connected with his.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBitzer knuckled his forehead again, and again begged pardon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, Bitzer,\xe2\x80\x99 continued Mrs. Sparsit, \xe2\x80\x98say an individual, and I will hear\r\nyou; say Mr. Thomas, and you must excuse me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98With the usual exception, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bitzer, trying back, \xe2\x80\x98of an\r\nindividual.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah\xe2\x80\x94h!\xe2\x80\x99  Mrs. Sparsit repeated the ejaculation, the shake of the head\r\nover her tea-cup, and the long gulp, as taking up the conversation again\r\nat the point where it had been interrupted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98An individual, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bitzer, \xe2\x80\x98has never been what he ought to\r\nhave been, since he first came into the place.  He is a dissipated,\r\nextravagant idler.  He is not worth his salt, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.  He wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t get it\r\neither, if he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t a friend and relation at court, ma\xe2\x80\x99am!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah\xe2\x80\x94h!\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit, with another melancholy shake of her head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I only hope, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 pursued Bitzer, \xe2\x80\x98that his friend and relation may\r\nnot supply him with the means of carrying on.  Otherwise, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, we know\r\nout of whose pocket _that_ money comes.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah\xe2\x80\x94h!\xe2\x80\x99 sighed Mrs. Sparsit again, with another melancholy shake of her\r\nhead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He is to be pitied, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.  The last party I have alluded to, is to be\r\npitied, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bitzer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, Bitzer,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit.  \xe2\x80\x98I have always pitied the delusion,\r\nalways.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98As to an individual, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bitzer, dropping his voice and drawing\r\nnearer, \xe2\x80\x98he is as improvident as any of the people in this town.  And you\r\nknow what _their_ improvidence is, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.  No one could wish to know it\r\nbetter than a lady of your eminence does.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98They would do well,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Mrs. Sparsit, \xe2\x80\x98to take example by you,\r\nBitzer.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thank you, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.  But, since you do refer to me, now look at me, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.\r\nI have put by a little, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, already.  That gratuity which I receive at\r\nChristmas, ma\xe2\x80\x99am: I never touch it.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t even go the length of my\r\nwages, though they\xe2\x80\x99re not high, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.  Why can\xe2\x80\x99t they do as I have done,\r\nma\xe2\x80\x99am?  What one person can do, another can do.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThis, again, was among the fictions of Coketown.  Any capitalist there,\r\nwho had made sixty thousand pounds out of sixpence, always professed to\r\nwonder why the sixty thousand nearest Hands didn\xe2\x80\x99t each make sixty\r\nthousand pounds out of sixpence, and more or less reproached them every\r\none for not accomplishing the little feat.  What I did you can do.  Why\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t you go and do it?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98As to their wanting recreations, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bitzer, \xe2\x80\x98it\xe2\x80\x99s stuff and\r\nnonsense.  _I_ don\xe2\x80\x99t want recreations.  I never did, and I never shall; I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t like \xe2\x80\x99em.  As to their combining together; there are many of them,\r\nI have no doubt, that by watching and informing upon one another could\r\nearn a trifle now and then, whether in money or good will, and improve\r\ntheir livelihood.  Then, why don\xe2\x80\x99t they improve it, ma\xe2\x80\x99am!  It\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nfirst consideration of a rational creature, and it\xe2\x80\x99s what they pretend to\r\nwant.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Pretend indeed!\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am sure we are constantly hearing, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, till it becomes quite\r\nnauseous, concerning their wives and families,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bitzer.  \xe2\x80\x98Why look\r\nat me, ma\xe2\x80\x99am!  I don\xe2\x80\x99t want a wife and family.  Why should they?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Because they are improvident,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Bitzer, \xe2\x80\x98that\xe2\x80\x99s where it is.  If they were more\r\nprovident and less perverse, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, what would they do?  They would say,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhile my hat covers my family,\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cwhile my bonnet covers my\r\nfamily,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94as the case might be, ma\xe2\x80\x99am\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI have only one to feed, and that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthe person I most like to feed.\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98To be sure,\xe2\x80\x99 assented Mrs. Sparsit, eating muffin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thank you, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bitzer, knuckling his forehead again, in return\r\nfor the favour of Mrs. Sparsit\xe2\x80\x99s improving conversation.  \xe2\x80\x98Would you wish\r\na little more hot water, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, or is there anything else that I could\r\nfetch you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Nothing just now, Bitzer.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thank you, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.  I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t wish to disturb you at your meals, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\r\nparticularly tea, knowing your partiality for it,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bitzer, craning a\r\nlittle to look over into the street from where he stood; \xe2\x80\x98but there\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\ngentleman been looking up here for a minute or so, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, and he has come\r\nacross as if he was going to knock.  That _is_ his knock, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, no\r\ndoubt.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe stepped to the window; and looking out, and drawing in his head again,\r\nconfirmed himself with, \xe2\x80\x98Yes, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.  Would you wish the gentleman to be\r\nshown in, ma\xe2\x80\x99am?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t know who it can be,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit, wiping her mouth and\r\narranging her mittens.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98A stranger, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, evidently.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What a stranger can want at the Bank at this time of the evening, unless\r\nhe comes upon some business for which he is too late, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x99 said\r\nMrs. Sparsit, \xe2\x80\x98but I hold a charge in this establishment from Mr.\r\nBounderby, and I will never shrink from it.  If to see him is any part of\r\nthe duty I have accepted, I will see him.  Use your own discretion,\r\nBitzer.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHere the visitor, all unconscious of Mrs. Sparsit\xe2\x80\x99s magnanimous words,\r\nrepeated his knock so loudly that the light porter hastened down to open\r\nthe door; while Mrs. Sparsit took the precaution of concealing her little\r\ntable, with all its appliances upon it, in a cupboard, and then decamped\r\nup-stairs, that she might appear, if needful, with the greater dignity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If you please, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, the gentleman would wish to see you,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bitzer,\r\nwith his light eye at Mrs. Sparsit\xe2\x80\x99s keyhole.  So, Mrs. Sparsit, who had\r\nimproved the interval by touching up her cap, took her classical features\r\ndown-stairs again, and entered the board-room in the manner of a Roman\r\nmatron going outside the city walls to treat with an invading general.\r\n\r\nThe visitor having strolled to the window, and being then engaged in\r\nlooking carelessly out, was as unmoved by this impressive entry as man\r\ncould possibly be.  He stood whistling to himself with all imaginable\r\ncoolness, with his hat still on, and a certain air of exhaustion upon\r\nhim, in part arising from excessive summer, and in part from excessive\r\ngentility.  For it was to be seen with half an eye that he was a thorough\r\ngentleman, made to the model of the time; weary of everything, and\r\nputting no more faith in anything than Lucifer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I believe, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 quoth Mrs. Sparsit, \xe2\x80\x98you wished to see me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I beg your pardon,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, turning and removing his hat; \xe2\x80\x98pray excuse\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Humph!\xe2\x80\x99 thought Mrs. Sparsit, as she made a stately bend.  \xe2\x80\x98Five and\r\nthirty, good-looking, good figure, good teeth, good voice, good breeding,\r\nwell-dressed, dark hair, bold eyes.\xe2\x80\x99  All which Mrs. Sparsit observed in\r\nher womanly way\xe2\x80\x94like the Sultan who put his head in the pail of\r\nwater\xe2\x80\x94merely in dipping down and coming up again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Please to be seated, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thank you.  Allow me.\xe2\x80\x99  He placed a chair for her, but remained himself\r\ncarelessly lounging against the table.  \xe2\x80\x98I left my servant at the railway\r\nlooking after the luggage\xe2\x80\x94very heavy train and vast quantity of it in the\r\nvan\xe2\x80\x94and strolled on, looking about me.  Exceedingly odd place.  Will you\r\nallow me to ask you if it\xe2\x80\x99s _always_ as black as this?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98In general much blacker,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Mrs. Sparsit, in her uncompromising\r\nway.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Is it possible!  Excuse me: you are not a native, I think?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Mrs. Sparsit.  \xe2\x80\x98It was once my good or ill fortune,\r\nas it may be\xe2\x80\x94before I became a widow\xe2\x80\x94to move in a very different sphere.\r\nMy husband was a Powler.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Beg your pardon, really!\xe2\x80\x99 said the stranger.  \xe2\x80\x98Was\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMrs. Sparsit repeated, \xe2\x80\x98A Powler.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Powler Family,\xe2\x80\x99 said the stranger, after reflecting a few moments.  Mrs.\r\nSparsit signified assent.  The stranger seemed a little more fatigued\r\nthan before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You must be very much bored here?\xe2\x80\x99 was the inference he drew from the\r\ncommunication.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am the servant of circumstances, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit, \xe2\x80\x98and I have\r\nlong adapted myself to the governing power of my life.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Very philosophical,\xe2\x80\x99 returned the stranger, \xe2\x80\x98and very exemplary and\r\nlaudable, and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99  It seemed to be scarcely worth his while to finish the\r\nsentence, so he played with his watch-chain wearily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98May I be permitted to ask, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit, \xe2\x80\x98to what I am\r\nindebted for the favour of\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Assuredly,\xe2\x80\x99 said the stranger.  \xe2\x80\x98Much obliged to you for reminding me.\r\nI am the bearer of a letter of introduction to Mr. Bounderby, the banker.\r\nWalking through this extraordinarily black town, while they were getting\r\ndinner ready at the hotel, I asked a fellow whom I met; one of the\r\nworking people; who appeared to have been taking a shower-bath of\r\nsomething fluffy, which I assume to be the raw material\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMrs. Sparsit inclined her head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x94Raw material\xe2\x80\x94where Mr. Bounderby, the banker, might reside.  Upon\r\nwhich, misled no doubt by the word Banker, he directed me to the Bank.\r\nFact being, I presume, that Mr. Bounderby the Banker does _not_ reside in\r\nthe edifice in which I have the honour of offering this explanation?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Mrs. Sparsit, \xe2\x80\x98he does not.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thank you.  I had no intention of delivering my letter at the present\r\nmoment, nor have I. But strolling on to the Bank to kill time, and having\r\nthe good fortune to observe at the window,\xe2\x80\x99 towards which he languidly\r\nwaved his hand, then slightly bowed, \xe2\x80\x98a lady of a very superior and\r\nagreeable appearance, I considered that I could not do better than take\r\nthe liberty of asking that lady where Mr. Bounderby the Banker _does_\r\nlive.  Which I accordingly venture, with all suitable apologies, to do.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe inattention and indolence of his manner were sufficiently relieved,\r\nto Mrs. Sparsit\xe2\x80\x99s thinking, by a certain gallantry at ease, which offered\r\nher homage too.  Here he was, for instance, at this moment, all but\r\nsitting on the table, and yet lazily bending over her, as if he\r\nacknowledged an attraction in her that made her charming\xe2\x80\x94in her way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Banks, I know, are always suspicious, and officially must be,\xe2\x80\x99 said the\r\nstranger, whose lightness and smoothness of speech were pleasant\r\nlikewise; suggesting matter far more sensible and humorous than it ever\r\ncontained\xe2\x80\x94which was perhaps a shrewd device of the founder of this\r\nnumerous sect, whosoever may have been that great man: \xe2\x80\x98therefore I may\r\nobserve that my letter\xe2\x80\x94here it is\xe2\x80\x94is from the member for this\r\nplace\xe2\x80\x94Gradgrind\xe2\x80\x94whom I have had the pleasure of knowing in London.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMrs. Sparsit recognized the hand, intimated that such confirmation was\r\nquite unnecessary, and gave Mr. Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s address, with all needful\r\nclues and directions in aid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thousand thanks,\xe2\x80\x99 said the stranger.  \xe2\x80\x98Of course you know the Banker\r\nwell?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 rejoined Mrs. Sparsit.  \xe2\x80\x98In my dependent relation towards\r\nhim, I have known him ten years.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Quite an eternity!  I think he married Gradgrind\xe2\x80\x99s daughter?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit, suddenly compressing her mouth, \xe2\x80\x98he had\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x94honour.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The lady is quite a philosopher, I am told?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Indeed, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit.  \xe2\x80\x98_Is_ she?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Excuse my impertinent curiosity,\xe2\x80\x99 pursued the stranger, fluttering over\r\nMrs. Sparsit\xe2\x80\x99s eyebrows, with a propitiatory air, \xe2\x80\x98but you know the\r\nfamily, and know the world.  I am about to know the family, and may have\r\nmuch to do with them.  Is the lady so very alarming?  Her father gives\r\nher such a portentously hard-headed reputation, that I have a burning\r\ndesire to know.  Is she absolutely unapproachable?  Repellently and\r\nstunningly clever?  I see, by your meaning smile, you think not.  You\r\nhave poured balm into my anxious soul.  As to age, now.  Forty?  Five and\r\nthirty?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMrs. Sparsit laughed outright.  \xe2\x80\x98A chit,\xe2\x80\x99 said she.  \xe2\x80\x98Not twenty when she\r\nwas married.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I give you my honour, Mrs. Powler,\xe2\x80\x99 returned the stranger, detaching\r\nhimself from the table, \xe2\x80\x98that I never was so astonished in my life!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIt really did seem to impress him, to the utmost extent of his capacity\r\nof being impressed.  He looked at his informant for full a quarter of a\r\nminute, and appeared to have the surprise in his mind all the time.  \xe2\x80\x98I\r\nassure you, Mrs. Powler,\xe2\x80\x99 he then said, much exhausted, \xe2\x80\x98that the\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s manner prepared me for a grim and stony maturity.  I am obliged\r\nto you, of all things, for correcting so absurd a mistake.  Pray excuse\r\nmy intrusion.  Many thanks.  Good day!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe bowed himself out; and Mrs. Sparsit, hiding in the window curtain, saw\r\nhim languishing down the street on the shady side of the way, observed of\r\nall the town.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What do you think of the gentleman, Bitzer?\xe2\x80\x99 she asked the light porter,\r\nwhen he came to take away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Spends a deal of money on his dress, ma\xe2\x80\x99am.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It must be admitted,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit, \xe2\x80\x98that it\xe2\x80\x99s very tasteful.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Bitzer, \xe2\x80\x98if that\xe2\x80\x99s worth the money.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Besides which, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 resumed Bitzer, while he was polishing the table,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98he looks to me as if he gamed.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s immoral to game,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s ridiculous, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bitzer, \xe2\x80\x98because the chances are against\r\nthe players.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWhether it was that the heat prevented Mrs. Sparsit from working, or\r\nwhether it was that her hand was out, she did no work that night.  She\r\nsat at the window, when the sun began to sink behind the smoke; she sat\r\nthere, when the smoke was burning red, when the colour faded from it,\r\nwhen darkness seemed to rise slowly out of the ground, and creep upward,\r\nupward, up to the house-tops, up the church steeple, up to the summits of\r\nthe factory chimneys, up to the sky.  Without a candle in the room, Mrs.\r\nSparsit sat at the window, with her hands before her, not thinking much\r\nof the sounds of evening; the whooping of boys, the barking of dogs, the\r\nrumbling of wheels, the steps and voices of passengers, the shrill street\r\ncries, the clogs upon the pavement when it was their hour for going by,\r\nthe shutting-up of shop-shutters.  Not until the light porter announced\r\nthat her nocturnal sweetbread was ready, did Mrs. Sparsit arouse herself\r\nfrom her reverie, and convey her dense black eyebrows\xe2\x80\x94by that time\r\ncreased with meditation, as if they needed ironing out-up-stairs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98O, you Fool!\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit, when she was alone at her supper.  Whom\r\nshe meant, she did not say; but she could scarcely have meant the\r\nsweetbread.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II\r\nMR. JAMES HARTHOUSE\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE Gradgrind party wanted assistance in cutting the throats of the\r\nGraces.  They went about recruiting; and where could they enlist recruits\r\nmore hopefully, than among the fine gentlemen who, having found out\r\neverything to be worth nothing, were equally ready for anything?\r\n\r\nMoreover, the healthy spirits who had mounted to this sublime height were\r\nattractive to many of the Gradgrind school.  They liked fine gentlemen;\r\nthey pretended that they did not, but they did.  They became exhausted in\r\nimitation of them; and they yaw-yawed in their speech like them; and they\r\nserved out, with an enervated air, the little mouldy rations of political\r\neconomy, on which they regaled their disciples.  There never before was\r\nseen on earth such a wonderful hybrid race as was thus produced.\r\n\r\nAmong the fine gentlemen not regularly belonging to the Gradgrind school,\r\nthere was one of a good family and a better appearance, with a happy turn\r\nof humour which had told immensely with the House of Commons on the\r\noccasion of his entertaining it with his (and the Board of Directors)\r\nview of a railway accident, in which the most careful officers ever\r\nknown, employed by the most liberal managers ever heard of, assisted by\r\nthe finest mechanical contrivances ever devised, the whole in action on\r\nthe best line ever constructed, had killed five people and wounded\r\nthirty-two, by a casualty without which the excellence of the whole\r\nsystem would have been positively incomplete.  Among the slain was a cow,\r\nand among the scattered articles unowned, a widow\xe2\x80\x99s cap.  And the\r\nhonourable member had so tickled the House (which has a delicate sense of\r\nhumour) by putting the cap on the cow, that it became impatient of any\r\nserious reference to the Coroner\xe2\x80\x99s Inquest, and brought the railway off\r\nwith Cheers and Laughter.\r\n\r\nNow, this gentleman had a younger brother of still better appearance than\r\nhimself, who had tried life as a Cornet of Dragoons, and found it a bore;\r\nand had afterwards tried it in the train of an English minister abroad,\r\nand found it a bore; and had then strolled to Jerusalem, and got bored\r\nthere; and had then gone yachting about the world, and got bored\r\neverywhere.  To whom this honourable and jocular, member fraternally said\r\none day, \xe2\x80\x98Jem, there\xe2\x80\x99s a good opening among the hard Fact fellows, and\r\nthey want men.  I wonder you don\xe2\x80\x99t go in for statistics.\xe2\x80\x99  Jem, rather\r\ntaken by the novelty of the idea, and very hard up for a change, was as\r\nready to \xe2\x80\x98go in\xe2\x80\x99 for statistics as for anything else.  So, he went in.\r\nHe coached himself up with a blue-book or two; and his brother put it\r\nabout among the hard Fact fellows, and said, \xe2\x80\x98If you want to bring in,\r\nfor any place, a handsome dog who can make you a devilish good speech,\r\nlook after my brother Jem, for he\xe2\x80\x99s your man.\xe2\x80\x99  After a few dashes in the\r\npublic meeting way, Mr. Gradgrind and a council of political sages\r\napproved of Jem, and it was resolved to send him down to Coketown, to\r\nbecome known there and in the neighbourhood.  Hence the letter Jem had\r\nlast night shown to Mrs. Sparsit, which Mr. Bounderby now held in his\r\nhand; superscribed, \xe2\x80\x98Josiah Bounderby, Esquire, Banker, Coketown.\r\nSpecially to introduce James Harthouse, Esquire.  Thomas Gradgrind.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWithin an hour of the receipt of this dispatch and Mr. James Harthouse\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncard, Mr. Bounderby put on his hat and went down to the Hotel.  There he\r\nfound Mr. James Harthouse looking out of window, in a state of mind so\r\ndisconsolate, that he was already half-disposed to \xe2\x80\x98go in\xe2\x80\x99 for something\r\nelse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My name, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 said his visitor, \xe2\x80\x98is Josiah Bounderby, of Coketown.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. James Harthouse was very happy indeed (though he scarcely looked so)\r\nto have a pleasure he had long expected.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Coketown, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, obstinately taking a chair, \xe2\x80\x98is not the\r\nkind of place you have been accustomed to.  Therefore, if you will allow\r\nme\xe2\x80\x94or whether you will or not, for I am a plain man\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you\r\nsomething about it before we go any further.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. Harthouse would be charmed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t be too sure of that,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby.  \xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t promise it.  First\r\nof all, you see our smoke.  That\xe2\x80\x99s meat and drink to us.  It\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nhealthiest thing in the world in all respects, and particularly for the\r\nlungs.  If you are one of those who want us to consume it, I differ from\r\nyou.  We are not going to wear the bottoms of our boilers out any faster\r\nthan we wear \xe2\x80\x99em out now, for all the humbugging sentiment in Great\r\nBritain and Ireland.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBy way of \xe2\x80\x98going in\xe2\x80\x99 to the fullest extent, Mr. Harthouse rejoined, \xe2\x80\x98Mr.\r\nBounderby, I assure you I am entirely and completely of your way of\r\nthinking.  On conviction.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am glad to hear it,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby.  \xe2\x80\x98Now, you have heard a lot of\r\ntalk about the work in our mills, no doubt.  You have?  Very good.  I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nstate the fact of it to you.  It\xe2\x80\x99s the pleasantest work there is, and\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s the lightest work there is, and it\xe2\x80\x99s the best-paid work there is.\r\nMore than that, we couldn\xe2\x80\x99t improve the mills themselves, unless we laid\r\ndown Turkey carpets on the floors.  Which we\xe2\x80\x99re not a-going to do.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mr. Bounderby, perfectly right.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Lastly,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98as to our Hands.  There\xe2\x80\x99s not a Hand in this\r\ntown, sir, man, woman, or child, but has one ultimate object in life.\r\nThat object is, to be fed on turtle soup and venison with a gold spoon.\r\nNow, they\xe2\x80\x99re not a-going\xe2\x80\x94none of \xe2\x80\x99em\xe2\x80\x94ever to be fed on turtle soup and\r\nvenison with a gold spoon.  And now you know the place.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. Harthouse professed himself in the highest degree instructed and\r\nrefreshed, by this condensed epitome of the whole Coketown question.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why, you see,\xe2\x80\x99 replied Mr. Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98it suits my disposition to have a\r\nfull understanding with a man, particularly with a public man, when I\r\nmake his acquaintance.  I have only one thing more to say to you, Mr.\r\nHarthouse, before assuring you of the pleasure with which I shall\r\nrespond, to the utmost of my poor ability, to my friend Tom Gradgrind\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nletter of introduction.  You are a man of family.  Don\xe2\x80\x99t you deceive\r\nyourself by supposing for a moment that I am a man of family.  I am a bit\r\nof dirty riff-raff, and a genuine scrap of tag, rag, and bobtail.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIf anything could have exalted Jem\xe2\x80\x99s interest in Mr. Bounderby, it would\r\nhave been this very circumstance.  Or, so he told him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98So now,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98we may shake hands on equal terms.  I say,\r\nequal terms, because although I know what I am, and the exact depth of\r\nthe gutter I have lifted myself out of, better than any man does, I am as\r\nproud as you are.  I am just as proud as you are.  Having now asserted my\r\nindependence in a proper manner, I may come to how do you find yourself,\r\nand I hope you\xe2\x80\x99re pretty well.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe better, Mr. Harthouse gave him to understand as they shook hands, for\r\nthe salubrious air of Coketown.  Mr. Bounderby received the answer with\r\nfavour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Perhaps you know,\xe2\x80\x99 said he, \xe2\x80\x98or perhaps you don\xe2\x80\x99t know, I married Tom\r\nGradgrind\xe2\x80\x99s daughter.  If you have nothing better to do than to walk up\r\ntown with me, I shall be glad to introduce you to Tom Gradgrind\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndaughter.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mr. Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 said Jem, \xe2\x80\x98you anticipate my dearest wishes.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThey went out without further discourse; and Mr. Bounderby piloted the\r\nnew acquaintance who so strongly contrasted with him, to the private red\r\nbrick dwelling, with the black outside shutters, the green inside blinds,\r\nand the black street door up the two white steps.  In the drawing-room of\r\nwhich mansion, there presently entered to them the most remarkable girl\r\nMr. James Harthouse had ever seen.  She was so constrained, and yet so\r\ncareless; so reserved, and yet so watchful; so cold and proud, and yet so\r\nsensitively ashamed of her husband\xe2\x80\x99s braggart humility\xe2\x80\x94from which she\r\nshrunk as if every example of it were a cut or a blow; that it was quite\r\na new sensation to observe her.  In face she was no less remarkable than\r\nin manner.  Her features were handsome; but their natural play was so\r\nlocked up, that it seemed impossible to guess at their genuine\r\nexpression.  Utterly indifferent, perfectly self-reliant, never at a\r\nloss, and yet never at her ease, with her figure in company with them\r\nthere, and her mind apparently quite alone\xe2\x80\x94it was of no use \xe2\x80\x98going in\xe2\x80\x99\r\nyet awhile to comprehend this girl, for she baffled all penetration.\r\n\r\nFrom the mistress of the house, the visitor glanced to the house itself.\r\nThere was no mute sign of a woman in the room.  No graceful little\r\nadornment, no fanciful little device, however trivial, anywhere expressed\r\nher influence.  Cheerless and comfortless, boastfully and doggedly rich,\r\nthere the room stared at its present occupants, unsoftened and unrelieved\r\nby the least trace of any womanly occupation.  As Mr. Bounderby stood in\r\nthe midst of his household gods, so those unrelenting divinities occupied\r\ntheir places around Mr. Bounderby, and they were worthy of one another,\r\nand well matched.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98This, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98is my wife, Mrs. Bounderby: Tom Gradgrind\xe2\x80\x99s\r\neldest daughter.  Loo, Mr. James Harthouse.  Mr. Harthouse has joined\r\nyour father\xe2\x80\x99s muster-roll.  If he is not Tom Gradgrind\xe2\x80\x99s colleague before\r\nlong, I believe we shall at least hear of him in connexion with one of\r\nour neighbouring towns.  You observe, Mr. Harthouse, that my wife is my\r\njunior.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what she saw in me to marry me, but she saw\r\nsomething in me, I suppose, or she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have married me.  She has\r\nlots of expensive knowledge, sir, political and otherwise.  If you want\r\nto cram for anything, I should be troubled to recommend you to a better\r\nadviser than Loo Bounderby.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nTo a more agreeable adviser, or one from whom he would be more likely to\r\nlearn, Mr. Harthouse could never be recommended.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Come!\xe2\x80\x99 said his host.  \xe2\x80\x98If you\xe2\x80\x99re in the complimentary line, you\xe2\x80\x99ll get\r\non here, for you\xe2\x80\x99ll meet with no competition.  I have never been in the\r\nway of learning compliments myself, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t profess to understand the\r\nart of paying \xe2\x80\x99em.  In fact, despise \xe2\x80\x99em.  But, your bringing-up was\r\ndifferent from mine; mine was a real thing, by George!  You\xe2\x80\x99re a\r\ngentleman, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t pretend to be one.  I am Josiah Bounderby of\r\nCoketown, and that\xe2\x80\x99s enough for me.  However, though I am not influenced\r\nby manners and station, Loo Bounderby may be.  She hadn\xe2\x80\x99t my\r\nadvantages\xe2\x80\x94disadvantages you would call \xe2\x80\x99em, but I call \xe2\x80\x99em advantages\xe2\x80\x94so\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll not waste your power, I dare say.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mr. Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 said Jem, turning with a smile to Louisa, \xe2\x80\x98is a noble\r\nanimal in a comparatively natural state, quite free from the harness in\r\nwhich a conventional hack like myself works.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You respect Mr. Bounderby very much,\xe2\x80\x99 she quietly returned.  \xe2\x80\x98It is\r\nnatural that you should.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe was disgracefully thrown out, for a gentleman who had seen so much of\r\nthe world, and thought, \xe2\x80\x98Now, how am I to take this?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are going to devote yourself, as I gather from what Mr. Bounderby\r\nhas said, to the service of your country.  You have made up your mind,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsaid Louisa, still standing before him where she had first stopped\xe2\x80\x94in all\r\nthe singular contrariety of her self-possession, and her being obviously\r\nvery ill at ease\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98to show the nation the way out of all its\r\ndifficulties.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mrs. Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 he returned, laughing, \xe2\x80\x98upon my honour, no.  I will\r\nmake no such pretence to you.  I have seen a little, here and there, up\r\nand down; I have found it all to be very worthless, as everybody has, and\r\nas some confess they have, and some do not; and I am going in for your\r\nrespected father\xe2\x80\x99s opinions\xe2\x80\x94really because I have no choice of opinions,\r\nand may as well back them as anything else.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Have you none of your own?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Louisa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I have not so much as the slightest predilection left.  I assure you I\r\nattach not the least importance to any opinions.  The result of the\r\nvarieties of boredom I have undergone, is a conviction (unless conviction\r\nis too industrious a word for the lazy sentiment I entertain on the\r\nsubject), that any set of ideas will do just as much good as any other\r\nset, and just as much harm as any other set.  There\xe2\x80\x99s an English family\r\nwith a charming Italian motto.  What will be, will be.  It\xe2\x80\x99s the only\r\ntruth going!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThis vicious assumption of honesty in dishonesty\xe2\x80\x94a vice so dangerous, so\r\ndeadly, and so common\xe2\x80\x94seemed, he observed, a little to impress her in his\r\nfavour.  He followed up the advantage, by saying in his pleasantest\r\nmanner: a manner to which she might attach as much or as little meaning\r\nas she pleased: \xe2\x80\x98The side that can prove anything in a line of units,\r\ntens, hundreds, and thousands, Mrs. Bounderby, seems to me to afford the\r\nmost fun, and to give a man the best chance.  I am quite as much attached\r\nto it as if I believed it.  I am quite ready to go in for it, to the same\r\nextent as if I believed it.  And what more could I possibly do, if I did\r\nbelieve it!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are a singular politician,\xe2\x80\x99 said Louisa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Pardon me; I have not even that merit.  We are the largest party in the\r\nstate, I assure you, Mrs. Bounderby, if we all fell out of our adopted\r\nranks and were reviewed together.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. Bounderby, who had been in danger of bursting in silence, interposed\r\nhere with a project for postponing the family dinner till half-past six,\r\nand taking Mr. James Harthouse in the meantime on a round of visits to\r\nthe voting and interesting notabilities of Coketown and its vicinity.\r\nThe round of visits was made; and Mr. James Harthouse, with a discreet\r\nuse of his blue coaching, came off triumphantly, though with a\r\nconsiderable accession of boredom.\r\n\r\nIn the evening, he found the dinner-table laid for four, but they sat\r\ndown only three.  It was an appropriate occasion for Mr. Bounderby to\r\ndiscuss the flavour of the hap\xe2\x80\x99orth of stewed eels he had purchased in\r\nthe streets at eight years old; and also of the inferior water, specially\r\nused for laying the dust, with which he had washed down that repast.  He\r\nlikewise entertained his guest over the soup and fish, with the\r\ncalculation that he (Bounderby) had eaten in his youth at least three\r\nhorses under the guise of polonies and saveloys.  These recitals, Jem, in\r\na languid manner, received with \xe2\x80\x98charming!\xe2\x80\x99 every now and then; and they\r\nprobably would have decided him to \xe2\x80\x98go in\xe2\x80\x99 for Jerusalem again to-morrow\r\nmorning, had he been less curious respecting Louisa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Is there nothing,\xe2\x80\x99 he thought, glancing at her as she sat at the head of\r\nthe table, where her youthful figure, small and slight, but very\r\ngraceful, looked as pretty as it looked misplaced; \xe2\x80\x98is there nothing that\r\nwill move that face?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nYes!  By Jupiter, there was something, and here it was, in an unexpected\r\nshape.  Tom appeared.  She changed as the door opened, and broke into a\r\nbeaming smile.\r\n\r\nA beautiful smile.  Mr. James Harthouse might not have thought so much of\r\nit, but that he had wondered so long at her impassive face.  She put out\r\nher hand\xe2\x80\x94a pretty little soft hand; and her fingers closed upon her\r\nbrother\xe2\x80\x99s, as if she would have carried them to her lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ay, ay?\xe2\x80\x99 thought the visitor.  \xe2\x80\x98This whelp is the only creature she\r\ncares for.  So, so!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe whelp was presented, and took his chair.  The appellation was not\r\nflattering, but not unmerited.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98When I was your age, young Tom,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98I was punctual, or I\r\ngot no dinner!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98When you were my age,\xe2\x80\x99 resumed Tom, \xe2\x80\x98you hadn\xe2\x80\x99t a wrong balance to get\r\nright, and hadn\xe2\x80\x99t to dress afterwards.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Never mind that now,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, then,\xe2\x80\x99 grumbled Tom.  \xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t begin with me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mrs. Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 said Harthouse, perfectly hearing this under-strain as\r\nit went on; \xe2\x80\x98your brother\xe2\x80\x99s face is quite familiar to me.  Can I have\r\nseen him abroad?  Or at some public school, perhaps?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No,\xe2\x80\x99 she resumed, quite interested, \xe2\x80\x98he has never been abroad yet, and\r\nwas educated here, at home.  Tom, love, I am telling Mr. Harthouse that\r\nhe never saw you abroad.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No such luck, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom.\r\n\r\nThere was little enough in him to brighten her face, for he was a sullen\r\nyoung fellow, and ungracious in his manner even to her.  So much the\r\ngreater must have been the solitude of her heart, and her need of some\r\none on whom to bestow it.  \xe2\x80\x98So much the more is this whelp the only\r\ncreature she has ever cared for,\xe2\x80\x99 thought Mr. James Harthouse, turning it\r\nover and over.  \xe2\x80\x98So much the more.  So much the more.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBoth in his sister\xe2\x80\x99s presence, and after she had left the room, the whelp\r\ntook no pains to hide his contempt for Mr. Bounderby, whenever he could\r\nindulge it without the observation of that independent man, by making wry\r\nfaces, or shutting one eye.  Without responding to these telegraphic\r\ncommunications, Mr. Harthouse encouraged him much in the course of the\r\nevening, and showed an unusual liking for him.  At last, when he rose to\r\nreturn to his hotel, and was a little doubtful whether he knew the way by\r\nnight, the whelp immediately proffered his services as guide, and turned\r\nout with him to escort him thither.\r\n\r\n            [Picture: Mr. Harthouse dines at the Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s]\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III\r\nTHE WHELP\r\n\r\n\r\nIT was very remarkable that a young gentleman who had been brought up\r\nunder one continuous system of unnatural restraint, should be a\r\nhypocrite; but it was certainly the case with Tom.  It was very strange\r\nthat a young gentleman who had never been left to his own guidance for\r\nfive consecutive minutes, should be incapable at last of governing\r\nhimself; but so it was with Tom.  It was altogether unaccountable that a\r\nyoung gentleman whose imagination had been strangled in his cradle,\r\nshould be still inconvenienced by its ghost in the form of grovelling\r\nsensualities; but such a monster, beyond all doubt, was Tom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Do you smoke?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Mr. James Harthouse, when they came to the hotel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I believe you!\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom.\r\n\r\nHe could do no less than ask Tom up; and Tom could do no less than go up.\r\nWhat with a cooling drink adapted to the weather, but not so weak as\r\ncool; and what with a rarer tobacco than was to be bought in those parts;\r\nTom was soon in a highly free and easy state at his end of the sofa, and\r\nmore than ever disposed to admire his new friend at the other end.\r\n\r\nTom blew his smoke aside, after he had been smoking a little while, and\r\ntook an observation of his friend.  \xe2\x80\x98He don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to care about his\r\ndress,\xe2\x80\x99 thought Tom, \xe2\x80\x98and yet how capitally he does it.  What an easy\r\nswell he is!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. James Harthouse, happening to catch Tom\xe2\x80\x99s eye, remarked that he drank\r\nnothing, and filled his glass with his own negligent hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thank\xe2\x80\x99ee,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom.  \xe2\x80\x98Thank\xe2\x80\x99ee.  Well, Mr. Harthouse, I hope you have\r\nhad about a dose of old Bounderby to-night.\xe2\x80\x99  Tom said this with one eye\r\nshut up again, and looking over his glass knowingly, at his entertainer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98A very good fellow indeed!\xe2\x80\x99 returned Mr. James Harthouse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You think so, don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom.  And shut up his eye again.\r\n\r\nMr. James Harthouse smiled; and rising from his end of the sofa, and\r\nlounging with his back against the chimney-piece, so that he stood before\r\nthe empty fire-grate as he smoked, in front of Tom and looking down at\r\nhim, observed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What a comical brother-in-law you are!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What a comical brother-in-law old Bounderby is, I think you mean,\xe2\x80\x99 said\r\nTom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are a piece of caustic, Tom,\xe2\x80\x99 retorted Mr. James Harthouse.\r\n\r\nThere was something so very agreeable in being so intimate with such a\r\nwaistcoat; in being called Tom, in such an intimate way, by such a voice;\r\nin being on such off-hand terms so soon, with such a pair of whiskers;\r\nthat Tom was uncommonly pleased with himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh!  I don\xe2\x80\x99t care for old Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 said he, \xe2\x80\x98if you mean that.  I\r\nhave always called old Bounderby by the same name when I have talked\r\nabout him, and I have always thought of him in the same way.  I am not\r\ngoing to begin to be polite now, about old Bounderby.  It would be rather\r\nlate in the day.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t mind me,\xe2\x80\x99 returned James; \xe2\x80\x98but take care when his wife is by, you\r\nknow.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98His wife?\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom.  \xe2\x80\x98My sister Loo?  O yes!\xe2\x80\x99  And he laughed, and took\r\na little more of the cooling drink.\r\n\r\nJames Harthouse continued to lounge in the same place and attitude,\r\nsmoking his cigar in his own easy way, and looking pleasantly at the\r\nwhelp, as if he knew himself to be a kind of agreeable demon who had only\r\nto hover over him, and he must give up his whole soul if required.  It\r\ncertainly did seem that the whelp yielded to this influence.  He looked\r\nat his companion sneakingly, he looked at him admiringly, he looked at\r\nhim boldly, and put up one leg on the sofa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My sister Loo?\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom.  \xe2\x80\x98_She_ never cared for old Bounderby.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That\xe2\x80\x99s the past tense, Tom,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Mr. James Harthouse, striking the\r\nash from his cigar with his little finger.  \xe2\x80\x98We are in the present tense,\r\nnow.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Verb neuter, not to care.  Indicative mood, present tense.  First person\r\nsingular, I do not care; second person singular, thou dost not care;\r\nthird person singular, she does not care,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Tom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Good!  Very quaint!\xe2\x80\x99 said his friend.  \xe2\x80\x98Though you don\xe2\x80\x99t mean it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But I _do_ mean it,\xe2\x80\x99 cried Tom.  \xe2\x80\x98Upon my honour!  Why, you won\xe2\x80\x99t tell\r\nme, Mr. Harthouse, that you really suppose my sister Loo does care for\r\nold Bounderby.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My dear fellow,\xe2\x80\x99 returned the other, \xe2\x80\x98what am I bound to suppose, when I\r\nfind two married people living in harmony and happiness?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nTom had by this time got both his legs on the sofa.  If his second leg\r\nhad not been already there when he was called a dear fellow, he would\r\nhave put it up at that great stage of the conversation.  Feeling it\r\nnecessary to do something then, he stretched himself out at greater\r\nlength, and, reclining with the back of his head on the end of the sofa,\r\nand smoking with an infinite assumption of negligence, turned his common\r\nface, and not too sober eyes, towards the face looking down upon him so\r\ncarelessly yet so potently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You know our governor, Mr. Harthouse,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom, \xe2\x80\x98and therefore, you\r\nneedn\xe2\x80\x99t be surprised that Loo married old Bounderby.  She never had a\r\nlover, and the governor proposed old Bounderby, and she took him.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Very dutiful in your interesting sister,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. James Harthouse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, but she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have been as dutiful, and it would not have come\r\noff as easily,\xe2\x80\x99 returned the whelp, \xe2\x80\x98if it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been for me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe tempter merely lifted his eyebrows; but the whelp was obliged to go\r\non.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98_I_ persuaded her,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, with an edifying air of superiority.  \xe2\x80\x98I\r\nwas stuck into old Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s bank (where I never wanted to be), and I\r\nknew I should get into scrapes there, if she put old Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s pipe\r\nout; so I told her my wishes, and she came into them.  She would do\r\nanything for me.  It was very game of her, wasn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It was charming, Tom!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Not that it was altogether so important to her as it was to me,\xe2\x80\x99\r\ncontinued Tom coolly, \xe2\x80\x98because my liberty and comfort, and perhaps my\r\ngetting on, depended on it; and she had no other lover, and staying at\r\nhome was like staying in jail\xe2\x80\x94especially when I was gone.  It wasn\xe2\x80\x99t as\r\nif she gave up another lover for old Bounderby; but still it was a good\r\nthing in her.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Perfectly delightful.  And she gets on so placidly.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Tom, with contemptuous patronage, \xe2\x80\x98she\xe2\x80\x99s a regular girl.\r\nA girl can get on anywhere.  She has settled down to the life, and _she_\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t mind.  It does just as well as another.  Besides, though Loo is a\r\ngirl, she\xe2\x80\x99s not a common sort of girl.  She can shut herself up within\r\nherself, and think\xe2\x80\x94as I have often known her sit and watch the fire\xe2\x80\x94for\r\nan hour at a stretch.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ay, ay?  Has resources of her own,\xe2\x80\x99 said Harthouse, smoking quietly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Not so much of that as you may suppose,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Tom; \xe2\x80\x98for our governor\r\nhad her crammed with all sorts of dry bones and sawdust.  It\xe2\x80\x99s his\r\nsystem.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Formed his daughter on his own model?\xe2\x80\x99 suggested Harthouse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98His daughter?  Ah! and everybody else.  Why, he formed Me that way!\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsaid Tom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Impossible!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He did, though,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom, shaking his head.  \xe2\x80\x98I mean to say, Mr.\r\nHarthouse, that when I first left home and went to old Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s, I was\r\nas flat as a warming-pan, and knew no more about life, than any oyster\r\ndoes.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Come, Tom!  I can hardly believe that.  A joke\xe2\x80\x99s a joke.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Upon my soul!\xe2\x80\x99 said the whelp.  \xe2\x80\x98I am serious; I am indeed!\xe2\x80\x99  He smoked\r\nwith great gravity and dignity for a little while, and then added, in a\r\nhighly complacent tone, \xe2\x80\x98Oh!  I have picked up a little since.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndeny that.  But I have done it myself; no thanks to the governor.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And your intelligent sister?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My intelligent sister is about where she was.  She used to complain to\r\nme that she had nothing to fall back upon, that girls usually fall back\r\nupon; and I don\xe2\x80\x99t see how she is to have got over that since.  But _she_\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t mind,\xe2\x80\x99 he sagaciously added, puffing at his cigar again.  \xe2\x80\x98Girls\r\ncan always get on, somehow.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Calling at the Bank yesterday evening, for Mr. Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s address, I\r\nfound an ancient lady there, who seems to entertain great admiration for\r\nyour sister,\xe2\x80\x99 observed Mr. James Harthouse, throwing away the last small\r\nremnant of the cigar he had now smoked out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mother Sparsit!\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom.  \xe2\x80\x98What! you have seen her already, have you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHis friend nodded.  Tom took his cigar out of his mouth, to shut up his\r\neye (which had grown rather unmanageable) with the greater expression,\r\nand to tap his nose several times with his finger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mother Sparsit\xe2\x80\x99s feeling for Loo is more than admiration, I should\r\nthink,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom.  \xe2\x80\x98Say affection and devotion.  Mother Sparsit never set\r\nher cap at Bounderby when he was a bachelor.  Oh no!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThese were the last words spoken by the whelp, before a giddy drowsiness\r\ncame upon him, followed by complete oblivion.  He was roused from the\r\nlatter state by an uneasy dream of being stirred up with a boot, and also\r\nof a voice saying: \xe2\x80\x98Come, it\xe2\x80\x99s late.  Be off!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well!\xe2\x80\x99 he said, scrambling from the sofa.  \xe2\x80\x98I must take my leave of you\r\nthough.  I say.  Yours is very good tobacco.  But it\xe2\x80\x99s too mild.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, it\xe2\x80\x99s too mild,\xe2\x80\x99 returned his entertainer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s ridiculously mild,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom.  \xe2\x80\x98Where\xe2\x80\x99s the door!  Good\r\nnight!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe had another odd dream of being taken by a waiter through a mist,\r\nwhich, after giving him some trouble and difficulty, resolved itself into\r\nthe main street, in which he stood alone.  He then walked home pretty\r\neasily, though not yet free from an impression of the presence and\r\ninfluence of his new friend\xe2\x80\x94as if he were lounging somewhere in the air,\r\nin the same negligent attitude, regarding him with the same look.\r\n\r\nThe whelp went home, and went to bed.  If he had had any sense of what he\r\nhad done that night, and had been less of a whelp and more of a brother,\r\nhe might have turned short on the road, might have gone down to the\r\nill-smelling river that was dyed black, might have gone to bed in it for\r\ngood and all, and have curtained his head for ever with its filthy\r\nwaters.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV\r\nMEN AND BROTHERS\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98OH, my friends, the down-trodden operatives of Coketown!  Oh, my friends\r\nand fellow-countrymen, the slaves of an iron-handed and a grinding\r\ndespotism!  Oh, my friends and fellow-sufferers, and fellow-workmen, and\r\nfellow-men!  I tell you that the hour is come, when we must rally round\r\none another as One united power, and crumble into dust the oppressors\r\nthat too long have battened upon the plunder of our families, upon the\r\nsweat of our brows, upon the labour of our hands, upon the strength of\r\nour sinews, upon the God-created glorious rights of Humanity, and upon\r\nthe holy and eternal privileges of Brotherhood!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Good!\xe2\x80\x99  \xe2\x80\x98Hear, hear, hear!\xe2\x80\x99  \xe2\x80\x98Hurrah!\xe2\x80\x99 and other cries, arose in many\r\nvoices from various parts of the densely crowded and suffocatingly close\r\nHall, in which the orator, perched on a stage, delivered himself of this\r\nand what other froth and fume he had in him.  He had declaimed himself\r\ninto a violent heat, and was as hoarse as he was hot.  By dint of roaring\r\nat the top of his voice under a flaring gaslight, clenching his fists,\r\nknitting his brows, setting his teeth, and pounding with his arms, he had\r\ntaken so much out of himself by this time, that he was brought to a stop,\r\nand called for a glass of water.\r\n\r\nAs he stood there, trying to quench his fiery face with his drink of\r\nwater, the comparison between the orator and the crowd of attentive faces\r\nturned towards him, was extremely to his disadvantage.  Judging him by\r\nNature\xe2\x80\x99s evidence, he was above the mass in very little but the stage on\r\nwhich he stood.  In many great respects he was essentially below them.\r\nHe was not so honest, he was not so manly, he was not so good-humoured;\r\nhe substituted cunning for their simplicity, and passion for their safe\r\nsolid sense.  An ill-made, high-shouldered man, with lowering brows, and\r\nhis features crushed into an habitually sour expression, he contrasted\r\nmost unfavourably, even in his mongrel dress, with the great body of his\r\nhearers in their plain working clothes.  Strange as it always is to\r\nconsider any assembly in the act of submissively resigning itself to the\r\ndreariness of some complacent person, lord or commoner, whom\r\nthree-fourths of it could, by no human means, raise out of the slough of\r\ninanity to their own intellectual level, it was particularly strange, and\r\nit was even particularly affecting, to see this crowd of earnest faces,\r\nwhose honesty in the main no competent observer free from bias could\r\ndoubt, so agitated by such a leader.\r\n\r\nGood!  Hear, hear!  Hurrah!  The eagerness both of attention and\r\nintention, exhibited in all the countenances, made them a most impressive\r\nsight.  There was no carelessness, no languor, no idle curiosity; none of\r\nthe many shades of indifference to be seen in all other assemblies,\r\nvisible for one moment there.  That every man felt his condition to be,\r\nsomehow or other, worse than it might be; that every man considered it\r\nincumbent on him to join the rest, towards the making of it better; that\r\nevery man felt his only hope to be in his allying himself to the comrades\r\nby whom he was surrounded; and that in this belief, right or wrong\r\n(unhappily wrong then), the whole of that crowd were gravely, deeply,\r\nfaithfully in earnest; must have been as plain to any one who chose to\r\nsee what was there, as the bare beams of the roof and the whitened brick\r\nwalls.  Nor could any such spectator fail to know in his own breast, that\r\nthese men, through their very delusions, showed great qualities,\r\nsusceptible of being turned to the happiest and best account; and that to\r\npretend (on the strength of sweeping axioms, howsoever cut and dried)\r\nthat they went astray wholly without cause, and of their own irrational\r\nwills, was to pretend that there could be smoke without fire, death\r\nwithout birth, harvest without seed, anything or everything produced from\r\nnothing.\r\n\r\nThe orator having refreshed himself, wiped his corrugated forehead from\r\nleft to right several times with his handkerchief folded into a pad, and\r\nconcentrated all his revived forces, in a sneer of great disdain and\r\nbitterness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But oh, my friends and brothers!  Oh, men and Englishmen, the\r\ndown-trodden operatives of Coketown!  What shall we say of that man\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nworking-man, that I should find it necessary so to libel the glorious\r\nname\xe2\x80\x94who, being practically and well acquainted with the grievances and\r\nwrongs of you, the injured pith and marrow of this land, and having heard\r\nyou, with a noble and majestic unanimity that will make Tyrants tremble,\r\nresolve for to subscribe to the funds of the United Aggregate Tribunal,\r\nand to abide by the injunctions issued by that body for your benefit,\r\nwhatever they may be\xe2\x80\x94what, I ask you, will you say of that working-man,\r\nsince such I must acknowledge him to be, who, at such a time, deserts his\r\npost, and sells his flag; who, at such a time, turns a traitor and a\r\ncraven and a recreant, who, at such a time, is not ashamed to make to you\r\nthe dastardly and humiliating avowal that he will hold himself aloof, and\r\nwill _not_ be one of those associated in the gallant stand for Freedom\r\nand for Right?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe assembly was divided at this point.  There were some groans and\r\nhisses, but the general sense of honour was much too strong for the\r\ncondemnation of a man unheard.  \xe2\x80\x98Be sure you\xe2\x80\x99re right, Slackbridge!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Put him up!\xe2\x80\x99  \xe2\x80\x98Let\xe2\x80\x99s hear him!\xe2\x80\x99  Such things were said on many sides.\r\nFinally, one strong voice called out, \xe2\x80\x98Is the man heer?  If the man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nheer, Slackbridge, let\xe2\x80\x99s hear the man himseln, \xe2\x80\x99stead o\xe2\x80\x99 yo.\xe2\x80\x99  Which was\r\nreceived with a round of applause.\r\n\r\nSlackbridge, the orator, looked about him with a withering smile; and,\r\nholding out his right hand at arm\xe2\x80\x99s length (as the manner of all\r\nSlackbridges is), to still the thundering sea, waited until there was a\r\nprofound silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh, my friends and fellow-men!\xe2\x80\x99 said Slackbridge then, shaking his head\r\nwith violent scorn, \xe2\x80\x98I do not wonder that you, the prostrate sons of\r\nlabour, are incredulous of the existence of such a man.  But he who sold\r\nhis birthright for a mess of pottage existed, and Judas Iscariot existed,\r\nand Castlereagh existed, and this man exists!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHere, a brief press and confusion near the stage, ended in the man\r\nhimself standing at the orator\xe2\x80\x99s side before the concourse.  He was pale\r\nand a little moved in the face\xe2\x80\x94his lips especially showed it; but he\r\nstood quiet, with his left hand at his chin, waiting to be heard.  There\r\nwas a chairman to regulate the proceedings, and this functionary now took\r\nthe case into his own hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My friends,\xe2\x80\x99 said he, \xe2\x80\x98by virtue o\xe2\x80\x99 my office as your president, I askes\r\no\xe2\x80\x99 our friend Slackbridge, who may be a little over hetter in this\r\nbusiness, to take his seat, whiles this man Stephen Blackpool is heern.\r\nYou all know this man Stephen Blackpool.  You know him awlung o\xe2\x80\x99 his\r\nmisfort\xe2\x80\x99ns, and his good name.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWith that, the chairman shook him frankly by the hand, and sat down\r\nagain.  Slackbridge likewise sat down, wiping his hot forehead\xe2\x80\x94always\r\nfrom left to right, and never the reverse way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My friends,\xe2\x80\x99 Stephen began, in the midst of a dead calm; \xe2\x80\x98I ha\xe2\x80\x99 hed\r\nwhat\xe2\x80\x99s been spok\xe2\x80\x99n o\xe2\x80\x99 me, and \xe2\x80\x99tis lickly that I shan\xe2\x80\x99t mend it.  But I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nliefer you\xe2\x80\x99d hearn the truth concernin myseln, fro my lips than fro onny\r\nother man\xe2\x80\x99s, though I never cud\xe2\x80\x99n speak afore so monny, wi\xe2\x80\x99out bein\r\nmoydert and muddled.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSlackbridge shook his head as if he would shake it off, in his\r\nbitterness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m th\xe2\x80\x99 one single Hand in Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s mill, o\xe2\x80\x99 a\xe2\x80\x99 the men theer, as\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t coom in wi\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 proposed reg\xe2\x80\x99lations.  I canna coom in wi\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99em.  My\r\nfriends, I doubt their doin\xe2\x80\x99 yo onny good.  Licker they\xe2\x80\x99ll do yo hurt.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSlackbridge laughed, folded his arms, and frowned sarcastically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But \xe2\x80\x99t an\xe2\x80\x99t sommuch for that as I stands out.  If that were aw, I\xe2\x80\x99d coom\r\nin wi\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 rest.  But I ha\xe2\x80\x99 my reasons\xe2\x80\x94mine, yo see\xe2\x80\x94for being hindered;\r\nnot on\xe2\x80\x99y now, but awlus\xe2\x80\x94awlus\xe2\x80\x94life long!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSlackbridge jumped up and stood beside him, gnashing and tearing.  \xe2\x80\x98Oh,\r\nmy friends, what but this did I tell you?  Oh, my fellow-countrymen, what\r\nwarning but this did I give you?  And how shows this recreant conduct in\r\na man on whom unequal laws are known to have fallen heavy?  Oh, you\r\nEnglishmen, I ask you how does this subornation show in one of\r\nyourselves, who is thus consenting to his own undoing and to yours, and\r\nto your children\xe2\x80\x99s and your children\xe2\x80\x99s children\xe2\x80\x99s?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThere was some applause, and some crying of Shame upon the man; but the\r\ngreater part of the audience were quiet.  They looked at Stephen\xe2\x80\x99s worn\r\nface, rendered more pathetic by the homely emotions it evinced; and, in\r\nthe kindness of their nature, they were more sorry than indignant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x99Tis this Delegate\xe2\x80\x99s trade for t\xe2\x80\x99 speak,\xe2\x80\x99 said Stephen, \xe2\x80\x98an\xe2\x80\x99 he\xe2\x80\x99s paid\r\nfor \xe2\x80\x99t, an\xe2\x80\x99 he knows his work.  Let him keep to \xe2\x80\x99t.  Let him give no heed\r\nto what I ha had\xe2\x80\x99n to bear.  That\xe2\x80\x99s not for him.  That\xe2\x80\x99s not for nobbody\r\nbut me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThere was a propriety, not to say a dignity in these words, that made the\r\nhearers yet more quiet and attentive.  The same strong voice called out,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Slackbridge, let the man be heern, and howd thee tongue!\xe2\x80\x99  Then the\r\nplace was wonderfully still.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My brothers,\xe2\x80\x99 said Stephen, whose low voice was distinctly heard, \xe2\x80\x98and\r\nmy fellow-workmen\xe2\x80\x94for that yo are to me, though not, as I knows on, to\r\nthis delegate here\xe2\x80\x94I ha but a word to sen, and I could sen nommore if I\r\nwas to speak till Strike o\xe2\x80\x99 day.  I know weel, aw what\xe2\x80\x99s afore me.  I\r\nknow weel that yo aw resolve to ha nommore ado wi\xe2\x80\x99 a man who is not wi\xe2\x80\x99\r\nyo in this matther.  I know weel that if I was a lyin parisht i\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99\r\nroad, yo\xe2\x80\x99d feel it right to pass me by, as a forrenner and stranger.\r\nWhat I ha getn, I mun mak th\xe2\x80\x99 best on.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Stephen Blackpool,\xe2\x80\x99 said the chairman, rising, \xe2\x80\x98think on \xe2\x80\x99t agen.  Think\r\non \xe2\x80\x99t once agen, lad, afore thou\xe2\x80\x99rt shunned by aw owd friends.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThere was an universal murmur to the same effect, though no man\r\narticulated a word.  Every eye was fixed on Stephen\xe2\x80\x99s face.  To repent of\r\nhis determination, would be to take a load from all their minds.  He\r\nlooked around him, and knew that it was so.  Not a grain of anger with\r\nthem was in his heart; he knew them, far below their surface weaknesses\r\nand misconceptions, as no one but their fellow-labourer could.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I ha thowt on \xe2\x80\x99t, above a bit, sir.  I simply canna coom in.  I mun go\r\nth\xe2\x80\x99 way as lays afore me.  I mun tak my leave o\xe2\x80\x99 aw heer.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe made a sort of reverence to them by holding up his arms, and stood for\r\nthe moment in that attitude; not speaking until they slowly dropped at\r\nhis sides.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Monny\xe2\x80\x99s the pleasant word as soom heer has spok\xe2\x80\x99n wi\xe2\x80\x99 me; monny\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nface I see heer, as I first seen when I were yoong and lighter heart\xe2\x80\x99n\r\nthan now.  I ha\xe2\x80\x99 never had no fratch afore, sin ever I were born, wi\xe2\x80\x99 any\r\no\xe2\x80\x99 my like; Gonnows I ha\xe2\x80\x99 none now that\xe2\x80\x99s o\xe2\x80\x99 my makin\xe2\x80\x99.  Yo\xe2\x80\x99ll ca\xe2\x80\x99 me\r\ntraitor and that\xe2\x80\x94yo I mean t\xe2\x80\x99 say,\xe2\x80\x99 addressing Slackbridge, \xe2\x80\x98but \xe2\x80\x99tis\r\neasier to ca\xe2\x80\x99 than mak\xe2\x80\x99 out.  So let be.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe had moved away a pace or two to come down from the platform, when he\r\nremembered something he had not said, and returned again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Haply,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, turning his furrowed face slowly about, that he might\r\nas it were individually address the whole audience, those both near and\r\ndistant; \xe2\x80\x98haply, when this question has been tak\xe2\x80\x99n up and discoosed,\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99ll be a threat to turn out if I\xe2\x80\x99m let to work among yo.  I hope I\r\nshall die ere ever such a time cooms, and I shall work solitary among yo\r\nunless it cooms\xe2\x80\x94truly, I mun do \xe2\x80\x99t, my friends; not to brave yo, but to\r\nlive.  I ha nobbut work to live by; and wheerever can I go, I who ha\r\nworked sin I were no heighth at aw, in Coketown heer?  I mak\xe2\x80\x99 no\r\ncomplaints o\xe2\x80\x99 bein turned to the wa\xe2\x80\x99, o\xe2\x80\x99 bein outcasten and overlooken\r\nfro this time forrard, but hope I shall be let to work.  If there is any\r\nright for me at aw, my friends, I think \xe2\x80\x99tis that.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nNot a word was spoken.  Not a sound was audible in the building, but the\r\nslight rustle of men moving a little apart, all along the centre of the\r\nroom, to open a means of passing out, to the man with whom they had all\r\nbound themselves to renounce companionship.  Looking at no one, and going\r\nhis way with a lowly steadiness upon him that asserted nothing and sought\r\nnothing, Old Stephen, with all his troubles on his head, left the scene.\r\n\r\nThen Slackbridge, who had kept his oratorical arm extended during the\r\ngoing out, as if he were repressing with infinite solicitude and by a\r\nwonderful moral power the vehement passions of the multitude, applied\r\nhimself to raising their spirits.  Had not the Roman Brutus, oh, my\r\nBritish countrymen, condemned his son to death; and had not the Spartan\r\nmothers, oh my soon to be victorious friends, driven their flying\r\nchildren on the points of their enemies\xe2\x80\x99 swords?  Then was it not the\r\nsacred duty of the men of Coketown, with forefathers before them, an\r\nadmiring world in company with them, and a posterity to come after them,\r\nto hurl out traitors from the tents they had pitched in a sacred and a\r\nGod-like cause?  The winds of heaven answered Yes; and bore Yes, east,\r\nwest, north, and south.  And consequently three cheers for the United\r\nAggregate Tribunal!\r\n\r\nSlackbridge acted as fugleman, and gave the time.  The multitude of\r\ndoubtful faces (a little conscience-stricken) brightened at the sound,\r\nand took it up.  Private feeling must yield to the common cause.  Hurrah!\r\nThe roof yet vibrated with the cheering, when the assembly dispersed.\r\n\r\nThus easily did Stephen Blackpool fall into the loneliest of lives, the\r\nlife of solitude among a familiar crowd.  The stranger in the land who\r\nlooks into ten thousand faces for some answering look and never finds it,\r\nis in cheering society as compared with him who passes ten averted faces\r\ndaily, that were once the countenances of friends.  Such experience was\r\nto be Stephen\xe2\x80\x99s now, in every waking moment of his life; at his work, on\r\nhis way to it and from it, at his door, at his window, everywhere.  By\r\ngeneral consent, they even avoided that side of the street on which he\r\nhabitually walked; and left it, of all the working men, to him only.\r\n\r\nHe had been for many years, a quiet silent man, associating but little\r\nwith other men, and used to companionship with his own thoughts.  He had\r\nnever known before the strength of the want in his heart for the frequent\r\nrecognition of a nod, a look, a word; or the immense amount of relief\r\nthat had been poured into it by drops through such small means.  It was\r\neven harder than he could have believed possible, to separate in his own\r\nconscience his abandonment by all his fellows from a baseless sense of\r\nshame and disgrace.\r\n\r\nThe first four days of his endurance were days so long and heavy, that he\r\nbegan to be appalled by the prospect before him.  Not only did he see no\r\nRachael all the time, but he avoided every chance of seeing her; for,\r\nalthough he knew that the prohibition did not yet formally extend to the\r\nwomen working in the factories, he found that some of them with whom he\r\nwas acquainted were changed to him, and he feared to try others, and\r\ndreaded that Rachael might be even singled out from the rest if she were\r\nseen in his company.  So, he had been quite alone during the four days,\r\nand had spoken to no one, when, as he was leaving his work at night, a\r\nyoung man of a very light complexion accosted him in the street.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Your name\xe2\x80\x99s Blackpool, ain\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x99 said the young man.\r\n\r\nStephen coloured to find himself with his hat in his hand, in his\r\ngratitude for being spoken to, or in the suddenness of it, or both.  He\r\nmade a feint of adjusting the lining, and said, \xe2\x80\x98Yes.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are the Hand they have sent to Coventry, I mean?\xe2\x80\x99 said Bitzer, the\r\nvery light young man in question.\r\n\r\nStephen answered \xe2\x80\x98Yes,\xe2\x80\x99 again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I supposed so, from their all appearing to keep away from you.  Mr.\r\nBounderby wants to speak to you.  You know his house, don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nStephen said \xe2\x80\x98Yes,\xe2\x80\x99 again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then go straight up there, will you?\xe2\x80\x99 said Bitzer.  \xe2\x80\x98You\xe2\x80\x99re expected,\r\nand have only to tell the servant it\xe2\x80\x99s you.  I belong to the Bank; so, if\r\nyou go straight up without me (I was sent to fetch you), you\xe2\x80\x99ll save me a\r\nwalk.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nStephen, whose way had been in the contrary direction, turned about, and\r\nbetook himself as in duty bound, to the red brick castle of the giant\r\nBounderby.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V\r\nMEN AND MASTERS\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98WELL, Stephen,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, in his windy manner, \xe2\x80\x98what\xe2\x80\x99s this I\r\nhear?  What have these pests of the earth been doing to _you_?  Come in,\r\nand speak up.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIt was into the drawing-room that he was thus bidden.  A tea-table was\r\nset out; and Mr. Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s young wife, and her brother, and a great\r\ngentleman from London, were present.  To whom Stephen made his obeisance,\r\nclosing the door and standing near it, with his hat in his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98This is the man I was telling you about, Harthouse,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Bounderby.\r\nThe gentleman he addressed, who was talking to Mrs. Bounderby on the\r\nsofa, got up, saying in an indolent way, \xe2\x80\x98Oh really?\xe2\x80\x99 and dawdled to the\r\nhearthrug where Mr. Bounderby stood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98speak up!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAfter the four days he had passed, this address fell rudely and\r\ndiscordantly on Stephen\xe2\x80\x99s ear.  Besides being a rough handling of his\r\nwounded mind, it seemed to assume that he really was the self-interested\r\ndeserter he had been called.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What were it, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 said Stephen, \xe2\x80\x98as yo were pleased to want wi\xe2\x80\x99 me?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why, I have told you,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Bounderby.  \xe2\x80\x98Speak up like a man, since\r\nyou are a man, and tell us about yourself and this Combination.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Wi\xe2\x80\x99 yor pardon, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 said Stephen Blackpool, \xe2\x80\x98I ha\xe2\x80\x99 nowt to sen about\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. Bounderby, who was always more or less like a Wind, finding something\r\nin his way here, began to blow at it directly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now, look here, Harthouse,\xe2\x80\x99 said he, \xe2\x80\x98here\xe2\x80\x99s a specimen of \xe2\x80\x99em.  When\r\nthis man was here once before, I warned this man against the mischievous\r\nstrangers who are always about\xe2\x80\x94and who ought to be hanged wherever they\r\nare found\xe2\x80\x94and I told this man that he was going in the wrong direction.\r\nNow, would you believe it, that although they have put this mark upon\r\nhim, he is such a slave to them still, that he\xe2\x80\x99s afraid to open his lips\r\nabout them?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I sed as I had nowt to sen, sir; not as I was fearfo\xe2\x80\x99 o\xe2\x80\x99 openin\xe2\x80\x99 my\r\nlips.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You said!  Ah!  _I_ know what you said; more than that, I know what you\r\nmean, you see.  Not always the same thing, by the Lord Harry!  Quite\r\ndifferent things.  You had better tell us at once, that that fellow\r\nSlackbridge is not in the town, stirring up the people to mutiny; and\r\nthat he is not a regular qualified leader of the people: that is, a most\r\nconfounded scoundrel.  You had better tell us so at once; you can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndeceive me.  You want to tell us so.  Why don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m as sooary as yo, sir, when the people\xe2\x80\x99s leaders is bad,\xe2\x80\x99 said\r\nStephen, shaking his head.  \xe2\x80\x98They taks such as offers.  Haply \xe2\x80\x99tis na\xe2\x80\x99\r\nthe sma\xe2\x80\x99est o\xe2\x80\x99 their misfortuns when they can get no better.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe wind began to get boisterous.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now, you\xe2\x80\x99ll think this pretty well, Harthouse,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Bounderby.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You\xe2\x80\x99ll think this tolerably strong.
