Pray, Mr.\r\nBlackpool\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94wind springing up very fast\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98may I take the liberty of asking\r\nyou how it happens that you refused to be in this Combination?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98How \xe2\x80\x99t happens?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah!\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Bounderby, with his thumbs in the arms of his coat, and\r\njerking his head and shutting his eyes in confidence with the opposite\r\nwall: \xe2\x80\x98how it happens.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99d leefer not coom to \xe2\x80\x99t, sir; but sin you put th\xe2\x80\x99 question\xe2\x80\x94an\xe2\x80\x99 not\r\nwant\xe2\x80\x99n t\xe2\x80\x99 be ill-manner\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll answer.  I ha passed a promess.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Not to me, you know,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby.  (Gusty weather with deceitful\r\ncalms.  One now prevailing.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98O no, sir.  Not to yo.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98As for me, any consideration for me has had just nothing at all to do\r\nwith it,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, still in confidence with the wall.  \xe2\x80\x98If only\r\nJosiah Bounderby of Coketown had been in question, you would have joined\r\nand made no bones about it?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why yes, sir.  \xe2\x80\x99Tis true.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Though he knows,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Bounderby, now blowing a gale, \xe2\x80\x98that there\r\nare a set of rascals and rebels whom transportation is too good for!\r\nNow, Mr. Harthouse, you have been knocking about in the world some time.\r\nDid you ever meet with anything like that man out of this blessed\r\ncountry?\xe2\x80\x99  And Mr. Bounderby pointed him out for inspection, with an\r\nangry finger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Nay, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 said Stephen Blackpool, staunchly protesting against the\r\nwords that had been used, and instinctively addressing himself to Louisa,\r\nafter glancing at her face.  \xe2\x80\x98Not rebels, nor yet rascals. They\xe2\x80\x99re true to one another, faithfo\xe2\x80\x99 to one another,\r\n\xe2\x80\x99fectionate to one another, e\xe2\x80\x99en to death. Go through with it while you are about\r\nit. But \xe2\x80\x99tis so.  I know\r\n\xe2\x80\x99tis, as I know the heavens is over me ahint the smoke.  We\xe2\x80\x99re patient\r\ntoo, an\xe2\x80\x99 wants in general to do right.  An\xe2\x80\x99 I canna think the fawt is aw\r\nwi\xe2\x80\x99 us.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now, my friend,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Bounderby, whom he could not have exasperated\r\nmore, quite unconscious of it though he was, than by seeming to appeal to\r\nany one else, \xe2\x80\x98if you will favour me with your attention for half a\r\nminute, I should like to have a word or two with you.  You said just now,\r\nthat you had nothing to tell us about this business.  You are quite sure\r\nof that before we go any further.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Sir, I am sure on \xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Here\xe2\x80\x99s a gentleman from London present,\xe2\x80\x99 Mr. Bounderby made a backhanded\r\npoint at Mr. James Harthouse with his thumb, \xe2\x80\x98a Parliament gentleman.  I\r\nshould like him to hear a short bit of dialogue between you and me,\r\ninstead of taking the substance of it\xe2\x80\x94for I know precious well,\r\nbeforehand, what it will be; nobody knows better than I do, take\r\nnotice!\xe2\x80\x94instead of receiving it on trust from my mouth.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nStephen bent his head to the gentleman from London, and showed a rather\r\nmore troubled mind than usual. Look round town\xe2\x80\x94so rich as\r\n\xe2\x80\x99tis\xe2\x80\x94and see the numbers o\xe2\x80\x99 people as has been broughten into bein heer,\r\nfur to weave, an\xe2\x80\x99 to card, an\xe2\x80\x99 to piece out a livin\xe2\x80\x99, aw the same one\r\nway, somehows, \xe2\x80\x99twixt their cradles and their graves. Aw that\xe2\x80\x99s now about me in this room were heer afore I coom, an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nwill be heer when I am gone.  Put that clock aboard a ship an\xe2\x80\x99 pack it\r\noff to Norfolk Island, an\xe2\x80\x99 the time will go on just the same.  So \xe2\x80\x99tis\r\nwi\xe2\x80\x99 Slackbridge every bit.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nReverting for a moment to his former refuge, he observed a cautionary\r\nmovement of her eyes towards the door.  Stepping back, he put his hand\r\nupon the lock.  But he had not spoken out of his own will and desire; and\r\nhe felt it in his heart a noble return for his late injurious treatment\r\nto be faithful to the last to those who had repudiated him.  He stayed to\r\nfinish what was in his mind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Sir, I canna, wi\xe2\x80\x99 my little learning an\xe2\x80\x99 my common way, tell the\r\ngenelman what will better aw this\xe2\x80\x94though some working men o\xe2\x80\x99 this town\r\ncould, above my powers\xe2\x80\x94but I can tell him what I know will never do \xe2\x80\x99t.\r\nThe strong hand will never do \xe2\x80\x99t.  Vict\xe2\x80\x99ry and triumph will never do \xe2\x80\x99t.\r\nAgreeing fur to mak one side unnat\xe2\x80\x99rally awlus and for ever right, and\r\ntoother side unnat\xe2\x80\x99rally awlus and for ever wrong, will never, never do\r\n\xe2\x80\x99t.  Nor yet lettin alone will never do \xe2\x80\x99t.  Let thousands upon thousands\r\nalone, aw leading the like lives and aw faw\xe2\x80\x99en into the like muddle, and\r\nthey will be as one, and yo will be as anoother, wi\xe2\x80\x99 a black unpassable\r\nworld betwixt yo, just as long or short a time as sich-like misery can\r\nlast.  Not drawin nigh to fok, wi\xe2\x80\x99 kindness and patience an\xe2\x80\x99 cheery ways,\r\nthat so draws nigh to one another in their monny troubles, and so\r\ncherishes one another in their distresses wi\xe2\x80\x99 what they need\r\nthemseln\xe2\x80\x94like, I humbly believe, as no people the genelman ha seen in aw\r\nhis travels can beat\xe2\x80\x94will never do \xe2\x80\x99t till th\xe2\x80\x99 Sun turns t\xe2\x80\x99 ice. And you go about, sowing it and\r\nraising crops.  That\xe2\x80\x99s the business of _your_ life, my friend.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nStephen shook his head, mutely protesting that indeed he had other\r\nbusiness to do for his life.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are such a waspish, raspish, ill-conditioned chap, you see,\xe2\x80\x99 said\r\nMr. Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98that even your own Union, the men who know you best, will\r\nhave nothing to do with you.  I never thought those fellows could be\r\nright in anything; but I tell you what!  I so far go along with them for\r\na novelty, that _I_\xe2\x80\x99ll have nothing to do with you either.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nStephen raised his eyes quickly to his face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You can finish off what you\xe2\x80\x99re at,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Bounderby, with a meaning\r\nnod, \xe2\x80\x98and then go elsewhere.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Sir, yo know weel,\xe2\x80\x99 said Stephen expressively, \xe2\x80\x98that if I canna get work\r\nwi\xe2\x80\x99 yo, I canna get it elsewheer.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe reply was, \xe2\x80\x98What I know, I know; and what you know, you know.  I have\r\nno more to say about it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nStephen glanced at Louisa again, but her eyes were raised to his no more;\r\ntherefore, with a sigh, and saying, barely above his breath, \xe2\x80\x98Heaven help\r\nus aw in this world!\xe2\x80\x99 he departed.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI\r\nFADING AWAY\r\n\r\n\r\nIT was falling dark when Stephen came out of Mr. Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s house.  The\r\nshadows of night had gathered so fast, that he did not look about him\r\nwhen he closed the door, but plodded straight along the street.  Nothing\r\nwas further from his thoughts than the curious old woman he had\r\nencountered on his previous visit to the same house, when he heard a step\r\nbehind him that he knew, and turning, saw her in Rachael\xe2\x80\x99s company.\r\n\r\nHe saw Rachael first, as he had heard her only.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah, Rachael, my dear!  Missus, thou wi\xe2\x80\x99 her!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, and now you are surprised to be sure, and with reason I must say,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nthe old woman returned.  \xe2\x80\x98Here I am again, you see.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But how wi\xe2\x80\x99 Rachael?\xe2\x80\x99 said Stephen, falling into their step, walking\r\nbetween them, and looking from the one to the other.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why, I come to be with this good lass pretty much as I came to be with\r\nyou,\xe2\x80\x99 said the old woman, cheerfully, taking the reply upon herself.  \xe2\x80\x98My\r\nvisiting time is later this year than usual, for I have been rather\r\ntroubled with shortness of breath, and so put it off till the weather was\r\nfine and warm.  For the same reason I don\xe2\x80\x99t make all my journey in one\r\nday, but divide it into two days, and get a bed to-night at the\r\nTravellers\xe2\x80\x99 Coffee House down by the railroad (a nice clean house), and\r\ngo back Parliamentary, at six in the morning.  Well, but what has this to\r\ndo with this good lass, says you?  I\xe2\x80\x99m going to tell you.  I have heard\r\nof Mr. Bounderby being married.  I read it in the paper, where it looked\r\ngrand\xe2\x80\x94oh, it looked fine!\xe2\x80\x99 the old woman dwelt on it with strange\r\nenthusiasm: \xe2\x80\x98and I want to see his wife.  I have never seen her yet.\r\nNow, if you\xe2\x80\x99ll believe me, she hasn\xe2\x80\x99t come out of that house since noon\r\nto-day.  So not to give her up too easily, I was waiting about, a little\r\nlast bit more, when I passed close to this good lass two or three times;\r\nand her face being so friendly I spoke to her, and she spoke to me.\r\nThere!\xe2\x80\x99 said the old woman to Stephen, \xe2\x80\x98you can make all the rest out for\r\nyourself now, a deal shorter than I can, I dare say!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nOnce again, Stephen had to conquer an instinctive propensity to dislike\r\nthis old woman, though her manner was as honest and simple as a manner\r\npossibly could be.  With a gentleness that was as natural to him as he\r\nknew it to be to Rachael, he pursued the subject that interested her in\r\nher old age.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, missus,\xe2\x80\x99 said he, \xe2\x80\x98I ha seen the lady, and she were young and\r\nhansom.  Wi\xe2\x80\x99 fine dark thinkin eyes, and a still way, Rachael, as I ha\r\nnever seen the like on.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Young and handsome.  Yes!\xe2\x80\x99 cried the old woman, quite delighted.  \xe2\x80\x98As\r\nbonny as a rose!  And what a happy wife!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Aye, missus, I suppose she be,\xe2\x80\x99 said Stephen.  But with a doubtful\r\nglance at Rachael.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Suppose she be?  She must be.  She\xe2\x80\x99s your master\xe2\x80\x99s wife,\xe2\x80\x99 returned the\r\nold woman.\r\n\r\nStephen nodded assent.  \xe2\x80\x98Though as to master,\xe2\x80\x99 said he, glancing again at\r\nRachael, \xe2\x80\x98not master onny more.  That\xe2\x80\x99s aw enden \xe2\x80\x99twixt him and me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Have you left his work, Stephen?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Rachael, anxiously and quickly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why, Rachael,\xe2\x80\x99 he replied, \xe2\x80\x98whether I ha lef\xe2\x80\x99n his work, or whether his\r\nwork ha lef\xe2\x80\x99n me, cooms t\xe2\x80\x99 th\xe2\x80\x99 same.  His work and me are parted.  \xe2\x80\x99Tis\r\nas weel so\xe2\x80\x94better, I were thinkin when yo coom up wi\xe2\x80\x99 me.  It would ha\r\nbrought\xe2\x80\x99n trouble upon trouble if I had stayed theer.  Haply \xe2\x80\x99tis a\r\nkindness to monny that I go; haply \xe2\x80\x99tis a kindness to myseln; anyways it\r\nmun be done.  I mun turn my face fro Coketown fur th\xe2\x80\x99 time, and seek a\r\nfort\xe2\x80\x99n, dear, by beginnin fresh.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Where will you go, Stephen?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I donno t\xe2\x80\x99night,\xe2\x80\x99 said he, lifting off his hat, and smoothing his thin\r\nhair with the flat of his hand.  \xe2\x80\x98But I\xe2\x80\x99m not goin t\xe2\x80\x99night, Rachael, nor\r\nyet t\xe2\x80\x99morrow.  \xe2\x80\x99Tan\xe2\x80\x99t easy overmuch t\xe2\x80\x99 know wheer t\xe2\x80\x99 turn, but a good\r\nheart will coom to me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHerein, too, the sense of even thinking unselfishly aided him.  Before he\r\nhad so much as closed Mr. Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s door, he had reflected that at\r\nleast his being obliged to go away was good for her, as it would save her\r\nfrom the chance of being brought into question for not withdrawing from\r\nhim.  Though it would cost him a hard pang to leave her, and though he\r\ncould think of no similar place in which his condemnation would not\r\npursue him, perhaps it was almost a relief to be forced away from the\r\nendurance of the last four days, even to unknown difficulties and\r\ndistresses.\r\n\r\nSo he said, with truth, \xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m more leetsome, Rachael, under \xe2\x80\x99t, than I\r\ncould\xe2\x80\x99n ha believed.\xe2\x80\x99  It was not her part to make his burden heavier.\r\nShe answered with her comforting smile, and the three walked on together.\r\n\r\nAge, especially when it strives to be self-reliant and cheerful, finds\r\nmuch consideration among the poor.  The old woman was so decent and\r\ncontented, and made so light of her infirmities, though they had\r\nincreased upon her since her former interview with Stephen, that they\r\nboth took an interest in her.  She was too sprightly to allow of their\r\nwalking at a slow pace on her account, but she was very grateful to be\r\ntalked to, and very willing to talk to any extent: so, when they came to\r\ntheir part of the town, she was more brisk and vivacious than ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Come to my poor place, missus,\xe2\x80\x99 said Stephen, \xe2\x80\x98and tak a coop o\xe2\x80\x99 tea.\r\nRachael will coom then; and arterwards I\xe2\x80\x99ll see thee safe t\xe2\x80\x99 thy\r\nTravellers\xe2\x80\x99 lodgin.  \xe2\x80\x99T may be long, Rachael, ere ever I ha th\xe2\x80\x99 chance o\xe2\x80\x99\r\nthy coompany agen.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThey complied, and the three went on to the house where he lodged.  When\r\nthey turned into a narrow street, Stephen glanced at his window with a\r\ndread that always haunted his desolate home; but it was open, as he had\r\nleft it, and no one was there.  The evil spirit of his life had flitted\r\naway again, months ago, and he had heard no more of her since.  The only\r\nevidence of her last return now, were the scantier moveables in his room,\r\nand the grayer hair upon his head.\r\n\r\nHe lighted a candle, set out his little tea-board, got hot water from\r\nbelow, and brought in small portions of tea and sugar, a loaf, and some\r\nbutter from the nearest shop.  The bread was new and crusty, the butter\r\nfresh, and the sugar lump, of course\xe2\x80\x94in fulfilment of the standard\r\ntestimony of the Coketown magnates, that these people lived like princes,\r\nsir.  Rachael made the tea (so large a party necessitated the borrowing\r\nof a cup), and the visitor enjoyed it mightily.  It was the first glimpse\r\nof sociality the host had had for many days.  He too, with the world a\r\nwide heath before him, enjoyed the meal\xe2\x80\x94again in corroboration of the\r\nmagnates, as exemplifying the utter want of calculation on the part of\r\nthese people, sir.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I ha never thowt yet, missus,\xe2\x80\x99 said Stephen, \xe2\x80\x98o\xe2\x80\x99 askin thy name.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe old lady announced herself as \xe2\x80\x98Mrs. Pegler.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98A widder, I think?\xe2\x80\x99 said Stephen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh, many long years!\xe2\x80\x99  Mrs. Pegler\xe2\x80\x99s husband (one of the best on record)\r\nwas already dead, by Mrs. Pegler\xe2\x80\x99s calculation, when Stephen was born.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x99Twere a bad job, too, to lose so good a one,\xe2\x80\x99 said Stephen.  \xe2\x80\x98Onny\r\nchildren?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMrs. Pegler\xe2\x80\x99s cup, rattling against her saucer as she held it, denoted\r\nsome nervousness on her part.  \xe2\x80\x98No,\xe2\x80\x99 she said.  \xe2\x80\x98Not now, not now.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Dead, Stephen,\xe2\x80\x99 Rachael softly hinted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m sooary I ha spok\xe2\x80\x99n on \xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x99 said Stephen, \xe2\x80\x98I ought t\xe2\x80\x99 hadn in my mind\r\nas I might touch a sore place.  I\xe2\x80\x94I blame myseln.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWhile he excused himself, the old lady\xe2\x80\x99s cup rattled more and more.  \xe2\x80\x98I\r\nhad a son,\xe2\x80\x99 she said, curiously distressed, and not by any of the usual\r\nappearances of sorrow; \xe2\x80\x98and he did well, wonderfully well.  But he is not\r\nto be spoken of if you please.  He is\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99  Putting down her cup, she moved\r\nher hands as if she would have added, by her action, \xe2\x80\x98dead!\xe2\x80\x99  Then she\r\nsaid aloud, \xe2\x80\x98I have lost him.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nStephen had not yet got the better of his having given the old lady pain,\r\nwhen his landlady came stumbling up the narrow stairs, and calling him to\r\nthe door, whispered in his ear.  Mrs. Pegler was by no means deaf, for\r\nshe caught a word as it was uttered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Bounderby!\xe2\x80\x99 she cried, in a suppressed voice, starting up from the\r\ntable.  \xe2\x80\x98Oh hide me!  Don\xe2\x80\x99t let me be seen for the world.  Don\xe2\x80\x99t let him\r\ncome up till I\xe2\x80\x99ve got away.  Pray, pray!\xe2\x80\x99  She trembled, and was\r\nexcessively agitated; getting behind Rachael, when Rachael tried to\r\nreassure her; and not seeming to know what she was about.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But hearken, missus, hearken,\xe2\x80\x99 said Stephen, astonished.  \xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x99Tisn\xe2\x80\x99t Mr.\r\nBounderby; \xe2\x80\x99tis his wife.  Yo\xe2\x80\x99r not fearfo\xe2\x80\x99 o\xe2\x80\x99 her.  Yo was hey-go-mad\r\nabout her, but an hour sin.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But are you sure it\xe2\x80\x99s the lady, and not the gentleman?\xe2\x80\x99 she asked, still\r\ntrembling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Certain sure!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well then, pray don\xe2\x80\x99t speak to me, nor yet take any notice of me,\xe2\x80\x99 said\r\nthe old woman.  \xe2\x80\x98Let me be quite to myself in this corner.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nStephen nodded; looking to Rachael for an explanation, which she was\r\nquite unable to give him; took the candle, went downstairs, and in a few\r\nmoments returned, lighting Louisa into the room.  She was followed by the\r\nwhelp.\r\n\r\nRachael had risen, and stood apart with her shawl and bonnet in her hand,\r\nwhen Stephen, himself profoundly astonished by this visit, put the candle\r\non the table.  Then he too stood, with his doubled hand upon the table\r\nnear it, waiting to be addressed.\r\n\r\nFor the first time in her life Louisa had come into one of the dwellings\r\nof the Coketown Hands; for the first time in her life she was face to\r\nface with anything like individuality in connection with them.  She knew\r\nof their existence by hundreds and by thousands.  She knew what results\r\nin work a given number of them would produce in a given space of time.\r\nShe knew them in crowds passing to and from their nests, like ants or\r\nbeetles.  But she knew from her reading infinitely more of the ways of\r\ntoiling insects than of these toiling men and women.\r\n\r\nSomething to be worked so much and paid so much, and there ended;\r\nsomething to be infallibly settled by laws of supply and demand;\r\nsomething that blundered against those laws, and floundered into\r\ndifficulty; something that was a little pinched when wheat was dear, and\r\nover-ate itself when wheat was cheap; something that increased at such a\r\nrate of percentage, and yielded such another percentage of crime, and\r\nsuch another percentage of pauperism; something wholesale, of which vast\r\nfortunes were made; something that occasionally rose like a sea, and did\r\nsome harm and waste (chiefly to itself), and fell again; this she knew\r\nthe Coketown Hands to be.  But, she had scarcely thought more of\r\nseparating them into units, than of separating the sea itself into its\r\ncomponent drops.\r\n\r\nShe stood for some moments looking round the room.  From the few chairs,\r\nthe few books, the common prints, and the bed, she glanced to the two\r\nwomen, and to Stephen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I have come to speak to you, in consequence of what passed just now.  I\r\nshould like to be serviceable to you, if you will let me.  Is this your\r\nwife?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nRachael raised her eyes, and they sufficiently answered no, and dropped\r\nagain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I remember,\xe2\x80\x99 said Louisa, reddening at her mistake; \xe2\x80\x98I recollect, now,\r\nto have heard your domestic misfortunes spoken of, though I was not\r\nattending to the particulars at the time.  It was not my meaning to ask a\r\nquestion that would give pain to any one here.  If I should ask any other\r\nquestion that may happen to have that result, give me credit, if you\r\nplease, for being in ignorance how to speak to you as I ought.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAs Stephen had but a little while ago instinctively addressed himself to\r\nher, so she now instinctively addressed herself to Rachael.  Her manner\r\nwas short and abrupt, yet faltering and timid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He has told you what has passed between himself and my husband?  You\r\nwould be his first resource, I think.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I have heard the end of it, young lady,\xe2\x80\x99 said Rachael.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Did I understand, that, being rejected by one employer, he would\r\nprobably be rejected by all?  I thought he said as much?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The chances are very small, young lady\xe2\x80\x94next to nothing\xe2\x80\x94for a man who\r\ngets a bad name among them.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What shall I understand that you mean by a bad name?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The name of being troublesome.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then, by the prejudices of his own class, and by the prejudices of the\r\nother, he is sacrificed alike?  Are the two so deeply separated in this\r\ntown, that there is no place whatever for an honest workman between\r\nthem?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nRachael shook her head in silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He fell into suspicion,\xe2\x80\x99 said Louisa, \xe2\x80\x98with his fellow-weavers,\r\nbecause\xe2\x80\x94he had made a promise not to be one of them.  I think it must\r\nhave been to you that he made that promise.  Might I ask you why he made\r\nit?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nRachael burst into tears.  \xe2\x80\x98I didn\xe2\x80\x99t seek it of him, poor lad.  I prayed\r\nhim to avoid trouble for his own good, little thinking he\xe2\x80\x99d come to it\r\nthrough me.  But I know he\xe2\x80\x99d die a hundred deaths, ere ever he\xe2\x80\x99d break\r\nhis word.  I know that of him well.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nStephen had remained quietly attentive, in his usual thoughtful attitude,\r\nwith his hand at his chin.  He now spoke in a voice rather less steady\r\nthan usual.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No one, excepting myseln, can ever know what honour, an\xe2\x80\x99 what love, an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nrespect, I bear to Rachael, or wi\xe2\x80\x99 what cause. Fortnet or\r\nmisfortnet, a man can but try; there\xe2\x80\x99s nowt to be done wi\xe2\x80\x99out tryin\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94cept\r\nlaying down and dying.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98How will you travel?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Afoot, my kind ledy, afoot.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nLouisa coloured, and a purse appeared in her hand.  The rustling of a\r\nbank-note was audible, as she unfolded one and laid it on the table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Rachael, will you tell him\xe2\x80\x94for you know how, without offence\xe2\x80\x94that this\r\nis freely his, to help him on his way? He was neither courtly, nor handsome, nor\r\npicturesque, in any respect; and yet his manner of accepting it, and of\r\nexpressing his thanks without more words, had a grace in it that Lord\r\nChesterfield could not have taught his son in a century.\r\n\r\nTom had sat upon the bed, swinging one leg and sucking his walking-stick\r\nwith sufficient unconcern, until the visit had attained this stage.\r\nSeeing his sister ready to depart, he got up, rather hurriedly, and put\r\nin a word.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Just wait a moment, Loo! But there\xe2\x80\x99s no harm in\r\nmy trying.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHis breath fell like a flame of fire on Stephen\xe2\x80\x99s ear, it was so hot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That was our light porter at the Bank,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom, \xe2\x80\x98who brought you the\r\nmessage to-night. Very well then.  Come along, Loo!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe pushed the door open as he called to her, but did not return into the\r\nroom, or wait to be lighted down the narrow stairs.  He was at the bottom\r\nwhen she began to descend, and was in the street before she could take\r\nhis arm.\r\n\r\nMrs. Pegler remained in her corner until the brother and sister were\r\ngone, and until Stephen came back with the candle in his hand.  She was\r\nin a state of inexpressible admiration of Mrs. Bounderby, and, like an\r\nunaccountable old woman, wept, \xe2\x80\x98because she was such a pretty dear.\xe2\x80\x99  Yet\r\nMrs. Pegler was so flurried lest the object of her admiration should\r\nreturn by chance, or anybody else should come, that her cheerfulness was\r\nended for that night.  It was late too, to people who rose early and\r\nworked hard; therefore the party broke up; and Stephen and Rachael\r\nescorted their mysterious acquaintance to the door of the Travellers\xe2\x80\x99\r\nCoffee House, where they parted from her.\r\n\r\nThey walked back together to the corner of the street where Rachael\r\nlived, and as they drew nearer and nearer to it, silence crept upon them.\r\nWhen they came to the dark corner where their unfrequent meetings always\r\nended, they stopped, still silent, as if both were afraid to speak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I shall strive t\xe2\x80\x99 see thee agen, Rachael, afore I go, but if not\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thou wilt not, Stephen, I know.  \xe2\x80\x99Tis better that we make up our minds\r\nto be open wi\xe2\x80\x99 one another.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thou\xe2\x80\x99rt awlus right.  \xe2\x80\x99Tis bolder and better.  I ha been thinkin then,\r\nRachael, that as \xe2\x80\x99tis but a day or two that remains, \xe2\x80\x99twere better for\r\nthee, my dear, not t\xe2\x80\x99 be seen wi\xe2\x80\x99 me.  \xe2\x80\x99T might bring thee into trouble,\r\nfur no good.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x99Tis not for that, Stephen, that I mind.  But thou know\xe2\x80\x99st our old\r\nagreement.  \xe2\x80\x99Tis for that.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, well,\xe2\x80\x99 said he.  \xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x99Tis better, onnyways.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thou\xe2\x80\x99lt write to me, and tell me all that happens, Stephen?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes.  What can I say now, but Heaven be wi\xe2\x80\x99 thee, Heaven bless thee,\r\nHeaven thank thee and reward thee!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98May it bless thee, Stephen, too, in all thy wanderings, and send thee\r\npeace and rest at last!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I towd thee, my dear,\xe2\x80\x99 said Stephen Blackpool\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98that night\xe2\x80\x94that I would\r\nnever see or think o\xe2\x80\x99 onnything that angered me, but thou, so much better\r\nthan me, should\xe2\x80\x99st be beside it.  Thou\xe2\x80\x99rt beside it now.  Thou mak\xe2\x80\x99st me\r\nsee it wi\xe2\x80\x99 a better eye.  Bless thee.  Good night.  Good-bye!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIt was but a hurried parting in a common street, yet it was a sacred\r\nremembrance to these two common people.  Utilitarian economists,\r\nskeletons of schoolmasters, Commissioners of Fact, genteel and used-up\r\ninfidels, gabblers of many little dog\xe2\x80\x99s-eared creeds, the poor you will\r\nhave always with you.  Cultivate in them, while there is yet time, the\r\nutmost graces of the fancies and affections, to adorn their lives so much\r\nin need of ornament; or, in the day of your triumph, when romance is\r\nutterly driven out of their souls, and they and a bare existence stand\r\nface to face, Reality will take a wolfish turn, and make an end of you.\r\n\r\nStephen worked the next day, and the next, uncheered by a word from any\r\none, and shunned in all his comings and goings as before.  At the end of\r\nthe second day, he saw land; at the end of the third, his loom stood\r\nempty.\r\n\r\nHe had overstayed his hour in the street outside the Bank, on each of the\r\ntwo first evenings; and nothing had happened there, good or bad.  That he\r\nmight not be remiss in his part of the engagement, he resolved to wait\r\nfull two hours, on this third and last night.\r\n\r\nThere was the lady who had once kept Mr. Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s house, sitting at\r\nthe first-floor window as he had seen her before; and there was the light\r\nporter, sometimes talking with her there, and sometimes looking over the\r\nblind below which had BANK upon it, and sometimes coming to the door and\r\nstanding on the steps for a breath of air.  When he first came out,\r\nStephen thought he might be looking for him, and passed near; but the\r\nlight porter only cast his winking eyes upon him slightly, and said\r\nnothing.\r\n\r\nTwo hours were a long stretch of lounging about, after a long day\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlabour.  Stephen sat upon the step of a door, leaned against a wall under\r\nan archway, strolled up and down, listened for the church clock, stopped\r\nand watched children playing in the street.  Some purpose or other is so\r\nnatural to every one, that a mere loiterer always looks and feels\r\nremarkable.  When the first hour was out, Stephen even began to have an\r\nuncomfortable sensation upon him of being for the time a disreputable\r\ncharacter.\r\n\r\nThen came the lamplighter, and two lengthening lines of light all down\r\nthe long perspective of the street, until they were blended and lost in\r\nthe distance.  Mrs. Sparsit closed the first-floor window, drew down the\r\nblind, and went up-stairs.  Presently, a light went up-stairs after her,\r\npassing first the fanlight of the door, and afterwards the two staircase\r\nwindows, on its way up.  By and by, one corner of the second-floor blind\r\nwas disturbed, as if Mrs. Sparsit\xe2\x80\x99s eye were there; also the other\r\ncorner, as if the light porter\xe2\x80\x99s eye were on that side.  Still, no\r\ncommunication was made to Stephen.  Much relieved when the two hours were\r\nat last accomplished, he went away at a quick pace, as a recompense for\r\nso much loitering.\r\n\r\nHe had only to take leave of his landlady, and lie down on his temporary\r\nbed upon the floor; for his bundle was made up for to-morrow, and all was\r\narranged for his departure.  He meant to be clear of the town very early;\r\nbefore the Hands were in the streets.\r\n\r\nIt was barely daybreak, when, with a parting look round his room,\r\nmournfully wondering whether he should ever see it again, he went out.\r\nThe town was as entirely deserted as if the inhabitants had abandoned it,\r\nrather than hold communication with him.  Everything looked wan at that\r\nhour.  Even the coming sun made but a pale waste in the sky, like a sad\r\nsea.\r\n\r\nBy the place where Rachael lived, though it was not in his way; by the\r\nred brick streets; by the great silent factories, not trembling yet; by\r\nthe railway, where the danger-lights were waning in the strengthening\r\nday; by the railway\xe2\x80\x99s crazy neighbourhood, half pulled down and half\r\nbuilt up; by scattered red brick villas, where the besmoked evergreens\r\nwere sprinkled with a dirty powder, like untidy snuff-takers; by\r\ncoal-dust paths and many varieties of ugliness; Stephen got to the top of\r\nthe hill, and looked back.\r\n\r\nDay was shining radiantly upon the town then, and the bells were going\r\nfor the morning work.  Domestic fires were not yet lighted, and the high\r\nchimneys had the sky to themselves.  Puffing out their poisonous volumes,\r\nthey would not be long in hiding it; but, for half an hour, some of the\r\nmany windows were golden, which showed the Coketown people a sun\r\neternally in eclipse, through a medium of smoked glass.\r\n\r\nSo strange to turn from the chimneys to the birds.  So strange, to have\r\nthe road-dust on his feet instead of the coal-grit.  So strange to have\r\nlived to his time of life, and yet to be beginning like a boy this summer\r\nmorning!  With these musings in his mind, and his bundle under his arm,\r\nStephen took his attentive face along the high road.  And the trees\r\narched over him, whispering that he left a true and loving heart behind.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII\r\nGUNPOWDER\r\n\r\n\r\nMR. JAMES HARTHOUSE, \xe2\x80\x98going in\xe2\x80\x99 for his adopted party, soon began to\r\nscore.  With the aid of a little more coaching for the political sages, a\r\nlittle more genteel listlessness for the general society, and a tolerable\r\nmanagement of the assumed honesty in dishonesty, most effective and most\r\npatronized of the polite deadly sins, he speedily came to be considered\r\nof much promise.  The not being troubled with earnestness was a grand\r\npoint in his favour, enabling him to take to the hard Fact fellows with\r\nas good a grace as if he had been born one of the tribe, and to throw all\r\nother tribes overboard, as conscious hypocrites.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Whom none of us believe, my dear Mrs. Bounderby, and who do not believe\r\nthemselves.  The only difference between us and the professors of virtue\r\nor benevolence, or philanthropy\xe2\x80\x94never mind the name\xe2\x80\x94is, that we know it\r\nis all meaningless, and say so; while they know it equally and will never\r\nsay so.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWhy should she be shocked or warned by this reiteration?  It was not so\r\nunlike her father\xe2\x80\x99s principles, and her early training, that it need\r\nstartle her.  Where was the great difference between the two schools,\r\nwhen each chained her down to material realities, and inspired her with\r\nno faith in anything else?  What was there in her soul for James\r\nHarthouse to destroy, which Thomas Gradgrind had nurtured there in its\r\nstate of innocence!\r\n\r\nIt was even the worse for her at this pass, that in her mind\xe2\x80\x94implanted\r\nthere before her eminently practical father began to form it\xe2\x80\x94a struggling\r\ndisposition to believe in a wider and nobler humanity than she had ever\r\nheard of, constantly strove with doubts and resentments.  With doubts,\r\nbecause the aspiration had been so laid waste in her youth.  With\r\nresentments, because of the wrong that had been done her, if it were\r\nindeed a whisper of the truth.  Upon a nature long accustomed to\r\nself-suppression, thus torn and divided, the Harthouse philosophy came as\r\na relief and justification.  Everything being hollow and worthless, she\r\nhad missed nothing and sacrificed nothing.  What did it matter, she had\r\nsaid to her father, when he proposed her husband.  What did it matter,\r\nshe said still.  With a scornful self-reliance, she asked herself, What\r\ndid anything matter\xe2\x80\x94and went on.\r\n\r\nTowards what?  Step by step, onward and downward, towards some end, yet\r\nso gradually, that she believed herself to remain motionless.  As to Mr.\r\nHarthouse, whither _he_ tended, he neither considered nor cared.  He had\r\nno particular design or plan before him: no energetic wickedness ruffled\r\nhis lassitude.  He was as much amused and interested, at present, as it\r\nbecame so fine a gentleman to be; perhaps even more than it would have\r\nbeen consistent with his reputation to confess.  Soon after his arrival\r\nhe languidly wrote to his brother, the honourable and jocular member,\r\nthat the Bounderbys were \xe2\x80\x98great fun;\xe2\x80\x99 and further, that the female\r\nBounderby, instead of being the Gorgon he had expected, was young, and\r\nremarkably pretty.  After that, he wrote no more about them, and devoted\r\nhis leisure chiefly to their house.  He was very often in their house, in\r\nhis flittings and visitings about the Coketown district; and was much\r\nencouraged by Mr. Bounderby.  It was quite in Mr. Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s gusty way\r\nto boast to all his world that _he_ didn\xe2\x80\x99t care about your highly\r\nconnected people, but that if his wife Tom Gradgrind\xe2\x80\x99s daughter did, she\r\nwas welcome to their company.\r\n\r\nMr. James Harthouse began to think it would be a new sensation, if the\r\nface which changed so beautifully for the whelp, would change for him.\r\n\r\nHe was quick enough to observe; he had a good memory, and did not forget\r\na word of the brother\xe2\x80\x99s revelations.  He interwove them with everything\r\nhe saw of the sister, and he began to understand her.  To be sure, the\r\nbetter and profounder part of her character was not within his scope of\r\nperception; for in natures, as in seas, depth answers unto depth; but he\r\nsoon began to read the rest with a student\xe2\x80\x99s eye.\r\n\r\nMr. Bounderby had taken possession of a house and grounds, about fifteen\r\nmiles from the town, and accessible within a mile or two, by a railway\r\nstriding on many arches over a wild country, undermined by deserted\r\ncoal-shafts, and spotted at night by fires and black shapes of stationary\r\nengines at pits\xe2\x80\x99 mouths.  This country, gradually softening towards the\r\nneighbourhood of Mr. Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s retreat, there mellowed into a rustic\r\nlandscape, golden with heath, and snowy with hawthorn in the spring of\r\nthe year, and tremulous with leaves and their shadows all the summer\r\ntime.  The bank had foreclosed a mortgage effected on the property thus\r\npleasantly situated, by one of the Coketown magnates, who, in his\r\ndetermination to make a shorter cut than usual to an enormous fortune,\r\noverspeculated himself by about two hundred thousand pounds.  These\r\naccidents did sometimes happen in the best regulated families of\r\nCoketown, but the bankrupts had no connexion whatever with the\r\nimprovident classes.\r\n\r\nIt afforded Mr. Bounderby supreme satisfaction to instal himself in this\r\nsnug little estate, and with demonstrative humility to grow cabbages in\r\nthe flower-garden.  He delighted to live, barrack-fashion, among the\r\nelegant furniture, and he bullied the very pictures with his origin.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 he would say to a visitor, \xe2\x80\x98I am told that Nickits,\xe2\x80\x99 the late\r\nowner, \xe2\x80\x98gave seven hundred pound for that Seabeach.  Now, to be plain\r\nwith you, if I ever, in the whole course of my life, take seven looks at\r\nit, at a hundred pound a look, it will be as much as I shall do.  No, by\r\nGeorge!  I don\xe2\x80\x99t forget that I am Josiah Bounderby of Coketown.  For\r\nyears upon years, the only pictures in my possession, or that I could\r\nhave got into my possession, by any means, unless I stole \xe2\x80\x99em, were the\r\nengravings of a man shaving himself in a boot, on the blacking bottles\r\nthat I was overjoyed to use in cleaning boots with, and that I sold when\r\nthey were empty for a farthing a-piece, and glad to get it!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThen he would address Mr. Harthouse in the same style.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Harthouse, you have a couple of horses down here.  Bring half a dozen\r\nmore if you like, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll find room for \xe2\x80\x99em.  There\xe2\x80\x99s stabling in this\r\nplace for a dozen horses; and unless Nickits is belied, he kept the full\r\nnumber.  A round dozen of \xe2\x80\x99em, sir.  When that man was a boy, he went to\r\nWestminster School.  Went to Westminster School as a King\xe2\x80\x99s Scholar, when\r\nI was principally living on garbage, and sleeping in market baskets.\r\nWhy, if I wanted to keep a dozen horses\xe2\x80\x94which I don\xe2\x80\x99t, for one\xe2\x80\x99s enough\r\nfor me\xe2\x80\x94I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t bear to see \xe2\x80\x99em in their stalls here, and think what my\r\nown lodging used to be.  I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t look at \xe2\x80\x99em, sir, and not order \xe2\x80\x99em\r\nout.  Yet so things come round.  You see this place; you know what sort\r\nof a place it is; you are aware that there\xe2\x80\x99s not a completer place of its\r\nsize in this kingdom or elsewhere\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t care where\xe2\x80\x94and here, got into\r\nthe middle of it, like a maggot into a nut, is Josiah Bounderby.  While\r\nNickits (as a man came into my office, and told me yesterday), Nickits,\r\nwho used to act in Latin, in the Westminster School plays, with the\r\nchief-justices and nobility of this country applauding him till they were\r\nblack in the face, is drivelling at this minute\xe2\x80\x94drivelling, sir!\xe2\x80\x94in a\r\nfifth floor, up a narrow dark back street in Antwerp.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIt was among the leafy shadows of this retirement, in the long sultry\r\nsummer days, that Mr. Harthouse began to prove the face which had set him\r\nwondering when he first saw it, and to try if it would change for him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mrs. Bounderby, I esteem it a most fortunate accident that I find you\r\nalone here.  I have for some time had a particular wish to speak to you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIt was not by any wonderful accident that he found her, the time of day\r\nbeing that at which she was always alone, and the place being her\r\nfavourite resort.  It was an opening in a dark wood, where some felled\r\ntrees lay, and where she would sit watching the fallen leaves of last\r\nyear, as she had watched the falling ashes at home.\r\n\r\nHe sat down beside her, with a glance at her face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Your brother.  My young friend Tom\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHer colour brightened, and she turned to him with a look of interest.  \xe2\x80\x98I\r\nnever in my life,\xe2\x80\x99 he thought, \xe2\x80\x98saw anything so remarkable and so\r\ncaptivating as the lighting of those features!\xe2\x80\x99  His face betrayed his\r\nthoughts\xe2\x80\x94perhaps without betraying him, for it might have been according\r\nto its instructions so to do.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Pardon me.  The expression of your sisterly interest is so beautiful\xe2\x80\x94Tom\r\nshould be so proud of it\xe2\x80\x94I know this is inexcusable, but I am so\r\ncompelled to admire.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Being so impulsive,\xe2\x80\x99 she said composedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mrs. Bounderby, no: you know I make no pretence with you.  You know I am\r\na sordid piece of human nature, ready to sell myself at any time for any\r\nreasonable sum, and altogether incapable of any Arcadian proceeding\r\nwhatever.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am waiting,\xe2\x80\x99 she returned, \xe2\x80\x98for your further reference to my brother.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You are rigid with me, and I deserve it.  I am as worthless a dog as you\r\nwill find, except that I am not false\xe2\x80\x94not false.  But you surprised and\r\nstarted me from my subject, which was your brother.  I have an interest\r\nin him.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Have you an interest in anything, Mr. Harthouse?\xe2\x80\x99 she asked, half\r\nincredulously and half gratefully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If you had asked me when I first came here, I should have said no.  I\r\nmust say now\xe2\x80\x94even at the hazard of appearing to make a pretence, and of\r\njustly awakening your incredulity\xe2\x80\x94yes.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe made a slight movement, as if she were trying to speak, but could not\r\nfind voice; at length she said, \xe2\x80\x98Mr. Harthouse, I give you credit for\r\nbeing interested in my brother.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thank you.  I claim to deserve it.  You know how little I do claim, but\r\nI will go that length.  You have done so much for him, you are so fond of\r\nhim; your whole life, Mrs. Bounderby, expresses such charming\r\nself-forgetfulness on his account\xe2\x80\x94pardon me again\xe2\x80\x94I am running wide of\r\nthe subject.  I am interested in him for his own sake.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe had made the slightest action possible, as if she would have risen in\r\na hurry and gone away.  He had turned the course of what he said at that\r\ninstant, and she remained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mrs. Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 he resumed, in a lighter manner, and yet with a show of\r\neffort in assuming it, which was even more expressive than the manner he\r\ndismissed; \xe2\x80\x98it is no irrevocable offence in a young fellow of your\r\nbrother\xe2\x80\x99s years, if he is heedless, inconsiderate, and expensive\xe2\x80\x94a little\r\ndissipated, in the common phrase.  Is he?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Allow me to be frank.  Do you think he games at all?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I think he makes bets.\xe2\x80\x99  Mr. Harthouse waiting, as if that were not her\r\nwhole answer, she added, \xe2\x80\x98I know he does.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Of course he loses?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Everybody does lose who bets.  May I hint at the probability of your\r\nsometimes supplying him with money for these purposes?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe sat, looking down; but, at this question, raised her eyes searchingly\r\nand a little resentfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Acquit me of impertinent curiosity, my dear Mrs. Bounderby.  I think Tom\r\nmay be gradually falling into trouble, and I wish to stretch out a\r\nhelping hand to him from the depths of my wicked experience.\xe2\x80\x94Shall I say\r\nagain, for his sake?  Is that necessary?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe seemed to try to answer, but nothing came of it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Candidly to confess everything that has occurred to me,\xe2\x80\x99 said James\r\nHarthouse, again gliding with the same appearance of effort into his more\r\nairy manner; \xe2\x80\x98I will confide to you my doubt whether he has had many\r\nadvantages.  Whether\xe2\x80\x94forgive my plainness\xe2\x80\x94whether any great amount of\r\nconfidence is likely to have been established between himself and his\r\nmost worthy father.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I do not,\xe2\x80\x99 said Louisa, flushing with her own great remembrance in that\r\nwise, \xe2\x80\x98think it likely.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Or, between himself, and\xe2\x80\x94I may trust to your perfect understanding of my\r\nmeaning, I am sure\xe2\x80\x94and his highly esteemed brother-in-law.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe flushed deeper and deeper, and was burning red when she replied in a\r\nfainter voice, \xe2\x80\x98I do not think that likely, either.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mrs. Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 said Harthouse, after a short silence, \xe2\x80\x98may there be a\r\nbetter confidence between yourself and me?  Tom has borrowed a\r\nconsiderable sum of you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You will understand, Mr. Harthouse,\xe2\x80\x99 she returned, after some\r\nindecision: she had been more or less uncertain, and troubled throughout\r\nthe conversation, and yet had in the main preserved her self-contained\r\nmanner; \xe2\x80\x98you will understand that if I tell you what you press to know,\r\nit is not by way of complaint or regret.  I would never complain of\r\nanything, and what I have done I do not in the least regret.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98So spirited, too!\xe2\x80\x99 thought James Harthouse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98When I married, I found that my brother was even at that time heavily in\r\ndebt.  Heavily for him, I mean.  Heavily enough to oblige me to sell some\r\ntrinkets.  They were no sacrifice.  I sold them very willingly.  I\r\nattached no value to them.  They, were quite worthless to me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nEither she saw in his face that he knew, or she only feared in her\r\nconscience that he knew, that she spoke of some of her husband\xe2\x80\x99s gifts.\r\nShe stopped, and reddened again.  If he had not known it before, he would\r\nhave known it then, though he had been a much duller man than he was.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Since then, I have given my brother, at various times, what money I\r\ncould spare: in short, what money I have had.  Confiding in you at all,\r\non the faith of the interest you profess for him, I will not do so by\r\nhalves.  Since you have been in the habit of visiting here, he has wanted\r\nin one sum as much as a hundred pounds.  I have not been able to give it\r\nto him.  I have felt uneasy for the consequences of his being so\r\ninvolved, but I have kept these secrets until now, when I trust them to\r\nyour honour.  I have held no confidence with any one, because\xe2\x80\x94you\r\nanticipated my reason just now.\xe2\x80\x99  She abruptly broke off.\r\n\r\nHe was a ready man, and he saw, and seized, an opportunity here of\r\npresenting her own image to her, slightly disguised as her brother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mrs. Bounderby, though a graceless person, of the world worldly, I feel\r\nthe utmost interest, I assure you, in what you tell me.  I cannot\r\npossibly be hard upon your brother.  I understand and share the wise\r\nconsideration with which you regard his errors.  With all possible\r\nrespect both for Mr. Gradgrind and for Mr. Bounderby, I think I perceive\r\nthat he has not been fortunate in his training.  Bred at a disadvantage\r\ntowards the society in which he has his part to play, he rushes into\r\nthese extremes for himself, from opposite extremes that have long been\r\nforced\xe2\x80\x94with the very best intentions we have no doubt\xe2\x80\x94upon him.  Mr.\r\nBounderby\xe2\x80\x99s fine bluff English independence, though a most charming\r\ncharacteristic, does not\xe2\x80\x94as we have agreed\xe2\x80\x94invite confidence.  If I might\r\nventure to remark that it is the least in the world deficient in that\r\ndelicacy to which a youth mistaken, a character misconceived, and\r\nabilities misdirected, would turn for relief and guidance, I should\r\nexpress what it presents to my own view.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAs she sat looking straight before her, across the changing lights upon\r\nthe grass into the darkness of the wood beyond, he saw in her face her\r\napplication of his very distinctly uttered words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98All allowance,\xe2\x80\x99 he continued, \xe2\x80\x98must be made.  I have one great fault to\r\nfind with Tom, however, which I cannot forgive, and for which I take him\r\nheavily to account.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nLouisa turned her eyes to his face, and asked him what fault was that?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Perhaps,\xe2\x80\x99 he returned, \xe2\x80\x98I have said enough.  Perhaps it would have been\r\nbetter, on the whole, if no allusion to it had escaped me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You alarm me, Mr. Harthouse.  Pray let me know it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98To relieve you from needless apprehension\xe2\x80\x94and as this confidence\r\nregarding your brother, which I prize I am sure above all possible\r\nthings, has been established between us\xe2\x80\x94I obey.  I cannot forgive him for\r\nnot being more sensible in every word, look, and act of his life, of the\r\naffection of his best friend; of the devotion of his best friend; of her\r\nunselfishness; of her sacrifice.  The return he makes her, within my\r\nobservation, is a very poor one.  What she has done for him demands his\r\nconstant love and gratitude, not his ill-humour and caprice.  Careless\r\nfellow as I am, I am not so indifferent, Mrs. Bounderby, as to be\r\nregardless of this vice in your brother, or inclined to consider it a\r\nvenial offence.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe wood floated before her, for her eyes were suffused with tears.  They\r\nrose from a deep well, long concealed, and her heart was filled with\r\nacute pain that found no relief in them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98In a word, it is to correct your brother in this, Mrs. Bounderby, that I\r\nmust aspire.  My better knowledge of his circumstances, and my direction\r\nand advice in extricating them\xe2\x80\x94rather valuable, I hope, as coming from a\r\nscapegrace on a much larger scale\xe2\x80\x94will give me some influence over him,\r\nand all I gain I shall certainly use towards this end.  I have said\r\nenough, and more than enough.  I seem to be protesting that I am a sort\r\nof good fellow, when, upon my honour, I have not the least intention to\r\nmake any protestation to that effect, and openly announce that I am\r\nnothing of the sort.  Yonder, among the trees,\xe2\x80\x99 he added, having lifted\r\nup his eyes and looked about; for he had watched her closely until now;\r\n\xe2\x80\x98is your brother himself; no doubt, just come down.  As he seems to be\r\nloitering in this direction, it may be as well, perhaps, to walk towards\r\nhim, and throw ourselves in his way.  He has been very silent and doleful\r\nof late.  Perhaps, his brotherly conscience is touched\xe2\x80\x94if there are such\r\nthings as consciences.  Though, upon my honour, I hear of them much too\r\noften to believe in them.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe assisted her to rise, and she took his arm, and they advanced to meet\r\nthe whelp.  He was idly beating the branches as he lounged along: or he\r\nstooped viciously to rip the moss from the trees with his stick.  He was\r\nstartled when they came upon him while he was engaged in this latter\r\npastime, and his colour changed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Halloa!\xe2\x80\x99 he stammered; \xe2\x80\x98I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know you were here.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Whose name, Tom,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Harthouse, putting his hand upon his shoulder\r\nand turning him, so that they all three walked towards the house\r\ntogether, \xe2\x80\x98have you been carving on the trees?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Whose name?\xe2\x80\x99 returned Tom.  \xe2\x80\x98Oh!  You mean what girl\xe2\x80\x99s name?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You have a suspicious appearance of inscribing some fair creature\xe2\x80\x99s on\r\nthe bark, Tom.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n         [Picture: Mr. Harthouse and Tom Gradgrind in the garden]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Not much of that, Mr. Harthouse, unless some fair creature with a\r\nslashing fortune at her own disposal would take a fancy to me.  Or she\r\nmight be as ugly as she was rich, without any fear of losing me.  I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\ncarve her name as often as she liked.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am afraid you are mercenary, Tom.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mercenary,\xe2\x80\x99 repeated Tom.  \xe2\x80\x98Who is not mercenary?  Ask my sister.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Have you so proved it to be a failing of mine, Tom?\xe2\x80\x99 said Louisa,\r\nshowing no other sense of his discontent and ill-nature.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You know whether the cap fits you, Loo,\xe2\x80\x99 returned her brother sulkily.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If it does, you can wear it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Tom is misanthropical to-day, as all bored people are now and then,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsaid Mr. Harthouse.  \xe2\x80\x98Don\xe2\x80\x99t believe him, Mrs. Bounderby.  He knows much\r\nbetter.  I shall disclose some of his opinions of you, privately\r\nexpressed to me, unless he relents a little.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98At all events, Mr. Harthouse,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom, softening in his admiration of\r\nhis patron, but shaking his head sullenly too, \xe2\x80\x98you can\xe2\x80\x99t tell her that I\r\never praised her for being mercenary.  I may have praised her for being\r\nthe contrary, and I should do it again, if I had as good reason.\r\nHowever, never mind this now; it\xe2\x80\x99s not very interesting to you, and I am\r\nsick of the subject.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThey walked on to the house, where Louisa quitted her visitor\xe2\x80\x99s arm and\r\nwent in.  He stood looking after her, as she ascended the steps, and\r\npassed into the shadow of the door; then put his hand upon her brother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nshoulder again, and invited him with a confidential nod to a walk in the\r\ngarden.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Tom, my fine fellow, I want to have a word with you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThey had stopped among a disorder of roses\xe2\x80\x94it was part of Mr. Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhumility to keep Nickits\xe2\x80\x99s roses on a reduced scale\xe2\x80\x94and Tom sat down on a\r\nterrace-parapet, plucking buds and picking them to pieces; while his\r\npowerful Familiar stood over him, with a foot upon the parapet, and his\r\nfigure easily resting on the arm supported by that knee.  They were just\r\nvisible from her window.  Perhaps she saw them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Tom, what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh!  Mr. Harthouse,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom with a groan, \xe2\x80\x98I am hard up, and bothered\r\nout of my life.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My good fellow, so am I.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You!\xe2\x80\x99 returned Tom.  \xe2\x80\x98You are the picture of independence.  Mr.\r\nHarthouse, I am in a horrible mess.  You have no idea what a state I have\r\ngot myself into\xe2\x80\x94what a state my sister might have got me out of, if she\r\nwould only have done it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe took to biting the rosebuds now, and tearing them away from his teeth\r\nwith a hand that trembled like an infirm old man\xe2\x80\x99s.  After one\r\nexceedingly observant look at him, his companion relapsed into his\r\nlightest air.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Tom, you are inconsiderate: you expect too much of your sister.  You\r\nhave had money of her, you dog, you know you have.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, Mr. Harthouse, I know I have.  How else was I to get it?  Here\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nold Bounderby always boasting that at my age he lived upon twopence a\r\nmonth, or something of that sort.  Here\xe2\x80\x99s my father drawing what he calls\r\na line, and tying me down to it from a baby, neck and heels.  Here\xe2\x80\x99s my\r\nmother who never has anything of her own, except her complaints.  What\r\n_is_ a fellow to do for money, and where _am_ I to look for it, if not to\r\nmy sister?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe was almost crying, and scattered the buds about by dozens.  Mr.\r\nHarthouse took him persuasively by the coat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But, my dear Tom, if your sister has not got it\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Not got it, Mr. Harthouse?  I don\xe2\x80\x99t say she has got it.  I may have\r\nwanted more than she was likely to have got.  But then she ought to get\r\nit.  She could get it.  It\xe2\x80\x99s of no use pretending to make a secret of\r\nmatters now, after what I have told you already; you know she didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmarry old Bounderby for her own sake, or for his sake, but for my sake.\r\nThen why doesn\xe2\x80\x99t she get what I want, out of him, for my sake?  She is\r\nnot obliged to say what she is going to do with it; she is sharp enough;\r\nshe could manage to coax it out of him, if she chose.  Then why doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nshe choose, when I tell her of what consequence it is?  But no.  There\r\nshe sits in his company like a stone, instead of making herself agreeable\r\nand getting it easily.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you may call this, but I call\r\nit unnatural conduct.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThere was a piece of ornamental water immediately below the parapet, on\r\nthe other side, into which Mr. James Harthouse had a very strong\r\ninclination to pitch Mr. Thomas Gradgrind junior, as the injured men of\r\nCoketown threatened to pitch their property into the Atlantic.  But he\r\npreserved his easy attitude; and nothing more solid went over the stone\r\nbalustrades than the accumulated rosebuds now floating about, a little\r\nsurface-island.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My dear Tom,\xe2\x80\x99 said Harthouse, \xe2\x80\x98let me try to be your banker.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98For God\xe2\x80\x99s sake,\xe2\x80\x99 replied Tom, suddenly, \xe2\x80\x98don\xe2\x80\x99t talk about bankers!\xe2\x80\x99  And\r\nvery white he looked, in contrast with the roses.  Very white.\r\n\r\nMr. Harthouse, as a thoroughly well-bred man, accustomed to the best\r\nsociety, was not to be surprised\xe2\x80\x94he could as soon have been affected\xe2\x80\x94but\r\nhe raised his eyelids a little more, as if they were lifted by a feeble\r\ntouch of wonder.  Albeit it was as much against the precepts of his\r\nschool to wonder, as it was against the doctrines of the Gradgrind\r\nCollege.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What is the present need, Tom?  Three figures?  Out with them.  Say what\r\nthey are.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mr. Harthouse,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Tom, now actually crying; and his tears were\r\nbetter than his injuries, however pitiful a figure he made: \xe2\x80\x98it\xe2\x80\x99s too\r\nlate; the money is of no use to me at present.  I should have had it\r\nbefore to be of use to me.  But I am very much obliged to you; you\xe2\x80\x99re a\r\ntrue friend.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nA true friend!  \xe2\x80\x98Whelp, whelp!\xe2\x80\x99 thought Mr. Harthouse, lazily; \xe2\x80\x98what an\r\nAss you are!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And I take your offer as a great kindness,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom, grasping his hand.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98As a great kindness, Mr. Harthouse.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well,\xe2\x80\x99 returned the other, \xe2\x80\x98it may be of more use by and by.  And, my\r\ngood fellow, if you will open your bedevilments to me when they come\r\nthick upon you, I may show you better ways out of them than you can find\r\nfor yourself.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thank you,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom, shaking his head dismally, and chewing rosebuds.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I wish I had known you sooner, Mr. Harthouse.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now, you see, Tom,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Harthouse in conclusion, himself tossing\r\nover a rose or two, as a contribution to the island, which was always\r\ndrifting to the wall as if it wanted to become a part of the mainland:\r\n\xe2\x80\x98every man is selfish in everything he does, and I am exactly like the\r\nrest of my fellow-creatures.  I am desperately intent;\xe2\x80\x99 the languor of\r\nhis desperation being quite tropical; \xe2\x80\x98on your softening towards your\r\nsister\xe2\x80\x94which you ought to do; and on your being a more loving and\r\nagreeable sort of brother\xe2\x80\x94which you ought to be.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I will be, Mr. Harthouse.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No time like the present, Tom.  Begin at once.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Certainly I will.  And my sister Loo shall say so.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Having made which bargain, Tom,\xe2\x80\x99 said Harthouse, clapping him on the\r\nshoulder again, with an air which left him at liberty to infer\xe2\x80\x94as he did,\r\npoor fool\xe2\x80\x94that this condition was imposed upon him in mere careless good\r\nnature to lessen his sense of obligation, \xe2\x80\x98we will tear ourselves asunder\r\nuntil dinner-time.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWhen Tom appeared before dinner, though his mind seemed heavy enough, his\r\nbody was on the alert; and he appeared before Mr. Bounderby came in.  \xe2\x80\x98I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to be cross, Loo,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, giving her his hand, and kissing\r\nher.  \xe2\x80\x98I know you are fond of me, and you know I am fond of you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAfter this, there was a smile upon Louisa\xe2\x80\x99s face that day, for some one\r\nelse.  Alas, for some one else!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98So much the less is the whelp the only creature that she cares for,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nthought James Harthouse, reversing the reflection of his first day\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nknowledge of her pretty face.  \xe2\x80\x98So much the less, so much the less.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII\r\nEXPLOSION\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE next morning was too bright a morning for sleep, and James Harthouse\r\nrose early, and sat in the pleasant bay window of his dressing-room,\r\nsmoking the rare tobacco that had had so wholesome an influence on his\r\nyoung friend.  Reposing in the sunlight, with the fragrance of his\r\neastern pipe about him, and the dreamy smoke vanishing into the air, so\r\nrich and soft with summer odours, he reckoned up his advantages as an\r\nidle winner might count his gains.  He was not at all bored for the time,\r\nand could give his mind to it.\r\n\r\nHe had established a confidence with her, from which her husband was\r\nexcluded.  He had established a confidence with her, that absolutely\r\nturned upon her indifference towards her husband, and the absence, now\r\nand at all times, of any congeniality between them.  He had artfully, but\r\nplainly, assured her that he knew her heart in its last most delicate\r\nrecesses; he had come so near to her through its tenderest sentiment; he\r\nhad associated himself with that feeling; and the barrier behind which\r\nshe lived, had melted away.  All very odd, and very satisfactory!\r\n\r\nAnd yet he had not, even now, any earnest wickedness of purpose in him.\r\nPublicly and privately, it were much better for the age in which he\r\nlived, that he and the legion of whom he was one were designedly bad,\r\nthan indifferent and purposeless.  It is the drifting icebergs setting\r\nwith any current anywhere, that wreck the ships.\r\n\r\nWhen the Devil goeth about like a roaring lion, he goeth about in a shape\r\nby which few but savages and hunters are attracted.  But, when he is\r\ntrimmed, smoothed, and varnished, according to the mode; when he is\r\naweary of vice, and aweary of virtue, used up as to brimstone, and used\r\nup as to bliss; then, whether he take to the serving out of red tape, or\r\nto the kindling of red fire, he is the very Devil.\r\n\r\nSo James Harthouse reclined in the window, indolently smoking, and\r\nreckoning up the steps he had taken on the road by which he happened to\r\nbe travelling.  The end to which it led was before him, pretty plainly;\r\nbut he troubled himself with no calculations about it.  What will be,\r\nwill be.\r\n\r\nAs he had rather a long ride to take that day\xe2\x80\x94for there was a public\r\noccasion \xe2\x80\x98to do\xe2\x80\x99 at some distance, which afforded a tolerable opportunity\r\nof going in for the Gradgrind men\xe2\x80\x94he dressed early and went down to\r\nbreakfast.  He was anxious to see if she had relapsed since the previous\r\nevening.  No.  He resumed where he had left off.  There was a look of\r\ninterest for him again.\r\n\r\nHe got through the day as much (or as little) to his own satisfaction, as\r\nwas to be expected under the fatiguing circumstances; and came riding\r\nback at six o\xe2\x80\x99clock.  There was a sweep of some half-mile between the\r\nlodge and the house, and he was riding along at a foot pace over the\r\nsmooth gravel, once Nickits\xe2\x80\x99s, when Mr. Bounderby burst out of the\r\nshrubbery, with such violence as to make his horse shy across the road.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Harthouse!\xe2\x80\x99 cried Mr. Bounderby.  \xe2\x80\x98Have you heard?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Heard what?\xe2\x80\x99 said Harthouse, soothing his horse, and inwardly favouring\r\nMr. Bounderby with no good wishes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then you _haven\xe2\x80\x99t_ heard!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I have heard you, and so has this brute.  I have heard nothing else.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. Bounderby, red and hot, planted himself in the centre of the path\r\nbefore the horse\xe2\x80\x99s head, to explode his bombshell with more effect.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The Bank\xe2\x80\x99s robbed!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You don\xe2\x80\x99t mean it!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Robbed last night, sir.  Robbed in an extraordinary manner.  Robbed with\r\na false key.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Of much?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. Bounderby, in his desire to make the most of it, really seemed\r\nmortified by being obliged to reply, \xe2\x80\x98Why, no; not of very much.  But it\r\nmight have been.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Of how much?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh! as a sum\xe2\x80\x94if you stick to a sum\xe2\x80\x94of not more than a hundred and fifty\r\npound,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, with impatience.  \xe2\x80\x98But it\xe2\x80\x99s not the sum; it\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nfact.  It\xe2\x80\x99s the fact of the Bank being robbed, that\xe2\x80\x99s the important\r\ncircumstance.  I am surprised you don\xe2\x80\x99t see it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My dear Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 said James, dismounting, and giving his bridle to\r\nhis servant, \xe2\x80\x98I _do_ see it; and am as overcome as you can possibly\r\ndesire me to be, by the spectacle afforded to my mental view.\r\nNevertheless, I may be allowed, I hope, to congratulate you\xe2\x80\x94which I do\r\nwith all my soul, I assure you\xe2\x80\x94on your not having sustained a greater\r\nloss.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thank\xe2\x80\x99ee,\xe2\x80\x99 replied Bounderby, in a short, ungracious manner.  \xe2\x80\x98But I\r\ntell you what.  It might have been twenty thousand pound.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I suppose it might.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Suppose it might!  By the Lord, you _may_ suppose so.  By George!\xe2\x80\x99 said\r\nMr. Bounderby, with sundry menacing nods and shakes of his head.  \xe2\x80\x98It\r\nmight have been twice twenty.  There\xe2\x80\x99s no knowing what it would have\r\nbeen, or wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have been, as it was, but for the fellows\xe2\x80\x99 being\r\ndisturbed.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nLouisa had come up now, and Mrs. Sparsit, and Bitzer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Here\xe2\x80\x99s Tom Gradgrind\xe2\x80\x99s daughter knows pretty well what it might have\r\nbeen, if you don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x99 blustered Bounderby.  \xe2\x80\x98Dropped, sir, as if she was\r\nshot when I told her!  Never knew her do such a thing before.  Does her\r\ncredit, under the circumstances, in my opinion!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe still looked faint and pale.  James Harthouse begged her to take his\r\narm; and as they moved on very slowly, asked her how the robbery had been\r\ncommitted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why, I am going to tell you,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, irritably giving his arm\r\nto Mrs. Sparsit.  \xe2\x80\x98If you hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been so mighty particular about the sum,\r\nI should have begun to tell you before.  You know this lady (for she _is_\r\na lady), Mrs. Sparsit?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I have already had the honour\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Very well.  And this young man, Bitzer, you saw him too on the same\r\noccasion?\xe2\x80\x99  Mr. Harthouse inclined his head in assent, and Bitzer\r\nknuckled his forehead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Very well.  They live at the Bank.  You know they live at the Bank,\r\nperhaps?  Very well.  Yesterday afternoon, at the close of business\r\nhours, everything was put away as usual.  In the iron room that this\r\nyoung fellow sleeps outside of, there was never mind how much.  In the\r\nlittle safe in young Tom\xe2\x80\x99s closet, the safe used for petty purposes,\r\nthere was a hundred and fifty odd pound.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98A hundred and fifty-four, seven, one,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bitzer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Come!\xe2\x80\x99 retorted Bounderby, stopping to wheel round upon him, \xe2\x80\x98let\xe2\x80\x99s have\r\nnone of _your_ interruptions.  It\xe2\x80\x99s enough to be robbed while you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nsnoring because you\xe2\x80\x99re too comfortable, without being put right with\r\n_your_ four seven ones.  I didn\xe2\x80\x99t snore, myself, when I was your age, let\r\nme tell you.  I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t victuals enough to snore.  And I didn\xe2\x80\x99t four seven\r\none.  Not if I knew it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nBitzer knuckled his forehead again, in a sneaking manner, and seemed at\r\nonce particularly impressed and depressed by the instance last given of\r\nMr. Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s moral abstinence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98A hundred and fifty odd pound,\xe2\x80\x99 resumed Mr. Bounderby.  \xe2\x80\x98That sum of\r\nmoney, young Tom locked in his safe, not a very strong safe, but that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nno matter now.  Everything was left, all right.  Some time in the night,\r\nwhile this young fellow snored\xe2\x80\x94Mrs. Sparsit, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, you say you have\r\nheard him snore?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Sir,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Mrs. Sparsit, \xe2\x80\x98I cannot say that I have heard him\r\nprecisely snore, and therefore must not make that statement.  But on\r\nwinter evenings, when he has fallen asleep at his table, I have heard\r\nhim, what I should prefer to describe as partially choke.  I have heard\r\nhim on such occasions produce sounds of a nature similar to what may be\r\nsometimes heard in Dutch clocks.  Not,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mrs. Sparsit, with a lofty\r\nsense of giving strict evidence, \xe2\x80\x98that I would convey any imputation on\r\nhis moral character.  Far from it.  I have always considered Bitzer a\r\nyoung man of the most upright principle; and to that I beg to bear my\r\ntestimony.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well!\xe2\x80\x99 said the exasperated Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98while he was snoring, _or_\r\nchoking, _or_ Dutch-clocking, _or_ something _or_ other\xe2\x80\x94being asleep\xe2\x80\x94some\r\nfellows, somehow, whether previously concealed in the house or not\r\nremains to be seen, got to young Tom\xe2\x80\x99s safe, forced it, and abstracted\r\nthe contents.  Being then disturbed, they made off; letting themselves\r\nout at the main door, and double-locking it again (it was double-locked,\r\nand the key under Mrs. Sparsit\xe2\x80\x99s pillow) with a false key, which was\r\npicked up in the street near the Bank, about twelve o\xe2\x80\x99clock to-day.  No\r\nalarm takes place, till this chap, Bitzer, turns out this morning, and\r\nbegins to open and prepare the offices for business.  Then, looking at\r\nTom\xe2\x80\x99s safe, he sees the door ajar, and finds the lock forced, and the\r\nmoney gone.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Where is Tom, by the by?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Harthouse, glancing round.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He has been helping the police,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98and stays behind at\r\nthe Bank.  I wish these fellows had tried to rob me when I was at his\r\ntime of life.  They would have been out of pocket if they had invested\r\neighteenpence in the job; I can tell \xe2\x80\x99em that.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Is anybody suspected?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Suspected?  I should think there was somebody suspected.  Egod!\xe2\x80\x99 said\r\nBounderby, relinquishing Mrs. Sparsit\xe2\x80\x99s arm to wipe his heated head.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Josiah Bounderby of Coketown is not to be plundered and nobody\r\nsuspected.  No, thank you!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMight Mr. Harthouse inquire Who was suspected?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, stopping and facing about to confront them all,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you.  It\xe2\x80\x99s not to be mentioned everywhere; it\xe2\x80\x99s not to be\r\nmentioned anywhere: in order that the scoundrels concerned (there\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\ngang of \xe2\x80\x99em) may be thrown off their guard.  So take this in confidence.\r\nNow wait a bit.\xe2\x80\x99  Mr. Bounderby wiped his head again.  \xe2\x80\x98What should you\r\nsay to;\xe2\x80\x99 here he violently exploded: \xe2\x80\x98to a Hand being in it?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I hope,\xe2\x80\x99 said Harthouse, lazily, \xe2\x80\x98not our friend Blackpot?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Say Pool instead of Pot, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98and that\xe2\x80\x99s the man.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nLouisa faintly uttered some word of incredulity and surprise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98O yes!  I know!\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, immediately catching at the sound.  \xe2\x80\x98I\r\nknow!  I am used to that.  I know all about it.  They are the finest\r\npeople in the world, these fellows are.  They have got the gift of the\r\ngab, they have.  They only want to have their rights explained to them,\r\nthey do.  But I tell you what.  Show me a dissatisfied Hand, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nshow you a man that\xe2\x80\x99s fit for anything bad, I don\xe2\x80\x99t care what it is.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAnother of the popular fictions of Coketown, which some pains had been\r\ntaken to disseminate\xe2\x80\x94and which some people really believed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But I am acquainted with these chaps,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby.  \xe2\x80\x98I can read \xe2\x80\x99em\r\noff, like books.  Mrs. Sparsit, ma\xe2\x80\x99am, I appeal to you.  What warning did\r\nI give that fellow, the first time he set foot in the house, when the\r\nexpress object of his visit was to know how he could knock Religion over,\r\nand floor the Established Church?  Mrs. Sparsit, in point of high\r\nconnexions, you are on a level with the aristocracy,\xe2\x80\x94did I say, or did I\r\nnot say, to that fellow, \xe2\x80\x9cyou can\xe2\x80\x99t hide the truth from me: you are not\r\nthe kind of fellow I like; you\xe2\x80\x99ll come to no good\xe2\x80\x9d?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Assuredly, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Mrs. Sparsit, \xe2\x80\x98you did, in a highly impressive\r\nmanner, give him such an admonition.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98When he shocked you, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby; \xe2\x80\x98when he shocked your\r\nfeelings?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Mrs. Sparsit, with a meek shake of her head, \xe2\x80\x98he\r\ncertainly did so.  Though I do not mean to say but that my feelings may\r\nbe weaker on such points\xe2\x80\x94more foolish if the term is preferred\xe2\x80\x94than they\r\nmight have been, if I had always occupied my present position.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. Bounderby stared with a bursting pride at Mr. Harthouse, as much as\r\nto say, \xe2\x80\x98I am the proprietor of this female, and she\xe2\x80\x99s worth your\r\nattention, I think.\xe2\x80\x99  Then, resumed his discourse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You can recall for yourself, Harthouse, what I said to him when you saw\r\nhim.  I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mince the matter with him.  I am never mealy with \xe2\x80\x99em.  I\r\nKNOW \xe2\x80\x99em.  Very well, sir.  Three days after that, he bolted.  Went off,\r\nnobody knows where: as my mother did in my infancy\xe2\x80\x94only with this\r\ndifference, that he is a worse subject than my mother, if possible.  What\r\ndid he do before he went?  What do you say;\xe2\x80\x99 Mr. Bounderby, with his hat\r\nin his hand, gave a beat upon the crown at every little division of his\r\nsentences, as if it were a tambourine; \xe2\x80\x98to his being seen\xe2\x80\x94night after\r\nnight\xe2\x80\x94watching the Bank?\xe2\x80\x94to his lurking about there\xe2\x80\x94after dark?\xe2\x80\x94To its\r\nstriking Mrs. Sparsit\xe2\x80\x94that he could be lurking for no good\xe2\x80\x94To her calling\r\nBitzer\xe2\x80\x99s attention to him, and their both taking notice of him\xe2\x80\x94And to its\r\nappearing on inquiry to-day\xe2\x80\x94that he was also noticed by the neighbours?\xe2\x80\x99\r\nHaving come to the climax, Mr. Bounderby, like an oriental dancer, put\r\nhis tambourine on his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Suspicious,\xe2\x80\x99 said James Harthouse, \xe2\x80\x98certainly.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I think so, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, with a defiant nod.  \xe2\x80\x98I think so.  But\r\nthere are more of \xe2\x80\x99em in it.  There\xe2\x80\x99s an old woman.
