Sissy said I was to tell him when you woke.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What a beaming face you have, Jane!\xe2\x80\x99 said Louisa, as her young\r\nsister\xe2\x80\x94timidly still\xe2\x80\x94bent down to kiss her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Have I?  I am very glad you think so.  I am sure it must be Sissy\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndoing.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe arm Louisa had begun to twine around her neck, unbent itself.  \xe2\x80\x98You\r\ncan tell father if you will.\xe2\x80\x99  Then, staying her for a moment, she said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It was you who made my room so cheerful, and gave it this look of\r\nwelcome?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh no, Louisa, it was done before I came.  It was\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nLouisa turned upon her pillow, and heard no more.  When her sister had\r\nwithdrawn, she turned her head back again, and lay with her face towards\r\nthe door, until it opened and her father entered.\r\n\r\nHe had a jaded anxious look upon him, and his hand, usually steady,\r\ntrembled in hers.  He sat down at the side of the bed, tenderly asking\r\nhow she was, and dwelling on the necessity of her keeping very quiet\r\nafter her agitation and exposure to the weather last night.  He spoke in\r\na subdued and troubled voice, very different from his usual dictatorial\r\nmanner; and was often at a loss for words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My dear Louisa.  My poor daughter.\xe2\x80\x99  He was so much at a loss at that\r\nplace, that he stopped altogether.  He tried again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My unfortunate child.\xe2\x80\x99  The place was so difficult to get over, that he\r\ntried again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It would be hopeless for me, Louisa, to endeavour to tell you how\r\noverwhelmed I have been, and still am, by what broke upon me last night.\r\nThe ground on which I stand has ceased to be solid under my feet.  The\r\nonly support on which I leaned, and the strength of which it seemed, and\r\nstill does seem, impossible to question, has given way in an instant.  I\r\nam stunned by these discoveries.  I have no selfish meaning in what I\r\nsay; but I find the shock of what broke upon me last night, to be very\r\nheavy indeed.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe could give him no comfort herein.  She had suffered the wreck of her\r\nwhole life upon the rock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I will not say, Louisa, that if you had by any happy chance undeceived\r\nme some time ago, it would have been better for us both; better for your\r\npeace, and better for mine.  For I am sensible that it may not have been\r\na part of my system to invite any confidence of that kind.  I had proved\r\nmy\xe2\x80\x94my system to myself, and I have rigidly administered it; and I must\r\nbear the responsibility of its failures.  I only entreat you to believe,\r\nmy favourite child, that I have meant to do right.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe said it earnestly, and to do him justice he had.  In gauging\r\nfathomless deeps with his little mean excise-rod, and in staggering over\r\nthe universe with his rusty stiff-legged compasses, he had meant to do\r\ngreat things.  Within the limits of his short tether he had tumbled\r\nabout, annihilating the flowers of existence with greater singleness of\r\npurpose than many of the blatant personages whose company he kept.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am well assured of what you say, father.  I know I have been your\r\nfavourite child.  I know you have intended to make me happy.  I have\r\nnever blamed you, and I never shall.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe took her outstretched hand, and retained it in his.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My dear, I have remained all night at my table, pondering again and\r\nagain on what has so painfully passed between us.  When I consider your\r\ncharacter; when I consider that what has been known to me for hours, has\r\nbeen concealed by you for years; when I consider under what immediate\r\npressure it has been forced from you at last; I come to the conclusion\r\nthat I cannot but mistrust myself.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe might have added more than all, when he saw the face now looking at\r\nhim.  He did add it in effect, perhaps, as he softly moved her scattered\r\nhair from her forehead with his hand.  Such little actions, slight in\r\nanother man, were very noticeable in him; and his daughter received them\r\nas if they had been words of contrition.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Gradgrind, slowly, and with hesitation, as well as with a\r\nwretched sense of happiness, \xe2\x80\x98if I see reason to mistrust myself for the\r\npast, Louisa, I should also mistrust myself for the present and the\r\nfuture.  To speak unreservedly to you, I do.  I am far from feeling\r\nconvinced now, however differently I might have felt only this time\r\nyesterday, that I am fit for the trust you repose in me; that I know how\r\nto respond to the appeal you have come home to make to me; that I have\r\nthe right instinct\xe2\x80\x94supposing it for the moment to be some quality of that\r\nnature\xe2\x80\x94how to help you, and to set you right, my child.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe had turned upon her pillow, and lay with her face upon her arm, so\r\nthat he could not see it.  All her wildness and passion had subsided;\r\nbut, though softened, she was not in tears.  Her father was changed in\r\nnothing so much as in the respect that he would have been glad to see her\r\nin tears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Some persons hold,\xe2\x80\x99 he pursued, still hesitating, \xe2\x80\x98that there is a\r\nwisdom of the Head, and that there is a wisdom of the Heart.  I have not\r\nsupposed so; but, as I have said, I mistrust myself now.  I have supposed\r\nthe head to be all-sufficient.  It may not be all-sufficient; how can I\r\nventure this morning to say it is!  If that other kind of wisdom should\r\nbe what I have neglected, and should be the instinct that is wanted,\r\nLouisa\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe suggested it very doubtfully, as if he were half unwilling to admit it\r\neven now.  She made him no answer, lying before him on her bed, still\r\nhalf-dressed, much as he had seen her lying on the floor of his room last\r\nnight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Louisa,\xe2\x80\x99 and his hand rested on her hair again, \xe2\x80\x98I have been absent from\r\nhere, my dear, a good deal of late; and though your sister\xe2\x80\x99s training has\r\nbeen pursued according to\xe2\x80\x94the system,\xe2\x80\x99 he appeared to come to that word\r\nwith great reluctance always, \xe2\x80\x98it has necessarily been modified by daily\r\nassociations begun, in her case, at an early age.  I ask you\xe2\x80\x94ignorantly\r\nand humbly, my daughter\xe2\x80\x94for the better, do you think?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Father,\xe2\x80\x99 she replied, without stirring, \xe2\x80\x98if any harmony has been\r\nawakened in her young breast that was mute in mine until it turned to\r\ndiscord, let her thank Heaven for it, and go upon her happier way, taking\r\nit as her greatest blessing that she has avoided my way.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98O my child, my child!\xe2\x80\x99 he said, in a forlorn manner, \xe2\x80\x98I am an unhappy\r\nman to see you thus!  What avails it to me that you do not reproach me,\r\nif I so bitterly reproach myself!\xe2\x80\x99  He bent his head, and spoke low to\r\nher.  \xe2\x80\x98Louisa, I have a misgiving that some change may have been slowly\r\nworking about me in this house, by mere love and gratitude: that what the\r\nHead had left undone and could not do, the Heart may have been doing\r\nsilently.  Can it be so?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe made him no reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am not too proud to believe it, Louisa.  How could I be arrogant, and\r\nyou before me!  Can it be so?  Is it so, my dear?\xe2\x80\x99  He looked upon her\r\nonce more, lying cast away there; and without another word went out of\r\nthe room.  He had not been long gone, when she heard a light tread near\r\nthe door, and knew that some one stood beside her.\r\n\r\nShe did not raise her head.  A dull anger that she should be seen in her\r\ndistress, and that the involuntary look she had so resented should come\r\nto this fulfilment, smouldered within her like an unwholesome fire.  All\r\nclosely imprisoned forces rend and destroy.  The air that would be\r\nhealthful to the earth, the water that would enrich it, the heat that\r\nwould ripen it, tear it when caged up.  So in her bosom even now; the\r\nstrongest qualities she possessed, long turned upon themselves, became a\r\nheap of obduracy, that rose against a friend.\r\n\r\nIt was well that soft touch came upon her neck, and that she understood\r\nherself to be supposed to have fallen asleep.  The sympathetic hand did\r\nnot claim her resentment.  Let it lie there, let it lie.\r\n\r\nIt lay there, warming into life a crowd of gentler thoughts; and she\r\nrested.  As she softened with the quiet, and the consciousness of being\r\nso watched, some tears made their way into her eyes.  The face touched\r\nhers, and she knew that there were tears upon it too, and she the cause\r\nof them.\r\n\r\nAs Louisa feigned to rouse herself, and sat up, Sissy retired, so that\r\nshe stood placidly near the bedside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I hope I have not disturbed you.  I have come to ask if you would let me\r\nstay with you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why should you stay with me?  My sister will miss you.  You are\r\neverything to her.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Am I?\xe2\x80\x99 returned Sissy, shaking her head.  \xe2\x80\x98I would be something to you,\r\nif I might.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What?\xe2\x80\x99 said Louisa, almost sternly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Whatever you want most, if I could be that.  At all events, I would like\r\nto try to be as near it as I can.  And however far off that may be, I\r\nwill never tire of trying.  Will you let me?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My father sent you to ask me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No indeed,\xe2\x80\x99 replied Sissy.  \xe2\x80\x98He told me that I might come in now, but he\r\nsent me away from the room this morning\xe2\x80\x94or at least\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe hesitated and stopped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98At least, what?\xe2\x80\x99 said Louisa, with her searching eyes upon her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I thought it best myself that I should be sent away, for I felt very\r\nuncertain whether you would like to find me here.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Have I always hated you so much?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I hope not, for I have always loved you, and have always wished that you\r\nshould know it.  But you changed to me a little, shortly before you left\r\nhome.  Not that I wondered at it.  You knew so much, and I knew so\r\nlittle, and it was so natural in many ways, going as you were among other\r\nfriends, that I had nothing to complain of, and was not at all hurt.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHer colour rose as she said it modestly and hurriedly.  Louisa understood\r\nthe loving pretence, and her heart smote her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98May I try?\xe2\x80\x99 said Sissy, emboldened to raise her hand to the neck that\r\nwas insensibly drooping towards her.\r\n\r\nLouisa, taking down the hand that would have embraced her in another\r\nmoment, held it in one of hers, and answered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98First, Sissy, do you know what I am?  I am so proud and so hardened, so\r\nconfused and troubled, so resentful and unjust to every one and to\r\nmyself, that everything is stormy, dark, and wicked to me.  Does not that\r\nrepel you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am so unhappy, and all that should have made me otherwise is so laid\r\nwaste, that if I had been bereft of sense to this hour, and instead of\r\nbeing as learned as you think me, had to begin to acquire the simplest\r\ntruths, I could not want a guide to peace, contentment, honour, all the\r\ngood of which I am quite devoid, more abjectly than I do.  Does not that\r\nrepel you?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98No!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIn the innocence of her brave affection, and the brimming up of her old\r\ndevoted spirit, the once deserted girl shone like a beautiful light upon\r\nthe darkness of the other.\r\n\r\nLouisa raised the hand that it might clasp her neck and join its fellow\r\nthere.  She fell upon her knees, and clinging to this stroller\xe2\x80\x99s child\r\nlooked up at her almost with veneration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Forgive me, pity me, help me!  Have compassion on my great need, and let\r\nme lay this head of mine upon a loving heart!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98O lay it here!\xe2\x80\x99 cried Sissy.  \xe2\x80\x98Lay it here, my dear.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II\r\nVERY RIDICULOUS\r\n\r\n\r\nMR. JAMES HARTHOUSE passed a whole night and a day in a state of so much\r\nhurry, that the World, with its best glass in his eye, would scarcely\r\nhave recognized him during that insane interval, as the brother Jem of\r\nthe honourable and jocular member.  He was positively agitated.  He\r\nseveral times spoke with an emphasis, similar to the vulgar manner.  He\r\nwent in and went out in an unaccountable way, like a man without an\r\nobject.  He rode like a highwayman.  In a word, he was so horribly bored\r\nby existing circumstances, that he forgot to go in for boredom in the\r\nmanner prescribed by the authorities.\r\n\r\nAfter putting his horse at Coketown through the storm, as if it were a\r\nleap, he waited up all night: from time to time ringing his bell with the\r\ngreatest fury, charging the porter who kept watch with delinquency in\r\nwithholding letters or messages that could not fail to have been\r\nentrusted to him, and demanding restitution on the spot.  The dawn\r\ncoming, the morning coming, and the day coming, and neither message nor\r\nletter coming with either, he went down to the country house.  There, the\r\nreport was, Mr. Bounderby away, and Mrs. Bounderby in town.  Left for\r\ntown suddenly last evening.  Not even known to be gone until receipt of\r\nmessage, importing that her return was not to be expected for the\r\npresent.\r\n\r\nIn these circumstances he had nothing for it but to follow her to town.\r\nHe went to the house in town.  Mrs. Bounderby not there.  He looked in at\r\nthe Bank.  Mr. Bounderby away and Mrs. Sparsit away.  Mrs. Sparsit away?\r\nWho could have been reduced to sudden extremity for the company of that\r\ngriffin!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well!  I don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom, who had his own reasons for being uneasy\r\nabout it.  \xe2\x80\x98She was off somewhere at daybreak this morning.  She\xe2\x80\x99s always\r\nfull of mystery; I hate her.  So I do that white chap; he\xe2\x80\x99s always got\r\nhis blinking eyes upon a fellow.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Where were you last night, Tom?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Where was I last night!\xe2\x80\x99 said Tom.  \xe2\x80\x98Come!  I like that.  I was waiting\r\nfor you, Mr. Harthouse, till it came down as _I_ never saw it come down\r\nbefore.  Where was I too!  Where were you, you mean.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I was prevented from coming\xe2\x80\x94detained.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Detained!\xe2\x80\x99 murmured Tom.  \xe2\x80\x98Two of us were detained.  I was detained\r\nlooking for you, till I lost every train but the mail.  It would have\r\nbeen a pleasant job to go down by that on such a night, and have to walk\r\nhome through a pond.  I was obliged to sleep in town after all.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Where?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Where?  Why, in my own bed at Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Did you see your sister?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98How the deuce,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Tom, staring, \xe2\x80\x98could I see my sister when she\r\nwas fifteen miles off?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nCursing these quick retorts of the young gentleman to whom he was so true\r\na friend, Mr. Harthouse disembarrassed himself of that interview with the\r\nsmallest conceivable amount of ceremony, and debated for the hundredth\r\ntime what all this could mean?  He made only one thing clear.  It was,\r\nthat whether she was in town or out of town, whether he had been\r\npremature with her who was so hard to comprehend, or she had lost\r\ncourage, or they were discovered, or some mischance or mistake, at\r\npresent incomprehensible, had occurred, he must remain to confront his\r\nfortune, whatever it was.  The hotel where he was known to live when\r\ncondemned to that region of blackness, was the stake to which he was\r\ntied.  As to all the rest\xe2\x80\x94What will be, will be.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98So, whether I am waiting for a hostile message, or an assignation, or a\r\npenitent remonstrance, or an impromptu wrestle with my friend Bounderby\r\nin the Lancashire manner\xe2\x80\x94which would seem as likely as anything else in\r\nthe present state of affairs\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll dine,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. James Harthouse.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Bounderby has the advantage in point of weight; and if anything of a\r\nBritish nature is to come off between us, it may be as well to be in\r\ntraining.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nTherefore he rang the bell, and tossing himself negligently on a sofa,\r\nordered \xe2\x80\x98Some dinner at six\xe2\x80\x94with a beefsteak in it,\xe2\x80\x99 and got through the\r\nintervening time as well as he could.  That was not particularly well;\r\nfor he remained in the greatest perplexity, and, as the hours went on,\r\nand no kind of explanation offered itself, his perplexity augmented at\r\ncompound interest.\r\n\r\nHowever, he took affairs as coolly as it was in human nature to do, and\r\nentertained himself with the facetious idea of the training more than\r\nonce.  \xe2\x80\x98It wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be bad,\xe2\x80\x99 he yawned at one time, \xe2\x80\x98to give the waiter\r\nfive shillings, and throw him.\xe2\x80\x99  At another time it occurred to him, \xe2\x80\x98Or\r\na fellow of about thirteen or fourteen stone might be hired by the hour.\xe2\x80\x99\r\nBut these jests did not tell materially on the afternoon, or his\r\nsuspense; and, sooth to say, they both lagged fearfully.\r\n\r\nIt was impossible, even before dinner, to avoid often walking about in\r\nthe pattern of the carpet, looking out of the window, listening at the\r\ndoor for footsteps, and occasionally becoming rather hot when any steps\r\napproached that room.  But, after dinner, when the day turned to\r\ntwilight, and the twilight turned to night, and still no communication\r\nwas made to him, it began to be as he expressed it, \xe2\x80\x98like the Holy Office\r\nand slow torture.\xe2\x80\x99  However, still true to his conviction that\r\nindifference was the genuine high-breeding (the only conviction he had),\r\nhe seized this crisis as the opportunity for ordering candles and a\r\nnewspaper.\r\n\r\nHe had been trying in vain, for half an hour, to read this newspaper,\r\nwhen the waiter appeared and said, at once mysteriously and\r\napologetically:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Beg your pardon, sir.  You\xe2\x80\x99re wanted, sir, if you please.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nA general recollection that this was the kind of thing the Police said to\r\nthe swell mob, caused Mr. Harthouse to ask the waiter in return, with\r\nbristling indignation, what the Devil he meant by \xe2\x80\x98wanted\xe2\x80\x99?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Beg your pardon, sir.  Young lady outside, sir, wishes to see you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Outside?  Where?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Outside this door, sir.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nGiving the waiter to the personage before mentioned, as a block-head duly\r\nqualified for that consignment, Mr. Harthouse hurried into the gallery.\r\nA young woman whom he had never seen stood there.  Plainly dressed, very\r\nquiet, very pretty.  As he conducted her into the room and placed a chair\r\nfor her, he observed, by the light of the candles, that she was even\r\nprettier than he had at first believed.  Her face was innocent and\r\nyouthful, and its expression remarkably pleasant.  She was not afraid of\r\nhim, or in any way disconcerted; she seemed to have her mind entirely\r\npreoccupied with the occasion of her visit, and to have substituted that\r\nconsideration for herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I speak to Mr. Harthouse?\xe2\x80\x99 she said, when they were alone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98To Mr. Harthouse.\xe2\x80\x99  He added in his mind, \xe2\x80\x98And you speak to him with the\r\nmost confiding eyes I ever saw, and the most earnest voice (though so\r\nquiet) I ever heard.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If I do not understand\xe2\x80\x94and I do not, sir\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94said Sissy, \xe2\x80\x98what your honour\r\nas a gentleman binds you to, in other matters:\xe2\x80\x99 the blood really rose in\r\nhis face as she began in these words: \xe2\x80\x98I am sure I may rely upon it to\r\nkeep my visit secret, and to keep secret what I am going to say.  I will\r\nrely upon it, if you will tell me I may so far trust\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You may, I assure you.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am young, as you see; I am alone, as you see.  In coming to you, sir,\r\nI have no advice or encouragement beyond my own hope.\xe2\x80\x99  He thought, \xe2\x80\x98But\r\nthat is very strong,\xe2\x80\x99 as he followed the momentary upward glance of her\r\neyes.  He thought besides, \xe2\x80\x98This is a very odd beginning.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t see\r\nwhere we are going.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I think,\xe2\x80\x99 said Sissy, \xe2\x80\x98you have already guessed whom I left just now!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I have been in the greatest concern and uneasiness during the last\r\nfour-and-twenty hours (which have appeared as many years),\xe2\x80\x99 he returned,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98on a lady\xe2\x80\x99s account.  The hopes I have been encouraged to form that you\r\ncome from that lady, do not deceive me, I trust.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I left her within an hour.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98At\xe2\x80\x94!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98At her father\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. Harthouse\xe2\x80\x99s face lengthened in spite of his coolness, and his\r\nperplexity increased.  \xe2\x80\x98Then I certainly,\xe2\x80\x99 he thought, \xe2\x80\x98do _not_ see\r\nwhere we are going.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98She hurried there last night.  She arrived there in great agitation, and\r\nwas insensible all through the night.  I live at her father\xe2\x80\x99s, and was\r\nwith her.  You may be sure, sir, you will never see her again as long as\r\nyou live.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. Harthouse drew a long breath; and, if ever man found himself in the\r\nposition of not knowing what to say, made the discovery beyond all\r\nquestion that he was so circumstanced.  The child-like ingenuousness with\r\nwhich his visitor spoke, her modest fearlessness, her truthfulness which\r\nput all artifice aside, her entire forgetfulness of herself in her\r\nearnest quiet holding to the object with which she had come; all this,\r\ntogether with her reliance on his easily given promise\xe2\x80\x94which in itself\r\nshamed him\xe2\x80\x94presented something in which he was so inexperienced, and\r\nagainst which he knew any of his usual weapons would fall so powerless;\r\nthat not a word could he rally to his relief.\r\n\r\nAt last he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98So startling an announcement, so confidently made, and by such lips, is\r\nreally disconcerting in the last degree.  May I be permitted to inquire,\r\nif you are charged to convey that information to me in those hopeless\r\nwords, by the lady of whom we speak?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I have no charge from her.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The drowning man catches at the straw.  With no disrespect for your\r\njudgment, and with no doubt of your sincerity, excuse my saying that I\r\ncling to the belief that there is yet hope that I am not condemned to\r\nperpetual exile from that lady\xe2\x80\x99s presence.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There is not the least hope.  The first object of my coming here, sir,\r\nis to assure you that you must believe that there is no more hope of your\r\never speaking with her again, than there would be if she had died when\r\nshe came home last night.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Must believe?  But if I can\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94or if I should, by infirmity of nature, be\r\nobstinate\xe2\x80\x94and won\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It is still true.  There is no hope.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nJames Harthouse looked at her with an incredulous smile upon his lips;\r\nbut her mind looked over and beyond him, and the smile was quite thrown\r\naway.\r\n\r\nHe bit his lip, and took a little time for consideration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well!  If it should unhappily appear,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, \xe2\x80\x98after due pains and\r\nduty on my part, that I am brought to a position so desolate as this\r\nbanishment, I shall not become the lady\xe2\x80\x99s persecutor.  But you said you\r\nhad no commission from her?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I have only the commission of my love for her, and her love for me.  I\r\nhave no other trust, than that I have been with her since she came home,\r\nand that she has given me her confidence.  I have no further trust, than\r\nthat I know something of her character and her marriage.  O Mr.\r\nHarthouse, I think you had that trust too!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe was touched in the cavity where his heart should have been\xe2\x80\x94in that\r\nnest of addled eggs, where the birds of heaven would have lived if they\r\nhad not been whistled away\xe2\x80\x94by the fervour of this reproach.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am not a moral sort of fellow,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, \xe2\x80\x98and I never make any\r\npretensions to the character of a moral sort of fellow.  I am as immoral\r\nas need be.  At the same time, in bringing any distress upon the lady who\r\nis the subject of the present conversation, or in unfortunately\r\ncompromising her in any way, or in committing myself by any expression of\r\nsentiments towards her, not perfectly reconcilable with\xe2\x80\x94in fact with\xe2\x80\x94the\r\ndomestic hearth; or in taking any advantage of her father\xe2\x80\x99s being a\r\nmachine, or of her brother\xe2\x80\x99s being a whelp, or of her husband\xe2\x80\x99s being a\r\nbear; I beg to be allowed to assure you that I have had no particularly\r\nevil intentions, but have glided on from one step to another with a\r\nsmoothness so perfectly diabolical, that I had not the slightest idea the\r\ncatalogue was half so long until I began to turn it over.  Whereas I\r\nfind,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. James Harthouse, in conclusion, \xe2\x80\x98that it is really in\r\nseveral volumes.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThough he said all this in his frivolous way, the way seemed, for that\r\nonce, a conscious polishing of but an ugly surface.  He was silent for a\r\nmoment; and then proceeded with a more self-possessed air, though with\r\ntraces of vexation and disappointment that would not be polished out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98After what has been just now represented to me, in a manner I find it\r\nimpossible to doubt\xe2\x80\x94I know of hardly any other source from which I could\r\nhave accepted it so readily\xe2\x80\x94I feel bound to say to you, in whom the\r\nconfidence you have mentioned has been reposed, that I cannot refuse to\r\ncontemplate the possibility (however unexpected) of my seeing the lady no\r\nmore.  I am solely to blame for the thing having come to this\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94and, I\r\ncannot say,\xe2\x80\x99 he added, rather hard up for a general peroration, \xe2\x80\x98that I\r\nhave any sanguine expectation of ever becoming a moral sort of fellow, or\r\nthat I have any belief in any moral sort of fellow whatever.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSissy\xe2\x80\x99s face sufficiently showed that her appeal to him was not finished.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You spoke,\xe2\x80\x99 he resumed, as she raised her eyes to him again, \xe2\x80\x98of your\r\nfirst object.  I may assume that there is a second to be mentioned?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Will you oblige me by confiding it?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mr. Harthouse,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Sissy, with a blending of gentleness and\r\nsteadiness that quite defeated him, and with a simple confidence in his\r\nbeing bound to do what she required, that held him at a singular\r\ndisadvantage, \xe2\x80\x98the only reparation that remains with you, is to leave\r\nhere immediately and finally.  I am quite sure that you can mitigate in\r\nno other way the wrong and harm you have done.  I am quite sure that it\r\nis the only compensation you have left it in your power to make.  I do\r\nnot say that it is much, or that it is enough; but it is something, and\r\nit is necessary.  Therefore, though without any other authority than I\r\nhave given you, and even without the knowledge of any other person than\r\nyourself and myself, I ask you to depart from this place to-night, under\r\nan obligation never to return to it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIf she had asserted any influence over him beyond her plain faith in the\r\ntruth and right of what she said; if she had concealed the least doubt or\r\nirresolution, or had harboured for the best purpose any reserve or\r\npretence; if she had shown, or felt, the lightest trace of any\r\nsensitiveness to his ridicule or his astonishment, or any remonstrance he\r\nmight offer; he would have carried it against her at this point.  But he\r\ncould as easily have changed a clear sky by looking at it in surprise, as\r\naffect her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98But do you know,\xe2\x80\x99 he asked, quite at a loss, \xe2\x80\x98the extent of what you\r\nask?  You probably are not aware that I am here on a public kind of\r\nbusiness, preposterous enough in itself, but which I have gone in for,\r\nand sworn by, and am supposed to be devoted to in quite a desperate\r\nmanner?  You probably are not aware of that, but I assure you it\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nfact.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIt had no effect on Sissy, fact or no fact.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Besides which,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Harthouse, taking a turn or two across the\r\nroom, dubiously, \xe2\x80\x98it\xe2\x80\x99s so alarmingly absurd.  It would make a man so\r\nridiculous, after going in for these fellows, to back out in such an\r\nincomprehensible way.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am quite sure,\xe2\x80\x99 repeated Sissy, \xe2\x80\x98that it is the only reparation in\r\nyour power, sir.  I am quite sure, or I would not have come here.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe glanced at her face, and walked about again.  \xe2\x80\x98Upon my soul, I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow what to say.  So immensely absurd!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIt fell to his lot, now, to stipulate for secrecy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If I were to do such a very ridiculous thing,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, stopping again\r\npresently, and leaning against the chimney-piece, \xe2\x80\x98it could only be in\r\nthe most inviolable confidence.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I will trust to you, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Sissy, \xe2\x80\x98and you will trust to me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHis leaning against the chimney-piece reminded him of the night with the\r\nwhelp.  It was the self-same chimney-piece, and somehow he felt as if\r\n_he_ were the whelp to-night.  He could make no way at all.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I suppose a man never was placed in a more ridiculous position,\xe2\x80\x99 he\r\nsaid, after looking down, and looking up, and laughing, and frowning, and\r\nwalking off, and walking back again.  \xe2\x80\x98But I see no way out of it.  What\r\nwill be, will be.  _This_ will be, I suppose.  I must take off myself, I\r\nimagine\xe2\x80\x94in short, I engage to do it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSissy rose.  She was not surprised by the result, but she was happy in\r\nit, and her face beamed brightly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You will permit me to say,\xe2\x80\x99 continued Mr. James Harthouse, \xe2\x80\x98that I doubt\r\nif any other ambassador, or ambassadress, could have addressed me with\r\nthe same success.  I must not only regard myself as being in a very\r\nridiculous position, but as being vanquished at all points.  Will you\r\nallow me the privilege of remembering my enemy\xe2\x80\x99s name?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98_My_ name?\xe2\x80\x99 said the ambassadress.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The only name I could possibly care to know, to-night.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Sissy Jupe.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Pardon my curiosity at parting.  Related to the family?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am only a poor girl,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Sissy.  \xe2\x80\x98I was separated from my\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x94he was only a stroller\xe2\x80\x94and taken pity on by Mr. Gradgrind.  I have\r\nlived in the house ever since.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe was gone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It wanted this to complete the defeat,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. James Harthouse,\r\nsinking, with a resigned air, on the sofa, after standing transfixed a\r\nlittle while.  \xe2\x80\x98The defeat may now be considered perfectly accomplished.\r\nOnly a poor girl\xe2\x80\x94only a stroller\xe2\x80\x94only James Harthouse made nothing\r\nof\xe2\x80\x94only James Harthouse a Great Pyramid of failure.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe Great Pyramid put it into his head to go up the Nile.  He took a pen\r\nupon the instant, and wrote the following note (in appropriate\r\nhieroglyphics) to his brother:\r\n\r\n    Dear Jack,\xe2\x80\x94All up at Coketown.  Bored out of the place, and going in\r\n    for camels.\r\n\r\n                                                           Affectionately,\r\n                                                                      JEM.\r\n\r\nHe rang the bell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Send my fellow here.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Gone to bed, sir.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Tell him to get up, and pack up.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe wrote two more notes.  One, to Mr. Bounderby, announcing his\r\nretirement from that part of the country, and showing where he would be\r\nfound for the next fortnight.  The other, similar in effect, to Mr.\r\nGradgrind.  Almost as soon as the ink was dry upon their superscriptions,\r\nhe had left the tall chimneys of Coketown behind, and was in a railway\r\ncarriage, tearing and glaring over the dark landscape.\r\n\r\nThe moral sort of fellows might suppose that Mr. James Harthouse derived\r\nsome comfortable reflections afterwards, from this prompt retreat, as one\r\nof his few actions that made any amends for anything, and as a token to\r\nhimself that he had escaped the climax of a very bad business.  But it\r\nwas not so, at all.  A secret sense of having failed and been\r\nridiculous\xe2\x80\x94a dread of what other fellows who went in for similar sorts of\r\nthings, would say at his expense if they knew it\xe2\x80\x94so oppressed him, that\r\nwhat was about the very best passage in his life was the one of all\r\nothers he would not have owned to on any account, and the only one that\r\nmade him ashamed of himself.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III\r\nVERY DECIDED\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE indefatigable Mrs. Sparsit, with a violent cold upon her, her voice\r\nreduced to a whisper, and her stately frame so racked by continual\r\nsneezes that it seemed in danger of dismemberment, gave chase to her\r\npatron until she found him in the metropolis; and there, majestically\r\nsweeping in upon him at his hotel in St. James\xe2\x80\x99s Street, exploded the\r\ncombustibles with which she was charged, and blew up.  Having executed\r\nher mission with infinite relish, this high-minded woman then fainted\r\naway on Mr. Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s coat-collar.\r\n\r\nMr. Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s first procedure was to shake Mrs. Sparsit off, and leave\r\nher to progress as she might through various stages of suffering on the\r\nfloor.  He next had recourse to the administration of potent\r\nrestoratives, such as screwing the patient\xe2\x80\x99s thumbs, smiting her hands,\r\nabundantly watering her face, and inserting salt in her mouth.  When\r\nthese attentions had recovered her (which they speedily did), he hustled\r\nher into a fast train without offering any other refreshment, and carried\r\nher back to Coketown more dead than alive.\r\n\r\nRegarded as a classical ruin, Mrs. Sparsit was an interesting spectacle\r\non her arrival at her journey\xe2\x80\x99s end; but considered in any other light,\r\nthe amount of damage she had by that time sustained was excessive, and\r\nimpaired her claims to admiration.  Utterly heedless of the wear and tear\r\nof her clothes and constitution, and adamant to her pathetic sneezes, Mr.\r\nBounderby immediately crammed her into a coach, and bore her off to Stone\r\nLodge.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now, Tom Gradgrind,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, bursting into his father-in-law\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nroom late at night; \xe2\x80\x98here\xe2\x80\x99s a lady here\xe2\x80\x94Mrs. Sparsit\xe2\x80\x94you know Mrs.\r\nSparsit\xe2\x80\x94who has something to say to you that will strike you dumb.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You have missed my letter!\xe2\x80\x99 exclaimed Mr. Gradgrind, surprised by the\r\napparition.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Missed your letter, sir!\xe2\x80\x99 bawled Bounderby.  \xe2\x80\x98The present time is no\r\ntime for letters.  No man shall talk to Josiah Bounderby of Coketown\r\nabout letters, with his mind in the state it\xe2\x80\x99s in now.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Gradgrind, in a tone of temperate remonstrance, \xe2\x80\x98I\r\nspeak of a very special letter I have written to you, in reference to\r\nLouisa.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Tom Gradgrind,\xe2\x80\x99 replied Bounderby, knocking the flat of his hand several\r\ntimes with great vehemence on the table, \xe2\x80\x98I speak of a very special\r\nmessenger that has come to me, in reference to Louisa.  Mrs. Sparsit,\r\nma\xe2\x80\x99am, stand forward!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThat unfortunate lady hereupon essaying to offer testimony, without any\r\nvoice and with painful gestures expressive of an inflamed throat, became\r\nso aggravating and underwent so many facial contortions, that Mr.\r\nBounderby, unable to bear it, seized her by the arm and shook her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If you can\xe2\x80\x99t get it out, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98leave _me_ to get it\r\nout.  This is not a time for a lady, however highly connected, to be\r\ntotally inaudible, and seemingly swallowing marbles.  Tom Gradgrind, Mrs.\r\nSparsit latterly found herself, by accident, in a situation to overhear a\r\nconversation out of doors between your daughter and your precious\r\ngentleman-friend, Mr. James Harthouse.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Indeed!\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Gradgrind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Ah!  Indeed!\xe2\x80\x99 cried Bounderby.  \xe2\x80\x98And in that conversation\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It is not necessary to repeat its tenor, Bounderby.  I know what\r\npassed.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You do?  Perhaps,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, staring with all his might at his so\r\nquiet and assuasive father-in-law, \xe2\x80\x98you know where your daughter is at\r\nthe present time!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Undoubtedly.  She is here.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Here?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My dear Bounderby, let me beg you to restrain these loud out-breaks, on\r\nall accounts.  Louisa is here.  The moment she could detach herself from\r\nthat interview with the person of whom you speak, and whom I deeply\r\nregret to have been the means of introducing to you, Louisa hurried here,\r\nfor protection.  I myself had not been at home many hours, when I\r\nreceived her\xe2\x80\x94here, in this room.  She hurried by the train to town, she\r\nran from town to this house, through a raging storm, and presented\r\nherself before me in a state of distraction.  Of course, she has remained\r\nhere ever since.  Let me entreat you, for your own sake and for hers, to\r\nbe more quiet.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. Bounderby silently gazed about him for some moments, in every\r\ndirection except Mrs. Sparsit\xe2\x80\x99s direction; and then, abruptly turning\r\nupon the niece of Lady Scadgers, said to that wretched woman:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now, ma\xe2\x80\x99am!  We shall be happy to hear any little apology you may think\r\nproper to offer, for going about the country at express pace, with no\r\nother luggage than a Cock-and-a-Bull, ma\xe2\x80\x99am!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Sir,\xe2\x80\x99 whispered Mrs. Sparsit, \xe2\x80\x98my nerves are at present too much shaken,\r\nand my health is at present too much impaired, in your service, to admit\r\nof my doing more than taking refuge in tears.\xe2\x80\x99  (Which she did.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98without making any observation to you\r\nthat may not be made with propriety to a woman of good family, what I\r\nhave got to add to that, is that there is something else in which it\r\nappears to me you may take refuge, namely, a coach.  And the coach in\r\nwhich we came here being at the door, you\xe2\x80\x99ll allow me to hand you down to\r\nit, and pack you home to the Bank: where the best course for you to\r\npursue, will be to put your feet into the hottest water you can bear, and\r\ntake a glass of scalding rum and butter after you get into bed.\xe2\x80\x99  With\r\nthese words, Mr. Bounderby extended his right hand to the weeping lady,\r\nand escorted her to the conveyance in question, shedding many plaintive\r\nsneezes by the way.  He soon returned alone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now, as you showed me in your face, Tom Gradgrind, that you wanted to\r\nspeak to me,\xe2\x80\x99 he resumed, \xe2\x80\x98here I am.  But, I am not in a very agreeable\r\nstate, I tell you plainly: not relishing this business, even as it is,\r\nand not considering that I am at any time as dutifully and submissively\r\ntreated by your daughter, as Josiah Bounderby of Coketown ought to be\r\ntreated by his wife.  You have your opinion, I dare say; and I have mine,\r\nI know.  If you mean to say anything to me to-night, that goes against\r\nthis candid remark, you had better let it alone.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. Gradgrind, it will be observed, being much softened, Mr. Bounderby\r\ntook particular pains to harden himself at all points.  It was his\r\namiable nature.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98My dear Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 Mr. Gradgrind began in reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now, you\xe2\x80\x99ll excuse me,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98but I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be too\r\ndear.  That, to start with.  When I begin to be dear to a man, I\r\ngenerally find that his intention is to come over me.  I am not speaking\r\nto you politely; but, as you are aware, I am _not_ polite.  If you like\r\npoliteness, you know where to get it.  You have your gentleman-friends,\r\nyou know, and they\xe2\x80\x99ll serve you with as much of the article as you want.\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t keep it myself.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 urged Mr. Gradgrind, \xe2\x80\x98we are all liable to mistakes\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I thought you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t make \xe2\x80\x99em,\xe2\x80\x99 interrupted Bounderby.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Perhaps I thought so.  But, I say we are all liable to mistakes and I\r\nshould feel sensible of your delicacy, and grateful for it, if you would\r\nspare me these references to Harthouse.  I shall not associate him in our\r\nconversation with your intimacy and encouragement; pray do not persist in\r\nconnecting him with mine.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I never mentioned his name!\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well, well!\xe2\x80\x99 returned Mr. Gradgrind, with a patient, even a submissive,\r\nair.  And he sat for a little while pondering.  \xe2\x80\x98Bounderby, I see reason\r\nto doubt whether we have ever quite understood Louisa.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Who do you mean by We?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Let me say I, then,\xe2\x80\x99 he returned, in answer to the coarsely blurted\r\nquestion; \xe2\x80\x98I doubt whether I have understood Louisa.  I doubt whether I\r\nhave been quite right in the manner of her education.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98There you hit it,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Bounderby.  \xe2\x80\x98There I agree with you.  You\r\nhave found it out at last, have you?  Education!  I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you what\r\neducation is\xe2\x80\x94To be tumbled out of doors, neck and crop, and put upon the\r\nshortest allowance of everything except blows.  That\xe2\x80\x99s what _I_ call\r\neducation.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I think your good sense will perceive,\xe2\x80\x99 Mr. Gradgrind remonstrated in\r\nall humility, \xe2\x80\x98that whatever the merits of such a system may be, it would\r\nbe difficult of general application to girls.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t see it at all, sir,\xe2\x80\x99 returned the obstinate Bounderby.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Well,\xe2\x80\x99 sighed Mr. Gradgrind, \xe2\x80\x98we will not enter into the question.  I\r\nassure you I have no desire to be controversial.  I seek to repair what\r\nis amiss, if I possibly can; and I hope you will assist me in a good\r\nspirit, Bounderby, for I have been very much distressed.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand you, yet,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, with determined obstinacy,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98and therefore I won\xe2\x80\x99t make any promises.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98In the course of a few hours, my dear Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 Mr. Gradgrind\r\nproceeded, in the same depressed and propitiatory manner, \xe2\x80\x98I appear to\r\nmyself to have become better informed as to Louisa\xe2\x80\x99s character, than in\r\nprevious years.  The enlightenment has been painfully forced upon me, and\r\nthe discovery is not mine.  I think there are\xe2\x80\x94Bounderby, you will be\r\nsurprised to hear me say this\xe2\x80\x94I think there are qualities in Louisa,\r\nwhich\xe2\x80\x94which have been harshly neglected, and\xe2\x80\x94and a little perverted.\r\nAnd\xe2\x80\x94and I would suggest to you, that\xe2\x80\x94that if you would kindly meet me in\r\na timely endeavour to leave her to her better nature for a while\xe2\x80\x94and to\r\nencourage it to develop itself by tenderness and consideration\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x94it\r\nwould be the better for the happiness of all of us.  Louisa,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr.\r\nGradgrind, shading his face with his hand, \xe2\x80\x98has always been my favourite\r\nchild.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe blustrous Bounderby crimsoned and swelled to such an extent on\r\nhearing these words, that he seemed to be, and probably was, on the brink\r\nof a fit.  With his very ears a bright purple shot with crimson, he pent\r\nup his indignation, however, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You\xe2\x80\x99d like to keep her here for a time?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x94I had intended to recommend, my dear Bounderby, that you should allow\r\nLouisa to remain here on a visit, and be attended by Sissy (I mean of\r\ncourse Cecilia Jupe), who understands her, and in whom she trusts.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I gather from all this, Tom Gradgrind,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, standing up with\r\nhis hands in his pockets, \xe2\x80\x98that you are of opinion that there\xe2\x80\x99s what\r\npeople call some incompatibility between Loo Bounderby and myself.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I fear there is at present a general incompatibility between Louisa,\r\nand\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94and almost all the relations in which I have placed her,\xe2\x80\x99 was her\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s sorrowful reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Now, look you here, Tom Gradgrind,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby the flushed,\r\nconfronting him with his legs wide apart, his hands deeper in his\r\npockets, and his hair like a hayfield wherein his windy anger was\r\nboisterous.  \xe2\x80\x98You have said your say; I am going to say mine.  I am a\r\nCoketown man.  I am Josiah Bounderby of Coketown.  I know the bricks of\r\nthis town, and I know the works of this town, and I know the chimneys of\r\nthis town, and I know the smoke of this town, and I know the Hands of\r\nthis town.  I know \xe2\x80\x99em all pretty well.  They\xe2\x80\x99re real.  When a man tells\r\nme anything about imaginative qualities, I always tell that man, whoever\r\nhe is, that I know what he means.  He means turtle soup and venison, with\r\na gold spoon, and that he wants to be set up with a coach and six.\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s what your daughter wants.  Since you are of opinion that she ought\r\nto have what she wants, I recommend you to provide it for her.  Because,\r\nTom Gradgrind, she will never have it from me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Gradgrind, \xe2\x80\x98I hoped, after my entreaty, you would\r\nhave taken a different tone.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Just wait a bit,\xe2\x80\x99 retorted Bounderby; \xe2\x80\x98you have said your say, I\r\nbelieve.  I heard you out; hear me out, if you please.  Don\xe2\x80\x99t make\r\nyourself a spectacle of unfairness as well as inconsistency, because,\r\nalthough I am sorry to see Tom Gradgrind reduced to his present position,\r\nI should be doubly sorry to see him brought so low as that.  Now, there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nan incompatibility of some sort or another, I am given to understand by\r\nyou, between your daughter and me.  I\xe2\x80\x99ll give _you_ to understand, in\r\nreply to that, that there unquestionably is an incompatibility of the\r\nfirst magnitude\xe2\x80\x94to be summed up in this\xe2\x80\x94that your daughter don\xe2\x80\x99t properly\r\nknow her husband\xe2\x80\x99s merits, and is not impressed with such a sense as\r\nwould become her, by George! of the honour of his alliance.  That\xe2\x80\x99s plain\r\nspeaking, I hope.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 urged Mr. Gradgrind, \xe2\x80\x98this is unreasonable.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Is it?\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby.  \xe2\x80\x98I am glad to hear you say so.  Because when\r\nTom Gradgrind, with his new lights, tells me that what I say is\r\nunreasonable, I am convinced at once it must be devilish sensible.  With\r\nyour permission I am going on.  You know my origin; and you know that for\r\na good many years of my life I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want a shoeing-horn, in consequence\r\nof not having a shoe.  Yet you may believe or not, as you think proper,\r\nthat there are ladies\xe2\x80\x94born ladies\xe2\x80\x94belonging to families\xe2\x80\x94Families!\xe2\x80\x94who\r\nnext to worship the ground I walk on.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHe discharged this like a Rocket, at his father-in-law\xe2\x80\x99s head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Whereas your daughter,\xe2\x80\x99 proceeded Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98is far from being a born\r\nlady.  That you know, yourself.  Not that I care a pinch of candle-snuff\r\nabout such things, for you are very well aware I don\xe2\x80\x99t; but that such is\r\nthe fact, and you, Tom Gradgrind, can\xe2\x80\x99t change it.  Why do I say this?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Not, I fear,\xe2\x80\x99 observed Mr. Gradgrind, in a low voice, \xe2\x80\x98to spare me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Hear me out,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98and refrain from cutting in till your\r\nturn comes round.  I say this, because highly connected females have been\r\nastonished to see the way in which your daughter has conducted herself,\r\nand to witness her insensibility.  They have wondered how I have suffered\r\nit.  And I wonder myself now, and I won\xe2\x80\x99t suffer it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Mr. Gradgrind, rising, \xe2\x80\x98the less we say to-night\r\nthe better, I think.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98On the contrary, Tom Gradgrind, the more we say to-night, the better, I\r\nthink.  That is,\xe2\x80\x99 the consideration checked him, \xe2\x80\x98till I have said all I\r\nmean to say, and then I don\xe2\x80\x99t care how soon we stop.  I come to a\r\nquestion that may shorten the business.  What do you mean by the proposal\r\nyou made just now?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What do I mean, Bounderby?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98By your visiting proposition,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, with an inflexible jerk\r\nof the hayfield.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I mean that I hope you may be induced to arrange in a friendly manner,\r\nfor allowing Louisa a period of repose and reflection here, which may\r\ntend to a gradual alteration for the better in many respects.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98To a softening down of your ideas of the incompatibility?\xe2\x80\x99 said\r\nBounderby.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If you put it in those terms.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What made you think of this?\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I have already said, I fear Louisa has not been understood.  Is it\r\nasking too much, Bounderby, that you, so far her elder, should aid in\r\ntrying to set her right?  You have accepted a great charge of her; for\r\nbetter for worse, for\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nMr. Bounderby may have been annoyed by the repetition of his own words to\r\nStephen Blackpool, but he cut the quotation short with an angry start.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Come!\xe2\x80\x99 said he, \xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be told about that.  I know what I took\r\nher for, as well as you do.  Never you mind what I took her for; that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmy look out.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I was merely going on to remark, Bounderby, that we may all be more or\r\nless in the wrong, not even excepting you; and that some yielding on your\r\npart, remembering the trust you have accepted, may not only be an act of\r\ntrue kindness, but perhaps a debt incurred towards Louisa.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I think differently,\xe2\x80\x99 blustered Bounderby.  \xe2\x80\x98I am going to finish this\r\nbusiness according to my own opinions.  Now, I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to make a\r\nquarrel of it with you, Tom Gradgrind.  To tell you the truth, I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthink it would be worthy of my reputation to quarrel on such a subject.\r\nAs to your gentleman-friend, he may take himself off, wherever he likes\r\nbest.  If he falls in my way, I shall tell him my mind; if he don\xe2\x80\x99t fall\r\nin my way, I shan\xe2\x80\x99t, for it won\xe2\x80\x99t be worth my while to do it.  As to your\r\ndaughter, whom I made Loo Bounderby, and might have done better by\r\nleaving Loo Gradgrind, if she don\xe2\x80\x99t come home to-morrow, by twelve\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock at noon, I shall understand that she prefers to stay away, and I\r\nshall send her wearing apparel and so forth over here, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll take\r\ncharge of her for the future.  What I shall say to people in general, of\r\nthe incompatibility that led to my so laying down the law, will be this.\r\nI am Josiah Bounderby, and I had my bringing-up; she\xe2\x80\x99s the daughter of\r\nTom Gradgrind, and she had her bringing-up; and the two horses wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\npull together.  I am pretty well known to be rather an uncommon man, I\r\nbelieve; and most people will understand fast enough that it must be a\r\nwoman rather out of the common, also, who, in the long run, would come up\r\nto my mark.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Let me seriously entreat you to reconsider this, Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 urged Mr.\r\nGradgrind, \xe2\x80\x98before you commit yourself to such a decision.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I always come to a decision,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby, tossing his hat on: \xe2\x80\x98and\r\nwhatever I do, I do at once.  I should be surprised at Tom Gradgrind\xe2\x80\x99s\r\naddressing such a remark to Josiah Bounderby of Coketown, knowing what he\r\nknows of him, if I could be surprised by anything Tom Gradgrind did,\r\nafter his making himself a party to sentimental humbug.  I have given you\r\nmy decision, and I have got no more to say.  Good night!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSo Mr. Bounderby went home to his town house to bed.  At five minutes\r\npast twelve o\xe2\x80\x99clock next day, he directed Mrs. Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s property to be\r\ncarefully packed up and sent to Tom Gradgrind\xe2\x80\x99s; advertised his country\r\nretreat for sale by private contract; and resumed a bachelor life.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV\r\nLOST\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE robbery at the Bank had not languished before, and did not cease to\r\noccupy a front place in the attention of the principal of that\r\nestablishment now.  In boastful proof of his promptitude and activity, as\r\na remarkable man, and a self-made man, and a commercial wonder more\r\nadmirable than Venus, who had risen out of the mud instead of the sea, he\r\nliked to show how little his domestic affairs abated his business ardour.\r\nConsequently, in the first few weeks of his resumed bachelorhood, he even\r\nadvanced upon his usual display of bustle, and every day made such a rout\r\nin renewing his investigations into the robbery, that the officers who\r\nhad it in hand almost wished it had never been committed.\r\n\r\nThey were at fault too, and off the scent.  Although they had been so\r\nquiet since the first outbreak of the matter, that most people really did\r\nsuppose it to have been abandoned as hopeless, nothing new occurred.  No\r\nimplicated man or woman took untimely courage, or made a self-betraying\r\nstep.  More remarkable yet, Stephen Blackpool could not be heard of, and\r\nthe mysterious old woman remained a mystery.\r\n\r\nThings having come to this pass, and showing no latent signs of stirring\r\nbeyond it, the upshot of Mr. Bounderby\xe2\x80\x99s investigations was, that he\r\nresolved to hazard a bold burst.  He drew up a placard, offering Twenty\r\nPounds reward for the apprehension of Stephen Blackpool, suspected of\r\ncomplicity in the robbery of Coketown Bank on such a night; he described\r\nthe said Stephen Blackpool by dress, complexion, estimated height, and\r\nmanner, as minutely as he could; he recited how he had left the town, and\r\nin what direction he had been last seen going; he had the whole printed\r\nin great black letters on a staring broadsheet; and he caused the walls\r\nto be posted with it in the dead of night, so that it should strike upon\r\nthe sight of the whole population at one blow.\r\n\r\nThe factory-bells had need to ring their loudest that morning to disperse\r\nthe groups of workers who stood in the tardy daybreak, collected round\r\nthe placards, devouring them with eager eyes.  Not the least eager of the\r\neyes assembled, were the eyes of those who could not read.  These people,\r\nas they listened to the friendly voice that read aloud\xe2\x80\x94there was always\r\nsome such ready to help them\xe2\x80\x94stared at the characters which meant so much\r\nwith a vague awe and respect that would have been half ludicrous, if any\r\naspect of public ignorance could ever be otherwise than threatening and\r\nfull of evil.  Many ears and eyes were busy with a vision of the matter\r\nof these placards, among turning spindles, rattling looms, and whirling\r\nwheels, for hours afterwards; and when the Hands cleared out again into\r\nthe streets, there were still as many readers as before.\r\n\r\nSlackbridge, the delegate, had to address his audience too that night;\r\nand Slackbridge had obtained a clean bill from the printer, and had\r\nbrought it in his pocket.  Oh, my friends and fellow-countrymen, the\r\ndown-trodden operatives of Coketown, oh, my fellow-brothers and\r\nfellow-workmen and fellow-citizens and fellow-men, what a to-do was\r\nthere, when Slackbridge unfolded what he called \xe2\x80\x98that damning document,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nand held it up to the gaze, and for the execration of the working-man\r\ncommunity!  \xe2\x80\x98Oh, my fellow-men, behold of what a traitor in the camp of\r\nthose great spirits who are enrolled upon the holy scroll of Justice and\r\nof Union, is appropriately capable!  Oh, my prostrate friends, with the\r\ngalling yoke of tyrants on your necks and the iron foot of despotism\r\ntreading down your fallen forms into the dust of the earth, upon which\r\nright glad would your oppressors be to see you creeping on your bellies\r\nall the days of your lives, like the serpent in the garden\xe2\x80\x94oh, my\r\nbrothers, and shall I as a man not add, my sisters too, what do you say,\r\n_now_, of Stephen Blackpool, with a slight stoop in his shoulders and\r\nabout five foot seven in height, as set forth in this degrading and\r\ndisgusting document, this blighting bill, this pernicious placard, this\r\nabominable advertisement; and with what majesty of denouncement will you\r\ncrush the viper, who would bring this stain and shame upon the God-like\r\nrace that happily has cast him out for ever!  Yes, my compatriots,\r\nhappily cast him out and sent him forth!  For you remember how he stood\r\nhere before you on this platform; you remember how, face to face and foot\r\nto foot, I pursued him through all his intricate windings; you remember\r\nhow he sneaked and slunk, and sidled, and splitted of straws, until, with\r\nnot an inch of ground to which to cling, I hurled him out from amongst\r\nus: an object for the undying finger of scorn to point at, and for the\r\navenging fire of every free and thinking mind to scorch and scar!  And\r\nnow, my friends\xe2\x80\x94my labouring friends, for I rejoice and triumph in that\r\nstigma\xe2\x80\x94my friends whose hard but honest beds are made in toil, and whose\r\nscanty but independent pots are boiled in hardship; and now, I say, my\r\nfriends, what appellation has that dastard craven taken to himself, when,\r\nwith the mask torn from his features, he stands before us in all his\r\nnative deformity, a What?  A thief!  A plunderer!  A proscribed fugitive,\r\nwith a price upon his head; a fester and a wound upon the noble character\r\nof the Coketown operative!  Therefore, my band of brothers in a sacred\r\nbond, to which your children and your children\xe2\x80\x99s children yet unborn have\r\nset their infant hands and seals, I propose to you on the part of the\r\nUnited Aggregate Tribunal, ever watchful for your welfare, ever zealous\r\nfor your benefit, that this meeting does Resolve: That Stephen Blackpool,\r\nweaver, referred to in this placard, having been already solemnly\r\ndisowned by the community of Coketown Hands, the same are free from the\r\nshame of his misdeeds, and cannot as a class be reproached with his\r\ndishonest actions!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThus Slackbridge; gnashing and perspiring after a prodigious sort.  A few\r\nstern voices called out \xe2\x80\x98No!\xe2\x80\x99 and a score or two hailed, with assenting\r\ncries of \xe2\x80\x98Hear, hear!\xe2\x80\x99 the caution from one man, \xe2\x80\x98Slackbridge, y\xe2\x80\x99or over\r\nhetter in\xe2\x80\x99t; y\xe2\x80\x99or a goen too fast!\xe2\x80\x99  But these were pigmies against an\r\narmy; the general assemblage subscribed to the gospel according to\r\nSlackbridge, and gave three cheers for him, as he sat demonstratively\r\npanting at them.\r\n\r\nThese men and women were yet in the streets, passing quietly to their\r\nhomes, when Sissy, who had been called away from Louisa some minutes\r\nbefore, returned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Who is it?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Louisa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It is Mr. Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 said Sissy, timid of the name, \xe2\x80\x98and your brother\r\nMr. Tom, and a young woman who says her name is Rachael, and that you\r\nknow her.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What do they want, Sissy dear?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98They want to see you.  Rachael has been crying, and seems angry.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Father,\xe2\x80\x99 said Louisa, for he was present, \xe2\x80\x98I cannot refuse to see them,\r\nfor a reason that will explain itself.  Shall they come in here?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAs he answered in the affirmative, Sissy went away to bring them.  She\r\nreappeared with them directly.  Tom was last; and remained standing in\r\nthe obscurest part of the room, near the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mrs. Bounderby,\xe2\x80\x99 said her husband, entering with a cool nod, \xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndisturb you, I hope.  This is an unseasonable hour, but here is a young\r\nwoman who has been making statements which render my visit necessary.\r\nTom Gradgrind, as your son, young Tom, refuses for some obstinate reason\r\nor other to say anything at all about those statements, good or bad, I am\r\nobliged to confront her with your daughter.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You have seen me once before, young lady,\xe2\x80\x99 said Rachael, standing in\r\nfront of Louisa.\r\n\r\nTom coughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You have seen me, young lady,\xe2\x80\x99 repeated Rachael, as she did not answer,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98once before.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nTom coughed again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I have.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nRachael cast her eyes proudly towards Mr. Bounderby, and said, \xe2\x80\x98Will you\r\nmake it known, young lady, where, and who was there?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I went to the house where Stephen Blackpool lodged, on the night of his\r\ndischarge from his work, and I saw you there.  He was there too; and an\r\nold woman who did not speak, and whom I could scarcely see, stood in a\r\ndark corner.  My brother was with me.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Why couldn\xe2\x80\x99t you say so, young Tom?\xe2\x80\x99 demanded Bounderby.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I promised my sister I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x99  Which Louisa hastily confirmed.  \xe2\x80\x98And\r\nbesides,\xe2\x80\x99 said the whelp bitterly, \xe2\x80\x98she tells her own story so precious\r\nwell\xe2\x80\x94and so full\xe2\x80\x94that what business had I to take it out of her mouth!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Say, young lady, if you please,\xe2\x80\x99 pursued Rachael, \xe2\x80\x98why, in an evil hour,\r\nyou ever came to Stephen\xe2\x80\x99s that night.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I felt compassion for him,\xe2\x80\x99 said Louisa, her colour deepening, \xe2\x80\x98and I\r\nwished to know what he was going to do, and wished to offer him\r\nassistance.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Thank you, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby.  \xe2\x80\x98Much flattered and obliged.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Did you offer him,\xe2\x80\x99 asked Rachael, \xe2\x80\x98a bank-note?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes; but he refused it, and would only take two pounds in gold.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nRachael cast her eyes towards Mr. Bounderby again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh, certainly!\xe2\x80\x99 said Bounderby.  \xe2\x80\x98If you put the question whether your\r\nridiculous and improbable account was true or not, I am bound to say it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nconfirmed.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Young lady,\xe2\x80\x99 said Rachael, \xe2\x80\x98Stephen Blackpool is now named as a thief in\r\npublic print all over this town, and where else!  There have been a\r\nmeeting to-night where he have been spoken of in the same shameful way.\r\nStephen!  The honestest lad, the truest lad, the best!\xe2\x80\x99  Her indignation\r\nfailed her, and she broke off sobbing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I am very, very sorry,\xe2\x80\x99 said Louisa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh, young lady, young lady,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Rachael, \xe2\x80\x98I hope you may be, but I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know!  I can\xe2\x80\x99t say what you may ha\xe2\x80\x99 done!  The like of you don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow us, don\xe2\x80\x99t care for us, don\xe2\x80\x99t belong to us.  I am not sure why you\r\nmay ha\xe2\x80\x99 come that night.  I can\xe2\x80\x99t tell but what you may ha\xe2\x80\x99 come wi\xe2\x80\x99 some\r\naim of your own, not mindin to what trouble you brought such as the poor\r\nlad.  I said then, Bless you for coming; and I said it of my heart, you\r\nseemed to take so pitifully to him; but I don\xe2\x80\x99t know now, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nLouisa could not reproach her for her unjust suspicions; she was so\r\nfaithful to her idea of the man, and so afflicted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And when I think,\xe2\x80\x99 said Rachael through her sobs, \xe2\x80\x98that the poor lad was\r\nso grateful, thinkin you so good to him\xe2\x80\x94when I mind that he put his hand\r\nover his hard-worken face to hide the tears that you brought up there\xe2\x80\x94Oh,\r\nI hope you may be sorry, and ha\xe2\x80\x99 no bad cause to be it; but I don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t know!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You\xe2\x80\x99re a pretty article,\xe2\x80\x99 growled the whelp, moving uneasily in his dark\r\ncorner, \xe2\x80\x98to come here with these precious imputations!  You ought to be\r\nbundled out for not knowing how to behave yourself, and you would be by\r\nrights.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nShe said nothing in reply; and her low weeping was the only sound that\r\nwas heard, until Mr. Bounderby spoke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Come!\xe2\x80\x99 said he, \xe2\x80\x98you know what you have engaged to do.  You had better\r\ngive your mind to that; not this.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98\xe2\x80\x99Deed, I am loath,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Rachael, drying her eyes, \xe2\x80\x98that any here\r\nshould see me like this; but I won\xe2\x80\x99t be seen so again.  Young lady, when\r\nI had read what\xe2\x80\x99s put in print of Stephen\xe2\x80\x94and what has just as much truth\r\nin it as if it had been put in print of you\xe2\x80\x94I went straight to the Bank\r\nto say I knew where Stephen was, and to give a sure and certain promise\r\nthat he should be here in two days.  I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t meet wi\xe2\x80\x99 Mr. Bounderby\r\nthen, and your brother sent me away, and I tried to find you, but you was\r\nnot to be found, and I went back to work.  Soon as I come out of the Mill\r\nto-night, I hastened to hear what was said of Stephen\xe2\x80\x94for I know wi\xe2\x80\x99\r\npride he will come back to shame it!\xe2\x80\x94and then I went again to seek Mr.\r\nBounderby, and I found him, and I told him every word I knew; and he\r\nbelieved no word I said, and brought me here.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98So far, that\xe2\x80\x99s true enough,\xe2\x80\x99 assented Mr. Bounderby, with his hands in\r\nhis pockets and his hat on.  \xe2\x80\x98But I have known you people before to-day,\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll observe, and I know you never die for want of talking.  Now, I\r\nrecommend you not so much to mind talking just now, as doing.  You have\r\nundertaken to do something; all I remark upon that at present is, do it!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I have written to Stephen by the post that went out this afternoon, as I\r\nhave written to him once before sin\xe2\x80\x99 he went away,\xe2\x80\x99 said Rachael; \xe2\x80\x98and he\r\nwill be here, at furthest, in two days.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then, I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you something.  You are not aware perhaps,\xe2\x80\x99 retorted Mr.\r\nBounderby, \xe2\x80\x98that you yourself have been looked after now and then, not\r\nbeing considered quite free from suspicion in this business, on account\r\nof most people being judged according to the company they keep.  The\r\npost-office hasn\xe2\x80\x99t been forgotten either.  What I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you is, that no\r\nletter to Stephen Blackpool has ever got into it.  Therefore, what has\r\nbecome of yours, I leave you to guess.  Perhaps you\xe2\x80\x99re mistaken, and\r\nnever wrote any.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been gone from here, young lady,\xe2\x80\x99 said Rachael, turning\r\nappealingly to Louisa, \xe2\x80\x98as much as a week, when he sent me the only\r\nletter I have had from him, saying that he was forced to seek work in\r\nanother name.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Oh, by George!\xe2\x80\x99 cried Bounderby, shaking his head, with a whistle, \xe2\x80\x98he\r\nchanges his name, does he!  That\xe2\x80\x99s rather unlucky, too, for such an\r\nimmaculate chap.  It\xe2\x80\x99s considered a little suspicious in Courts of\r\nJustice, I believe, when an Innocent happens to have many names.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What,\xe2\x80\x99 said Rachael, with the tears in her eyes again, \xe2\x80\x98what, young\r\nlady, in the name of Mercy, was left the poor lad to do!  The masters\r\nagainst him on one hand, the men against him on the other, he only wantin\r\nto work hard in peace, and do what he felt right.  Can a man have no soul\r\nof his own, no mind of his own?  Must he go wrong all through wi\xe2\x80\x99 this\r\nside, or must he go wrong all through wi\xe2\x80\x99 that, or else be hunted like a\r\nhare?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Indeed, indeed, I pity him from my heart,\xe2\x80\x99 returned Louisa; \xe2\x80\x98and I hope\r\nthat he will clear himself.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98You need have no fear of that, young lady.  He is sure!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98All the surer, I suppose,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98for your refusing to\r\ntell where he is?  Eh?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98He shall not, through any act of mine, come back wi\xe2\x80\x99 the unmerited\r\nreproach of being brought back.  He shall come back of his own accord to\r\nclear himself, and put all those that have injured his good character,\r\nand he not here for its defence, to shame.  I have told him what has been\r\ndone against him,\xe2\x80\x99 said Rachael, throwing off all distrust as a rock\r\nthrows off the sea, \xe2\x80\x98and he will be here, at furthest, in two days.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Notwithstanding which,\xe2\x80\x99 added Mr. Bounderby, \xe2\x80\x98if he can be laid hold of\r\nany sooner, he shall have an earlier opportunity of clearing himself.  As\r\nto you, I have nothing against you; what you came and told me turns out\r\nto be true, and I have given you the means of proving it to be true, and\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s an end of it.  I wish you good night all!  I must be off to look\r\na little further into this.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nTom came out of his corner when Mr. Bounderby moved, moved with him, kept\r\nclose to him, and went away with him.  The only parting salutation of\r\nwhich he delivered himself was a sulky \xe2\x80\x98Good night, father!\xe2\x80\x99  With a\r\nbrief speech, and a scowl at his sister, he left the house.\r\n\r\nSince his sheet-anchor had come home, Mr. Gradgrind had been sparing of\r\nspeech.  He still sat silent, when Louisa mildly said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Rachael, you will not distrust me one day, when you know me better.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It goes against me,\xe2\x80\x99 Rachael answered, in a gentler manner, \xe2\x80\x98to mistrust\r\nany one; but when I am so mistrusted\xe2\x80\x94when we all are\xe2\x80\x94I cannot keep such\r\nthings quite out of my mind.  I ask your pardon for having done you an\r\ninjury.  I don\xe2\x80\x99t think what I said now.  Yet I might come to think it\r\nagain, wi\xe2\x80\x99 the poor lad so wronged.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Did you tell him in your letter,\xe2\x80\x99 inquired Sissy, \xe2\x80\x98that suspicion seemed\r\nto have fallen upon him, because he had been seen about the Bank at\r\nnight?  He would then know what he would have to explain on coming back,\r\nand would be ready.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, dear,\xe2\x80\x99 she returned; \xe2\x80\x98but I can\xe2\x80\x99t guess what can have ever taken\r\nhim there.  He never used to go there.  It was never in his way.  His way\r\nwas the same as mine, and not near it.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nSissy had already been at her side asking her where she lived, and\r\nwhether she might come to-morrow night, to inquire if there were news of\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I doubt,\xe2\x80\x99 said Rachael, \xe2\x80\x98if he can be here till next day.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Then I will come next night too,\xe2\x80\x99 said Sissy.\r\n\r\nWhen Rachael, assenting to this, was gone, Mr. Gradgrind lifted up his\r\nhead, and said to his daughter:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Louisa, my dear, I have never, that I know of, seen this man.  Do you\r\nbelieve him to be implicated?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I think I have believed it, father, though with great difficulty.  I do\r\nnot believe it now.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98That is to say, you once persuaded yourself to believe it, from knowing\r\nhim to be suspected.  His appearance and manner; are they so honest?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Very honest.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98And her confidence not to be shaken!  I ask myself,\xe2\x80\x99 said Mr. Gradgrind,\r\nmusing, \xe2\x80\x98does the real culprit know of these accusations?  Where is he?\r\nWho is he?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nHis hair had latterly began to change its colour.  As he leaned upon his\r\nhand again, looking gray and old, Louisa, with a face of fear and pity,\r\nhurriedly went over to him, and sat close at his side.  Her eyes by\r\naccident met Sissy\xe2\x80\x99s at the moment.  Sissy flushed and started, and\r\nLouisa put her finger on her lip.\r\n\r\nNext night, when Sissy returned home and told Louisa that Stephen was not\r\ncome, she told it in a whisper.  Next night again, when she came home\r\nwith the same account, and added that he had not been heard of, she spoke\r\nin the same low frightened tone.  From the moment of that interchange of\r\nlooks, they never uttered his name, or any reference to him, aloud; nor\r\never pursued the subject of the robbery, when Mr. Gradgrind spoke of it.\r\n\r\nThe two appointed days ran out, three days and nights ran out, and\r\nStephen Blackpool was not come, and remained unheard of.  On the fourth\r\nday, Rachael, with unabated confidence, but considering her despatch to\r\nhave miscarried, went up to the Bank, and showed her letter from him with\r\nhis address, at a working colony, one of many, not upon the main road,\r\nsixty miles away.  Messengers were sent to that place, and the whole town\r\nlooked for Stephen to be brought in next day.\r\n\r\nDuring this whole time the whelp moved about with Mr. Bounderby like his\r\nshadow, assisting in all the proceedings.  He was greatly excited,\r\nhorribly fevered, bit his nails down to the quick, spoke in a hard\r\nrattling voice, and with lips that were black and burnt up.  At the hour\r\nwhen the suspected man was looked for, the whelp was at the station;\r\noffering to wager that he had made off before the arrival of those who\r\nwere sent in quest of him, and that he would not appear.\r\n\r\nThe whelp was right.  The messengers returned alone.  Rachael\xe2\x80\x99s letter\r\nhad gone, Rachael\xe2\x80\x99s letter had been delivered.  Stephen Blackpool had\r\ndecamped in that same hour; and no soul knew more of him.  The only doubt\r\nin Coketown was, whether Rachael had written in good faith, believing\r\nthat he really would come back, or warning him to fly.  On this point\r\nopinion was divided.\r\n\r\nSix days, seven days, far on into another week.  The wretched whelp\r\nplucked up a ghastly courage, and began to grow defiant.  \xe2\x80\x98_Was_ the\r\nsuspected fellow the thief?  A pretty question!  If not, where was the\r\nman, and why did he not come back?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nWhere was the man, and why did he not come back?  In the dead of night\r\nthe echoes of his own words, which had rolled Heaven knows how far away\r\nin the daytime, came back instead, and abided by him until morning.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V\r\nFOUND\r\n\r\n\r\nDAY and night again, day and night again.  No Stephen Blackpool.  Where\r\nwas the man, and why did he not come back?\r\n\r\nEvery night, Sissy went to Rachael\xe2\x80\x99s lodging, and sat with her in her\r\nsmall neat room.
